Al-Razi's Three Knowledges: The Relational Nature of Wajh
Al-Razi's three knowledges and the relational nature of knowledge of wajh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's classification of three knowledges (proof, indicated, and proof's indication) and why the third is distinct and relational.

Core Idea
Knowledge of the proof's indication is a relational knowledge distinct from knowledge of proof and indicated.

Al-Taftazani explains a key argument from al-Razi that distinguishes three types of knowledge: of the proof itself, of the indicated, and of the proof being a proof. The third is relational and hence distinct. This lesson unpacks that tripartite division and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Locate al-Razi's three knowledges
Find the sentence where the three things are listed: knowledge of the essence of the proof, knowledge of the essence of the indicated, and knowledge that the proof is a proof of the indicated.
Examples are given: possibility of the world, necessity of an effect, and the relation between them.
2
Understand why the third is distinct
The third knowledge is relational (knowing the proof as proof). This relation is an addition (iḍāfa) between proof and indicated, making it distinct from both.
The first two are direct, the third is meta-knowledge.
3
Address potential illusion
Al-Razi's wording might suggest that wajh is the proof itself. Note al-Taftazani's comment that this could be misleading.
The example of possibility might imply the proof's essence, but wajh is the relation.
4
Compare with al-Mawāqif's framing
Al-Taftazani notes that al-Mawāqif mentions a different framing of the disagreement, but it is not found in famous books. This clarifies the standard position.
This step is about contextualizing the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال حجة الإسلام: لما كان جهة الدلالة في القياس هو التفطن لوجود النتيجة بالقوة في المقدمة أشكل على الضعفاء فلم يعرفوا أن وجه الدلالة عين المدلول أو غيره، والحق أن المطلوب هو المدلول المنتج، وأنه غير التفطن لوجوده في المقدمة بالقوة. وبالجملة فالمشهور من الاختلاف في هذا البحث هو الاختلاف في: مغايرة جهة الدلالة للمدلول فيتفرع عليه الاختلاف في تغاير العلم بهما على ما قال الإمام الرازي وغيره أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل هل يغاير العلم بالمدلول؟ فيه خلاف والحق المغايرة. لتغاير المدلول ووجه الدلالة. وأما ما ذكر في المواقف من أن الخلاف في أن العلم بدلالة الدليل هل يغاير العلم بالمدلول وفي أن وجه الدلالة هل يغاير الدليل؟ فلم يوجد في الكتب المشهورة. إلا أن الإمام ذكر في بيان مغايرة العلم بوجه الدلالة للعلم بالمدلول أن ههنا أموراً ثلاثة: هي العلم بذات الدليل كالعلم بإمكان العالم، والعلم بذات المدلول كالعلم بأنه لا بد له من مؤثر، والعلم بكون الدليل دليلاً على المدلول ولا خفاء في تغاير الأولين وكذا في مغايرة الثالث لهما، لكونه علماً بإضافة بين الدليل والمدلول مغايرة لهما، وهذا الكلام ربما يوهم خلافاً في مغايرة العلم بدلالة الدليل للعلم بالمدلول، حيث احتيج إلى البيان وجعل العلم أنه الدلالة مثالاً بإمكان العالم مع وجه للعلم بذات الدليل يوهم القول بأن وجه الدلالة نفس الدليل، وفي نقد المحصل ما يشعر بالخلاف في وجوب مغايرته للدليل والمدلول، لأنه قال: إن هذه المسألة إنما تجري بين المتكلمين عند استدلالهم بوجود سوى الله تعالى على وجوده تعالى فيقولون: لا يجوز أن يكون وجه دلالة ما دلالة وجود سوى الله تعالى على وجوده مغايراً لهما لأن المغاير لوجوده تعالى داخل في وجود ما سواه، والمغاير لوجود ما سواه هو وجوده فقط. والجواب أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل على المدلول الذي هو مغاير لهما: هو أمر اعتباري عقلي. ليس بموجود في الخارج كما سيجيء في تحقيق التضايف. هذا كلامه وأنت خبير بأن الأمر الاعتباري الإضافي هو دلالة الدليل على المدلول لا وجه الدلالة الذي هو صفة في الدليل كالإمكان والحدوث في العالم، ثم ظاهر عبارته أن المحكوم عليه يكون أمراً اعتبارياً هو العلم بوجه الدلالة وفساده بين.
al-Ghazali said: Since the aspect of indication (jihāt al-dalāla) in syllogism is the noticing of the existence of the conclusion potentially in the premise, it became obscure to the weak, so they did not know whether the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalāla) is identical to the indicated (madlūl) or other than it. The truth is that the sought is the produced indicated, and that it is other than the noticing of its existence potentially in the premise. In general, the well-known disagreement in this investigation is the disagreement concerning the distinction (mukhāyara) of the aspect of indication from the indicated, and there branches from it the disagreement concerning the distinction of the knowledge of them, as al-Razi and others said: Is the knowledge of the aspect of indication of the proof distinct from the knowledge of the indicated? There is disagreement; and the truth is that they are distinct, due to the distinction of the indicated and the aspect of indication. As for what is mentioned in al-Mawāqif, that the disagreement is about whether the knowledge of the indication of the proof is distinct from the knowledge of the indicated, and whether the aspect of indication is distinct from the proof – that is not found in the well-known books. However, the Imam mentioned in explaining the distinction of the knowledge of the aspect of indication from the knowledge of the indicated that there are here three things: the knowledge of the essence of the proof, like the knowledge of the possibility of the world; the knowledge of the essence of the indicated, like the knowledge that it necessarily has an effect; and the knowledge that the proof is a proof of the indicated. There is no concealment of the distinction of the first two, and likewise of the distinction of the third from them, because it is knowledge of a relation between the proof and the indicated that is distinct from them. This statement might give the illusion of disagreement regarding the distinction of the knowledge of the indication of the proof from the knowledge of the indicated, since an explanation was needed and he made the knowledge that it is the indication an example by the possibility of the world, along with a face for the knowledge of the essence of the proof, suggesting the saying that the aspect of indication is the proof itself. In Naqd al-Muhassal, there is something that indicates disagreement concerning the necessity of its being distinct from the proof and the indicated, because he said: This issue only occurs among the theologians when they use the existence of other than God to prove His existence, and they say: It is not permissible that the aspect of the indication of the existence of other than God upon His existence be distinct from them both, because what is distinct from His existence is included in the existence of what is other than Him, and what is distinct from the existence of what is other than Him is only His existence. The answer is that the knowledge of the aspect of indication of the proof upon the indicated, which is distinct from them both, is a rational mental consideration (amr i‘tibārī ‘aqlī), not existing externally, as will come in the investigation of correlation (taḍāyuf). This is his statement. And you are aware that the relational mental consideration (al-amr al-i‘tibārī al-iḍāfī) is the indication of the proof upon the indicated, not the aspect of indication which is a quality in the proof, like possibility and temporal origination in the world. Then the apparent meaning of his statement is that the thing judged is a mental consideration, namely the knowledge of the aspect of indication, and its corruption is evident.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is an investigation into whether the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalāla) in a syllogism is identical to or distinct from the indicated (madlūl). Al-Taftazani begins by quoting al-Ghazali: The aspect of indication is the noticing of the conclusion potentially in the premise. This is obscure to some, leading to confusion. The truth is that the sought conclusion is distinct from this noticing. The main disagreement is about the distinction of the aspect of indication from the indicated, and consequently about whether the knowledge of each is distinct. Al-Taftazani sides with the majority that they are distinct. He then notes a discrepancy: al-Mawāqif mentions a different framing of the disagreement, but it is not found in famous books. He then explains al-Razi's position that there are three knowledges: knowledge of the essence of the proof, knowledge of the essence of the indicated, and knowledge of the proof's being a proof. The third is relational and distinct. However, al-Razi's wording may suggest that the aspect of indication is the proof itself. Then an objection is raised from Naqd al-Muhassal: when proving God's existence from contingent things, some argue that the aspect of indication cannot be distinct because what is distinct from God is part of contingent existence, etc. The answer is that the knowledge of the aspect of indication is a mental consideration, not an external existent. But al-Taftazani criticizes this answer, saying that the mental consideration is the indication itself, not the aspect of indication which is a quality in the proof. He concludes that the answer is corrupt.

Key Terms
العلم بذات الدليل
العلم بذات المدلول
العلم بكون الدليل دليلاً
الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the knowledge of the proof's indication is the same as the knowledge of the proof itself.
Correction: The third knowledge is relational, focusing on the proof-as-proof, which is different from the essence of the proof.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, how many types of knowledge are involved in understanding a proof's indication?
A) Two: knowledge of the proof and knowledge of the indicated
B) Three: knowledge of proof, indicated, and the relation between them
C) One: knowledge of the proof alone
D) Four: including the context

Key Takeaway
The relational knowledge of wajh is distinct from both the knowledge of the proof and the knowledge of the indicated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own study of proofs for God's existence, have you ever been satisfied simply knowing the proof (e.g., the world's possibility) without understanding why it necessarily indicates a Creator? How might you be neglecting the third, relational knowledge?
?
This lesson shows that knowledge of wajh is distinct. When you present an argument to someone, do you focus on the proof and the conclusion, or do you also explain the aspect of indication? How can you ensure you convey this relational knowledge?
?
If wajh is the relation that makes the proof indicate the indicated, then your certainty depends on grasping that relation. Are you certain that you truly understand how each proof you rely on actually indicates its conclusion? Will you take time to examine that?

Objection from Naqd al-Muhassal: Wajh as Mental Consideration
Objection and answer regarding wajh's distinctness, and critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that wajh al-dalālah cannot be distinct from proof and indicated because it would entail a problem in proving God's existence, and al-Taftazani's critique of the proposed answer.

Core Idea
The claim that wajh is a mental consideration is flawed because it confuses indication with the aspect of indication.

Al-Taftazani presents an objection from Naqd al-Muhassal: when using contingent existence to prove God, some argue that wajh cannot be distinct from both proof and indicated, otherwise it would lead to a contradiction. The proposed answer that knowledge of wajh is a mental consideration is then critiqued by al-Taftazani as mixing up indication and wajh.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that in the proof of God from contingent things, wajh cannot be distinct from both the contingent existence (proof) and God's existence (indicated), because what is distinct from God is part of contingent existence, and what is distinct from contingent existence is only God.
This is a theological-specific argument.
2
Understand the proposed answer
The answer given is that the knowledge of wajh is a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī ʿaqlī), not an external thing.
This would make the distinction non-problematic.
3
Analyze al-Taftazani's critique
Al-Taftazani points out that the mental consideration is actually the indication (dalāla) itself, not the aspect of indication (wajh) which is a quality in the proof.
He concludes that the answer is corrupt.
4
Identify the key distinction
Wajh is a quality in the proof (like possibility or temporal origination), while indication is the relational concept. The objection confuses them.
This lesson solidifies that wajh is an attribute of the proof, not a mental construct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال حجة الإسلام: لما كان جهة الدلالة في القياس هو التفطن لوجود النتيجة بالقوة في المقدمة أشكل على الضعفاء فلم يعرفوا أن وجه الدلالة عين المدلول أو غيره، والحق أن المطلوب هو المدلول المنتج، وأنه غير التفطن لوجوده في المقدمة بالقوة. وبالجملة فالمشهور من الاختلاف في هذا البحث هو الاختلاف في: مغايرة جهة الدلالة للمدلول فيتفرع عليه الاختلاف في تغاير العلم بهما على ما قال الإمام الرازي وغيره أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل هل يغاير العلم بالمدلول؟ فيه خلاف والحق المغايرة. لتغاير المدلول ووجه الدلالة. وأما ما ذكر في المواقف من أن الخلاف في أن العلم بدلالة الدليل هل يغاير العلم بالمدلول وفي أن وجه الدلالة هل يغاير الدليل؟ فلم يوجد في الكتب المشهورة. إلا أن الإمام ذكر في بيان مغايرة العلم بوجه الدلالة للعلم بالمدلول أن ههنا أموراً ثلاثة: هي العلم بذات الدليل كالعلم بإمكان العالم، والعلم بذات المدلول كالعلم بأنه لا بد له من مؤثر، والعلم بكون الدليل دليلاً على المدلول ولا خفاء في تغاير الأولين وكذا في مغايرة الثالث لهما، لكونه علماً بإضافة بين الدليل والمدلول مغايرة لهما، وهذا الكلام ربما يوهم خلافاً في مغايرة العلم بدلالة الدليل للعلم بالمدلول، حيث احتيج إلى البيان وجعل العلم أنه الدلالة مثالاً بإمكان العالم مع وجه للعلم بذات الدليل يوهم القول بأن وجه الدلالة نفس الدليل، وفي نقد المحصل ما يشعر بالخلاف في وجوب مغايرته للدليل والمدلول، لأنه قال: إن هذه المسألة إنما تجري بين المتكلمين عند استدلالهم بوجود سوى الله تعالى على وجوده تعالى فيقولون: لا يجوز أن يكون وجه دلالة ما دلالة وجود سوى الله تعالى على وجوده مغايراً لهما لأن المغاير لوجوده تعالى داخل في وجود ما سواه، والمغاير لوجود ما سواه هو وجوده فقط. والجواب أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل على المدلول الذي هو مغاير لهما: هو أمر اعتباري عقلي. ليس بموجود في الخارج كما سيجيء في تحقيق التضايف. هذا كلامه وأنت خبير بأن الأمر الاعتباري الإضافي هو دلالة الدليل على المدلول لا وجه الدلالة الذي هو صفة في الدليل كالإمكان والحدوث في العالم، ثم ظاهر عبارته أن المحكوم عليه يكون أمراً اعتبارياً هو العلم بوجه الدلالة وفساده بين.
al-Ghazali said: Since the aspect of indication (jihāt al-dalāla) in syllogism is the noticing of the existence of the conclusion potentially in the premise, it became obscure to the weak, so they did not know whether the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalāla) is identical to the indicated (madlūl) or other than it. There is disagreement; and the truth is that they are distinct, due to the distinction of the indicated and the aspect of indication. However, the Imam mentioned in explaining the distinction of the knowledge of the aspect of indication from the knowledge of the indicated that there are here three things: the knowledge of the essence of the proof, like the knowledge of the possibility of the world; the knowledge of the essence of the indicated, like the knowledge that it necessarily has an effect; and the knowledge that the proof is a proof of the indicated. Commentary
This passage is an investigation into whether the aspect of indication (wajh al-dalāla) in a syllogism is identical to or distinct from the indicated (madlūl). Al-Taftazani begins by quoting al-Ghazali: The aspect of indication is the noticing of the conclusion potentially in the premise. This is obscure to some, leading to confusion. The truth is that the sought conclusion is distinct from this noticing. The main disagreement is about the distinction of the aspect of indication from the indicated, and consequently about whether the knowledge of each is distinct. Al-Taftazani sides with the majority that they are distinct. He then notes a discrepancy: al-Mawāqif mentions a different framing of the disagreement, but it is not found in famous books. He then explains al-Razi's position that there are three knowledges: knowledge of the essence of the proof, knowledge of the essence of the indicated, and knowledge of the proof's being a proof. The third is relational and distinct. However, al-Razi's wording may suggest that the aspect of indication is the proof itself. Then an objection is raised from Naqd al-Muhassal: when proving God's existence from contingent things, some argue that the aspect of indication cannot be distinct because what is distinct from God is part of contingent existence, etc. The answer is that the knowledge of the aspect of indication is a mental consideration, not an external existent. But al-Taftazani criticizes this answer, saying that the mental consideration is the indication itself, not the aspect of indication which is a quality in the proof. He concludes that the answer is corrupt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the aspect of indication is a mental construct like an iʿtibārī concept.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that it is an actual quality in the proof (e.g., possibility), not a mental consideration.

Checkpoint
What is the main error in the answer from Naqd al-Muhassal according to al-Taftazani?
A) It denies that wajh is distinct from proof and indicated
B) It confuses the indication (dalāla) with the aspect of indication (wajh)
C) It claims that wajh is an external thing
D) It ignores the relational nature of knowledge

Key Takeaway
The aspect of indication (wajh) is a real quality in the proof, not a mental construct; the indication itself is the relational mental consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the signs of God in the universe, do you focus more on the intellectual exercise of proving His existence (the indication) or on the actual attributes of the signs that point to Him (the aspects)? Which approach brings you closer to certainty?
?
The passage warns against mistaking a mental concept for a real quality. In your relationship with Allah, have you ever been satisfied with a mental picture of His attributes rather than allowing those attributes to transform your heart? What would change if you treated the signs as real, demanding your full response?
?
Al-Taftazani shows that a small confusion (between dalala and wajh) can corrupt an entire argument. Are there areas in your understanding of faith where a similar confusion might be leading you to a wrong conclusion? What step can you take today to clarify that distinction?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
6 min
The Way of Indication as a Mental Construct
Commentator defines wajh al-dalāla as mental relational entity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the 'way of indication' (wajh al-dalāla) is a mental relational construct, not an external attribute inherent in the evidence.

Core Idea
The way of indication is a mental relation, not a physical attribute.

This passage from the commentary tackles a subtle but essential distinction in Islamic logic and theology: the nature of how evidence points to its conclusion. The commentator clarifies that the 'way of indication' is a mental construct (amr i'tibārī 'aqlī), not an inherent quality of the evidence itself. This sets the stage for understanding relational concepts in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms like 'amr i'tibārī 'aqlī'.
2
Identify the main claim
The commentator states that knowledge of the way of indication is a mental relational thing. Identify this claim and note that it does not exist externally.
3
Distinguish between two concepts
The passage distinguishes between the relational mental thing (the actual indication) and the way of indication as an attribute in the evidence (like contingency). Understand the difference.
The attribute is part of the evidence, while the relation is between evidence and indicated.
4
Notice the criticism
The commentator criticizes the author's phrasing, pointing out an apparent error in treating the predicated thing as knowledge of the way of indication.
This shows the commentator's role in refining doctrinal language.
5
Reflect on importance
Consider why this distinction matters for theological argumentation: it affects how we understand evidence and inference in kalam.
This connects to broader debates about mental vs. external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب أن العلم بوجه دلالة الدليل على المدلول الذي هو مغاير لهما : هو أمر اعتباري عقلي. ليس بموجود في الخارج كما سيجيء في تحقيق التضايف. هذا كلامه (۱) وأنت خبير بأن الأمر الاعتباري الإضافي هو دلالة الدليل على المدلول لا وجه الدلالة الذي هو صفة في الدليل كالإمكان والحدوث في العالم، ثم ظاهر عبارته أن المحكوم عليه يكون أمراً اعتبارياً هو العلم بوجه الدلالة وفساده بين.
The answer is that knowledge of the way of indication of the evidence to the indicated, which is different from both of them, is a mental relational thing (amr i'tibari 'aqli). It does not exist externally, as will come in the investigation of correlation (al-tadāyuf). This is his statement. And you are aware that the relational mental thing is the indication of the evidence to the indicated, not the way of indication which is an attribute in the evidence, like contingency (al-imkān) and origination (al-ḥudūth) in the world. Then the apparent meaning of his statement is that the predicated thing is a mental thing which is knowledge of the way of indication, and its corruption is clear.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a definition and clarification of the concept of 'way of indication' (wajh al-dalāla). The commentator begins by stating the answer to a prior objection: knowledge of the way an evidence points to its indicated thing (which is separate from both the evidence and the indicated) is a mental relational entity (amr i'tibārī 'aqlī), not an external existent. He then distinguishes between the relational mental thing, which is the actual indication relation, and the way of indication itself, which is an attribute in the evidence (like contingency or temporal origination in the world). The commentator then criticizes the author's phrasing, noting that the author seems to treat the thing being predicated as a mental thing that is knowledge of the way of indication, which the commentator finds erroneous.

Key Terms
وجه الدلالة
أمر اعتباري عقلي
التضايف
الإمكان
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the mental relation (dalāla) with the attribute in the evidence (wajh al-dalāla).
Correction: The way of indication is an attribute in the evidence (like contingency), while the indication itself is a mental relation between evidence and indicated.

Checkpoint
What is the nature of 'knowledge of the way of indication' according to the commentator?
A) A physical entity existing externally.
B) A mental relational thing (amr i'tibari aqli).
C) An attribute in the evidence itself.
D) The same as the indicated.

Key Takeaway
The 'way of indication' is a mental relation, not an attribute inherent in the evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the evidence for God's existence, do you sometimes confuse the attribute of the evidence (like the world's contingency) with your mental understanding of how it points to God? How might this confusion weaken your certainty, and what can you do to keep them distinct?
?
The commentator criticizes the author's phrasing, showing that even scholars must be precise. Are you willing to examine your own understanding of theological terms with the same rigor, even if it means realizing you have been unclear? What is at stake for your faith if you hold a confused concept?
?
The way of indication is a real attribute in the evidence, not just a mental construct. Do you treat the signs of God in creation as having real, objective properties that demand a response, or do you reduce them to subjective thoughts? How should this distinction change the way you engage with the world around you?

The Essential Condition of Definition: Coextensiveness and the Hadd/Rasm Distinction
Sanusi states the necessary condition for a valid definiens and introduces the hadd/rasm division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental condition that a valid definition must be coextensive (preventing exit and entry) and how this leads to the distinction between essential definition (hadd) and descriptive definition (rasm).

Core Idea
A valid definition must fit all and only the members of the defined term.

Before diving into the types of definition, Sanusi establishes a non-negotiable condition: the definiens must exactly match the definiendum. This simple logical requirement is the foundation for distinguishing between a proper essential definition and a mere descriptive trace. Let us see how he frames this condition and the immediate consequence for the terms hadd and rasm.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on coextensiveness
Read the Arabic sentence carefully, noting the twofold requirement: no individual of the definiendum may be left out, and no outsider may be let in. This is the condition called 'coextensiveness' or 'al-musāwāt'.
This is the baseline for all valid definitions.
2
Distinguish between hadd and rasm
Notice that the author says if this coextensiveness is based on essence and reality, the definition is more worthy of the name 'hadd'; if based on accidents, it is called 'rasm' (a trace). Reflect on why accident-based definitions are like traces—they point to the thing without revealing its essence.
Rasm is still a valid definition but secondary to hadd.
3
Check your understanding
Ask yourself: Can a definition that uses accidental properties ever be coextensive? The text implies yes, but it is still a rasm, not a hadd. This prevents a common misunderstanding.
The key is the basis (essence vs accident), not the coextensiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بد في المعرف من منع خروج شيء من الأفراد، ودخول شيء فيه مما سواها، فما كان ذلك فيه باعتبار الذات والحقيقة كان أولى بهذا الاسم، وإن كان عرضياً لها المعرف رسماً لكونه بمنزلة الأثر يستدل به على الطريق. ثم إن المميز إن كان مع كمال الجزء المشترك أعني ما يقع جواب السؤال بما هو عن الماهية وعن كل ما يشاركها وهو المسمى بالجنس القريب فالمعرف تام، أما الحد فلاشتماله على جميع الذاتيات وأما الرسم فلاشتماله على كمال الذاتي المشترك، وكمال العرضي المميز، وإلا فناقص، فالحد التام واحد ليس إلا وهو الجنس القريب مع الفصل القريب ويشترط تقديم الجنس حتى لو أخر لكان الحد ناقصاً، ومبنى هذا الكلام على أنه لا اعتبار بالعرض العام لأنه لا يفيد الامتياز، ولا الاطلاع على أجزاء الماهية ولا بالخاصة مع الفصل القريب، وإلا يلزم أن يكون المركب من الفصل القريب مع العرض العام أو مع الخاصة حداً ناقصاً، وليس كذلك في اصطلاح الجمهور، حيث خصوا اسم الحد بما يكون من محض الذاتيات وقد يصطلح على تسمية كل معرف حداً حتى اللفظي منه أعني بيان مدلول اللفظ بلفظ آخر أوضح دلالة، وكثير من المتقدمين على أن الرسم التام ما يفيد امتياز الماهية عن جميع ما عداها، والناقص ما يفيد الامتياز عن البعض فقط، إلا أنه استقر رأي المتأخرين على اشتراط كون المعرف مساوياً أي مطرداً، ومنعكساً، حتى لا يجوز التعريف بالأعم محافظة على الضبط
It is necessary in the definiens that it prevent the exit of any individual [from the definiendum] and the entry of anything else into it. So whatever possesses this [characteristic] in terms of essence and reality is more entitled to this name [i.e., definition], even if it is accidental to what is defined as a rasm, because it is like a trace by which the path is inferred. Then, if the differentiator is accompanied by the completeness of the common part—meaning what answers the question 'what is it?' concerning the quiddity and concerning everything that shares in it, which is called the proximate genus—then the definiens is perfect (tāmm). As for the ḥadd, it is because it includes all the essentialia; as for the rasm, it is because it includes the completeness of the common essential and the completeness of the accidental differentiator. Otherwise, it is deficient (nāqiṣ). Thus, the perfect ḥadd is only one, namely, the proximate genus together with the proximate differentia, and it is required to present the genus first, so that if it were delayed, the ḥadd would be deficient. The basis of this statement is that no consideration is given to the general accident because it does not afford distinctiveness, nor acquaintance with the parts of the quiddity, nor to the proper accident together with the proximate differentia; otherwise, it would follow that the composite of proximate differentia with general accident or with proper accident would be a deficient ḥadd. But this is not so in the terminology of the majority, for they restrict the name 'ḥadd' to what is composed purely of essentialia. However, it is customary to call every definiens a 'ḥadd', even the verbal one, i.e., explaining the meaning of a word by another word that is clearer in indication. Many of the early scholars hold that the perfect rasm is what affords distinction of the quiddity from everything else, and the deficient rasm is what affords distinction from only some [others]. But the later scholars have settled on the condition that the definiens be coextensive—i.e., universal in application (muṭṭarid) and reversible (munʿakis)—so that definition by a broader term is not permitted, in order to preserve precision.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions of a valid definition (al-taʿrīf) in the context of logic and kalam methodology. The author begins by stating a fundamental requirement: the definiens (al-muʿarraf) must be coextensive with the definiendum—it must prevent any member of the definiendum from being excluded, and prevent any non-member from being included. If this coextensiveness is based on the essence and reality of the thing, the definition is more properly called a ḥadd (strict definition); if it is based on accidental properties, it is called a rasm (descriptive definition) because it serves as a trace to the quiddity. The author then classifies definitions as perfect (tāmm) or deficient (nāqiṣ) based on whether the differentiator (al-mumayyiz) is accompanied by the complete common part (the proximate genus, al-jins al-qarīb). If the differentiator is essential (a proximate differentia, al-faṣl al-qarīb) and the genus is present, the definition is a perfect ḥadd, because it includes all essential constituents; if the differentiator is accidental but still complete with the common part, it is a perfect rasm. Otherwise, the definition is deficient. The author emphasizes that the perfect ḥadd is unique: the proximate genus plus the proximate differentia, with the genus stated first. The passage then clarifies that general accidents (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm) are not considered because they do not distinguish the species, and proper accidents (al-khāṣṣa) together with the proximate differentia would lead to a deficient ḥadd according to the majority terminology, which restricts 'ḥadd' to purely essential components. However, some terminologies allow any definiens to be called ḥadd, even a verbal explanation. Finally, the author notes that early scholars allowed a rasm to be either perfect (distinguishing from all others) or deficient (distinguishing from some), but later scholars insisted on coextensiveness (musāwāt)—i.e., the definiens must be universal in application (muṭṭarid) and reversible (munʿakis)—to ensure precision, thus prohibiting definition by a broader term.

Key Terms
المعرف
الحد
الرسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any definition containing an accidental property is automatically a rasm, regardless of coextensiveness.
Correction: The text clarifies that a rasm must still satisfy the coextensiveness condition; it is not just any accidental description. Moreover, an accident-based definition can only be a rasm, but a definition that includes both essential and accidental properties may still be considered a ḥadd by some terminologies (as seen later).

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental condition that any valid definiens must satisfy according to Sanusi?
A) It must be based on essential properties only.
B) It must prevent the exit of any individual of the definiendum and the entry of anything else.
C) It must be shorter than the definiendum.
D) It must be a single word.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must exactly match its definiendum; the type (hadd or rasm) depends on whether the match is based on essence or accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new Islamic teaching, do you take the time to ensure your understanding includes all that the Qur'an and Sunnah say and excludes anything they do not? The condition of coextensiveness demands vigilance—will you commit to checking your definitions against the sources today?
?
You may have heard people define 'iman' as 'belief in the heart,' but does that definition include all necessary elements (e.g., speech and actions) and exclude what is not part of iman (e.g., mere knowledge)? Reflect on one belief you hold about Allah: is your description based on essential or accidental properties? How can you move toward a hadd-like precision?
?
In your daily life, do you define others (e.g., 'a true Muslim') by accidental traits (e.g., culture, appearance) rather than by the essential properties of belief and practice? This lesson on the hadd/rasm distinction is a mirror for your own judgments—ask yourself: do I define myself by essence or accident before Allah?

Perfect and Deficient Definitions: The Role of Proximate Genus and Differentia
Sanusi classifies definitions as perfect or deficient based on the completeness of the common part and differentiator, and explains the unique perfect hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how definitions are classified as perfect (tāmm) or deficient (nāqiṣ) based on the completeness of the common part (proximate genus) and the differentiator, and what constitutes the unique perfect hadd.

Core Idea
A perfect definition includes the complete proximate genus and a complete differentiator; otherwise, it is deficient.

Having established the coextensiveness condition, Sanusi now focuses on the internal structure of definitions. He explains that a definition's perfection depends on whether the differentiator (mumayyiz) is accompanied by a complete common part (the proximate genus). This leads to a classification of definitions into perfect and deficient, and a clear statement of the unique perfect hadd. Let us unpack this intricate logical framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification framework
Read the passage from 'thumma inna al-mumayyiz' onward. The author explains that if the differentiator is accompanied by the complete common part (proximate genus), the definiens is perfect (tāmm). Otherwise, it is deficient (nāqiṣ). Note that this applies to both hadd and rasm.
The common part is the answer to 'what is it?' shared by the species and its genus.
2
Identify the perfect hadd
The author states that the perfect hadd is only one: the proximate genus together with the proximate differentia, with the genus presented first. This is the most precise essential definition. Ask yourself why the order matters.
Delaying the genus would make the definition deficient.
3
Examine the discussion of general and proper accidents
Read the section starting 'Wa mabnā hādhā al-kalām...' The author argues that the general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) is not considered because it does not provide distinction, nor does the proper accident (khāṣṣa) with the proximate differentia, because that would lead to a deficient hadd according to the majority terminology. Understand why.
The majority restricts 'hadd' to purely essential components.

?

?

In your personal belief system, do you prioritize the most fundamental truths (like God's existence and oneness) before contemplating His attributes? Or do you sometimes mix them up, putting attributes before essence? Reflect: are your religious priorities correctly ordered in your heart and practice? Terminological Variations and the Later Requirement of Coextensiveness
Sanusi discusses terminological variations and the later consensus on coextensiveness as a strict requirement for all definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different terminological uses of 'ḥadd', the early vs. later views on perfect and deficient rasm, and the later scholars' insistence on strict coextensiveness (musāwāt) to prevent definition by a broader term.

Core Idea
Later scholars tightened definition rules: the definiens must be exactly coextensive, not broader.

After presenting the core classification, Sanusi addresses variations in terminology among scholars. Some use 'ḥadd' loosely for any definiens, even verbal explanations. Early scholars allowed a rasm to be either perfect (distinguishing from all) or deficient (distinguishing from some). But the later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirūn) insisted that the definiens must be 'musāwī'—exactly coextensive—to ensure precision, prohibiting definitions that are broader or narrower. This passage clarifies these refinements.

Guided Path
1
Note the terminological flexibility of 'ḥadd'
Read the sentence beginning 'Wa qad yuṣṭalaḥu...' The author notes that some terminologies call every definiens a 'ḥadd', even verbal explanations. Understand that this is a broader usage than the majority's restricted sense.
This shows that key terms can vary by school or period.
2
Compare early and later views on rasm
Read the section 'Wa kathīr min al-mutaqaddimīn...' Early scholars held that a rasm can be perfect (distinguishing from all) or deficient (distinguishing from some). The later scholars, however, required that every definiens be coextensive—universal and reversible—to be valid, thus rejecting deficient rasm.
This shift reflects a drive for greater logical precision.
3
Understand the consequence for definition by a broader term
The author concludes that because of the coextensiveness requirement, definition by a broader term (e.g., defining 'man' as 'animal') is not permitted, as it would not be reversible. Reflect on why this matters for kalam arguments.
Coextensiveness ensures that the definition accurately delimits the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It is necessary in the definiens that it prevent the exit of any individual [from the definiendum] and the entry of anything else into it. So whatever possesses this [characteristic] in terms of essence and reality is more entitled to this name [i.e., definition], even if it is accidental to what is defined as a rasm, because it is like a trace by which the path is inferred. Then, if the differentiator is accompanied by the completeness of the common part—meaning what answers the question 'what is it?' concerning the quiddity and concerning everything that shares in it, which is called the proximate genus—then the definiens is perfect (tāmm). As for the ḥadd, it is because it includes all the essentialia; as for the rasm, it is because it includes the completeness of the common essential and the completeness of the accidental differentiator. Otherwise, it is deficient (nāqiṣ). Thus, the perfect ḥadd is only one, namely, the proximate genus together with the proximate differentia, and it is required to present the genus first, so that if it were delayed, the ḥadd would be deficient. The basis of this statement is that no consideration is given to the general accident because it does not afford distinctiveness, nor acquaintance with the parts of the quiddity, nor to the proper accident together with the proximate differentia; otherwise, it would follow that the composite of proximate differentia with general accident or with proper accident would be a deficient ḥadd. But this is not so in the terminology of the majority, for they restrict the name 'ḥadd' to what is composed purely of essentialia. However, it is customary to call every definiens a 'ḥadd', even the verbal one, i.e., explaining the meaning of a word by another word that is clearer in indication. Many of the early scholars hold that the perfect rasm is what affords distinction of the quiddity from everything else, and the deficient rasm is what affords distinction from only some [others]. But the later scholars have settled on the condition that the definiens be coextensive—i.e., universal in application (muṭṭarid) and reversible (munʿakis)—so that definition by a broader term is not permitted, in order to preserve precision.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions of a valid definition (al-taʿrīf) in the context of logic and kalam methodology. The author begins by stating a fundamental requirement: the definiens (al-muʿarraf) must be coextensive with the definiendum—it must prevent any member of the definiendum from being excluded, and prevent any non-member from being included. If this coextensiveness is based on the essence and reality of the thing, the definition is more properly called a ḥadd (strict definition); if it is based on accidental properties, it is called a rasm (descriptive definition) because it serves as a trace to the quiddity. The author then classifies definitions as perfect (tāmm) or deficient (nāqiṣ) based on whether the differentiator (al-mumayyiz) is accompanied by the complete common part (the proximate genus, al-jins al-qarīb). If the differentiator is essential (a proximate differentia, al-faṣl al-qarīb) and the genus is present, the definition is a perfect ḥadd, because it includes all essential constituents; if the differentiator is accidental but still complete with the common part, it is a perfect rasm. Otherwise, the definition is deficient. The author emphasizes that the perfect ḥadd is unique: the proximate genus plus the proximate differentia, with the genus stated first. The passage then clarifies that general accidents (al-ʿaraḍ al-ʿāmm) are not considered because they do not distinguish the species, and proper accidents (al-khāṣṣa) together with the proximate differentia would lead to a deficient ḥadd according to the majority terminology, which restricts 'ḥadd' to purely essential components. However, some terminologies allow any definiens to be called ḥadd, even a verbal explanation. Finally, the author notes that early scholars allowed a rasm to be either perfect (distinguishing from all others) or deficient (distinguishing from some), but later scholars insisted on coextensiveness (musāwāt)—i.e., the definiens must be universal in application (muṭṭarid) and reversible (munʿakis)—to ensure precision, thus prohibiting definition by a broader term.

Key Terms
مطرد
منعكس
مساو
متقدمون
متأخرون
اللفظي

Checkpoint

C) It must be a verbal explanation.
D) It must be perfect in distinguishing from all others.

Key Takeaway
Later kalam scholars required definitions to be exactly coextensive, rejecting broader terms and deficient rasm, to ensure logical precision.

The Three Types of Argument: Qiyas, Istiqra', and Tamthil
Defines dalīl and classifies three types of argument with logical relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of argument (qiyas, istiqra', tamthil) and the logical principle of correspondence that distinguishes them.

Core Idea
Arguments are classified by how premises and conclusion relate: containment defines the type.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid lays the foundation for understanding how proofs work in Islamic logic. It defines what a proof (dalīl) is, then classifies arguments into three categories based on the logical relationship between the evidence and the desired conclusion. The author also clarifies a common misunderstanding about what it means for one thing to 'fall under' another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the entire lesson. Notice the technical terms and the logical flow.
The passage is dense; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Understand the definition of dalīl and hujja
Identify how the author defines a proof (dalīl) and why it is also called a hujja. Focus on the idea of guidance and overcoming the opponent.
3
Learn the necessity of correspondence (munasaba)
The author states that there must be a correspondence between the argument and the desired conclusion. Understand why this is necessary and the two possibilities: containment or no containment.
The correspondence condition is the key to distinguishing the three types.
4
Explore the three types of argument
Study each type: qiyas (syllogism), istiqra' (induction), and tamthil (analogy). Note how containment determines the type: if the argument contains the conclusion, it is qiyas; if the conclusion contains the argument, it is istiqra'; if neither contains the other, a third common factor is needed, which is tamthil.
The Imam's summary reinforces this classification.
5
Examine the nuance of 'falling under' (indirāj)
The author clarifies that 'falling under' implies encompassing, not mere predication. Understand why relative particular is used instead of real particular, and how this refines the classification.
This part guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموصل إلى التصديق ويسمى الدليل لما فيه من الإرشاد إلى المطلوب والحجة لما في التمسك به من الغلبة على الخصم إما قياس، وإما استقراء، وإما تمثيل، إذ لا بد من مناسبة بين الحجة والمطلوب ليمكن استفادته منها، وتلك المناسبة إما أن تكون باشتمال أحدهما على الآخر أو لا وعلى الأول فإن اشتملت الحجة على المطلوب فهي القياس إذ النتيجة مندرجة في مقدمتيه، وإن اشتمل المطلوب على الحجة فهي الاستقراء، إذ المطلوب حكم كلي يثبت بتحقق الحكم على الجزئيات المندرجة تحته، وعلى الثاني لا بد أن يكون هناك أمر ثالث يشتمل عليهما، أو يندرجان فيه ليستفاد العلم بأحدهما من الآخر وهو التمثيل، فإن حكم الفرع وهو المطلوب يستفاد من حكم الأصل وهو الحجة، لاندراجهما تحت الجامع الذي هو العلة، وهذا ما قال الإمام : إنا إذا استدللنا بشيء على شيء فإن لم يدخل أحدهما تحت الآخر فهو التمثيل، وإن دخل فإما أن يستدل بالكلي على الجزئي وهو القياس، أو بالعكس وهو الاستقراء، وذكر في بعض كتبه بدل الكلي والجزئي الأعم والأخص تصريحاً بأن المراد الجزئي الإضافي لا الحقيقي، وتنبيهاً على أن التفسير الجزئي الإضافي بالمندرج تحت الغير مساو لتفسيره بالأخص تحت الأعم لا أعم منه . على ما سبق إلى بعض الأوهام من أن معنى اندراجه تحت الغير مجرد صدق الغير عليه كلياً، وذلك لأن لفظ الاندراج منبىء عن كون الغير شاملاً له ولغيره، ولم يعرف من اصطلاح القوم أن كلا من المتساويين جزئي إضافي للآخر، فلهذا قال صاحب الطوالع : إن استدل بالكلي على الجزئي
And that which leads to assent (al-taṣdīq) is called the proof (al-dalīl) because it contains guidance to the desired [conclusion], and the argument (al-ḥujja) because holding to it brings victory over the opponent. It is either syllogism (qiyās), or induction (istiqrāʾ), or analogy (tamthīl). For there must be a correspondence between the argument and the desired [conclusion] so that it can be derived from it. That correspondence is either by one of them containing the other or not. In the first case, if the argument contains the desired [conclusion], it is qiyās, because the conclusion is included in its premises. If the desired [conclusion] contains the argument, it is istiqrāʾ, because the desired [conclusion] is a universal judgment established by the verification of the judgment on the particulars subsumed under it. In the second case, there must be a third thing that contains both of them, or into which they both fall, so that knowledge of one of them from the other can be derived, and that is tamthīl. For the judgment of the branch (al-farʿ)—which is the desired [conclusion]—is derived from the judgment of the root (al-aṣl)—which is the argument—because they both fall under the common factor (al-jāmiʿ) which is the cause (al-ʿilla). This is what the Imām said: 'When we infer one thing from another, if one of them does not fall under the other, it is tamthīl; if it does fall [under the other], then either the universal is used to infer the particular, which is qiyās, or the reverse, which is istiqrāʾ.' And he mentioned in some of his books, instead of 'universal' and 'particular', 'the more general' and 'the more specific', explicitly stating that what is meant is the relative particular, not the real [particular], and as a warning that the interpretation of the relative particular as that which falls under another is equivalent to its interpretation as the more specific under the more general, not more general than it. This is because some misunderstood that the meaning of its falling under another is simply that the other is true of it universally. But the term 'falling under' implies that the other encompasses it and other things, and it is not known in the terminology of the people that each of two coextensive terms is a relative particular of the other. Hence, the author of al-Ṭawāliʿ said: 'If one infers the universal from the particular...'
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'proof' (al-dalīl) and classifies arguments into three types: qiyās (syllogism), istiqrāʾ (induction), and tamthīl (analogy). The author begins by stating that the means that leads to assent (al-taṣdīq) is called a proof (dalīl) because it guides to the desired conclusion, and also a ḥujja (argument) because it enables one to overcome an opponent. He then asserts that there must be a correspondence (munāsaba) between the argument (the premise) and the desired conclusion so that the latter can be derived from the former. This correspondence is either by one containing the other or not. In the first case, if the argument (the premise set) contains the conclusion, it is qiyās—since the conclusion is included in the premises. If the conclusion contains the argument, it is istiqrāʾ—because the conclusion is a universal judgment established by verifying the judgment on the particulars subsumed under it. In the second case (neither contains the other fully), a third thing must exist that contains both, or into which both fall, so that knowledge of one from the other can be derived; this is tamthīl. In tamthīl, the judgment of the branch (farʿ, the desired conclusion) is derived from the judgment of the root (aṣl, the argument) because both fall under a common factor (jāmiʿ) which is the cause (ʿilla). The author then cites an Imam (likely al-Ghazālī or Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) who summarizes: when inferring one thing from another, if one does not fall under the other, it is tamthīl; if it does fall, then either the universal is used to infer the particular (qiyās) or the particular to infer the universal (istiqrāʾ). The Imam also used 'the more general' and 'the more specific' instead of 'universal' and 'particular' to clarify that what is meant is relative particular (juzʾī iḍāfī), not real particular. The author explains that 'falling under' implies encompassing, not mere predication, and that coextensive terms are not considered relative particulars of each other. The passage ends with a quotation from the author of al-Ṭawāliʿ (al-Bayḍāwī) about inferring the universal from the particular, which continues in the next page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'falling under' (indirāj) means mere universal predication (i.e., that the predicate is true of the subject).
Correction: The author explains that 'falling under' implies that the broader term encompasses the narrower term and other things as well, not just predication. Coextensive terms do not qualify as relative particulars for each other.

Checkpoint
In the classification of arguments, what is the defining characteristic of tamthīl (analogy)?
A) The argument contains the conclusion
B) The conclusion contains the argument
C) Neither contains the other, and a third common factor is needed
D) It is the same as qiyās but with an extra premise

Key Takeaway
The classification of arguments into qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil is based on the logical containment relationship between the evidence and the conclusion, with tamthil requiring a third common factor.

When you defend a belief about Allah, do you ever rely on analogies that are not grounded in a valid common cause (عِلَّة)? How can you ensure your arguments are structurally sound like qiyas rather than weak tamthil?
?
Have you ever assumed that two things are in a containment relationship when in fact they are merely coextensive? This passage warns against that. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you confuse the general with the containing? How will you check your reasoning?
?
The passage defines arguments by logical containment. Consider your own process of seeking knowledge. Do you prioritize building syllogistic proofs from the Qur'an and Sunnah, or do you often settle for superficial analogies? What change do you need to make today?

Three Types of Reasoning in Kalam
Classification of arguments into qiyas, istiqra', and tamthil based on containment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of reasoning (qiyas, istiqra', tamthil) and how they are distinguished based on containment between evidence and conclusion.

Core Idea
Reasoning is classified by whether evidence contains conclusion, conclusion contains evidence, or a third thing contains both.

In Islamic logic and kalam, arguments are not all the same. Scholars like al-Sanusi classified reasoning into three fundamental types based on the relationship between the evidence (hujja) and the sought conclusion (matlub). This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents that classification clearly, using the concept of containment (ishtimal) or inclusion (indiraj). By understanding these types, you will see how different reasoning strategies work in theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two main divisions
The author first divides arguments based on whether the evidence and conclusion contain each other or not. Pay attention to the condition of 'suitability' (munasaba).
Suitability here means a logical relation that allows inference.
2
Learn qiyas and istiqra'
If the evidence contains the conclusion, it is qiyas (syllogism). If the conclusion contains the evidence, it is istiqra' (induction). Note how the direction of containment defines each.
In qiyas, the conclusion is already implicit in the premises.
3
Understand tamthil
When neither contains the other, a third thing (the common cause) must contain both. This is tamthil (analogy). The judgment of the branch is derived from the root due to the shared cause.
Tamthil is often used in legal reasoning (qiyas fiqhi).
4
Review the Imam's summary
The passage ends with a concise summary from 'the Imam': reasoning either involves falling under (dukhul) or not, leading to the three types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منها، وتلك المناسبة إما أن تكون باشتمال أحدهما على الآخر أو لا وعلى الأول فإن اشتملت الحجة على المطلوب فهي القياس إذ النتيجة مندرجة في مقدمتيه، وإن اشتمل المطلوب على الحجة فهي الاستقراء، إذ المطلوب حكم كلي يثبت بتحقق الحكم على الجزئيات المندرجة تحته، وعلى الثاني لا بد أن يكون هناك أمر ثالث يشتمل عليهما، أو يندرجان فيه ليستفاد العلم بأحدهما من الآخر وهو التمثيل، فإن حكم الفرع وهو المطلوب يستفاد من حكم الأصل وهو الحجة، لاندراجهما تحت الجامع الذي هو العلة، وهذا ما قال الإمام : إنا إذا استدللنا بشيء على شيء فإن لم يدخل أحدهما تحت الآخر فهو التمثيل، وإن دخل فإما أن يستدل بالكلي على الجزئي وهو القياس، أو بالعكس وهو الاستقراء
Among them, that suitability is either by one containing the other or not. In the first case, if the evidence contains the sought conclusion, it is qiyas (syllogism), because the conclusion is included in its two premises. If the sought conclusion contains the evidence, it is istiqra' (induction), because the sought conclusion is a universal judgment established by verifying the judgment on the particulars included under it. In the second case, there must be a third thing that contains them both, or they are both included in it, so that knowledge of one from the other is derived, and that is tamthil (analogy). For the judgment of the branch, which is the sought conclusion, is derived from the judgment of the root, which is the evidence, because they are both included under the common factor which is the cause. This is what the Imam said: 'If we reason from one thing to another, and one does not fall under the other, it is tamthil. If it does fall, then either we reason from the universal to the particular, which is qiyas, or the opposite, which is istiqra'.'
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines the three basic types of reasoning used in kalam and logic. The author begins by stating that the suitability (munasaba) between evidence and conclusion can be of two kinds: either one contains the other or not. If the evidence contains the conclusion, it is qiyas (syllogism), because the conclusion is implicitly present in the premises. If the conclusion contains the evidence, it is istiqra' (induction), because a universal conclusion is drawn from particulars. If neither contains the other, a third term must contain both, and that is tamthil (analogy), where the branch (far') is judged by the root (asl) due to a shared cause ('illa). The author then quotes the Imam to confirm this classification, using the language of 'falling under' (dukhul) and mentioning the universal/particular distinction.

Key Terms
قياس
استقراء
تمثيل
حجة
مطلوب
اندراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think tamthil is always based on a shared cause, but the passage specifies that the shared cause is only needed when there is no direct containment.
Correction: Tamthil requires a third thing (the shared cause) that contains both the evidence and the conclusion because they do not contain each other.

Checkpoint
What type of reasoning occurs when the evidence contains the sought conclusion?
A) Istiqra'
B) Qiyas
C) Tamthil
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The three types of reasoning are distinguished by whether the evidence contains the conclusion (qiyas), the conclusion contains the evidence (istiqra'), or a third term contains both (tamthil).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This week, whenever you make an argument, pause to identify which type of reasoning you are using and whether it meets the conditions of this passage.

Istiqra' (induction) requires examining many particulars before drawing a universal conclusion. In your personal study of Islam, have you ever made a sweeping judgment about a ruling or attribute based on only one or two examples? Commit to gathering sufficient evidence before concluding, as the definition of istiqra' demands.
?
Tamthil depends on a valid shared cause. When you compare Allah to His creation in any way, do you ensure that the commonality is real and not a mere metaphor? How can you test the 'illa (cause) to avoid misattributing human qualities to Allah? Resolve to study the conditions for valid analogy in Islamic theology.

Substitution of 'Aam/Akhass and Relative Particular
The author explains the substitution of terms and clarifies relative particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why logicians substitute 'aam and akhass for kulli and juz'i, and what the relative particular means in this context.

Core Idea
Relative particular is a more specific term under a more general one, not a universal.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies a subtle but important shift in terminology. Imam al-Sanusi replaces the terms universal and particular with more general and more specific to emphasize that the particular in question is relative, not absolute. Understanding this distinction prevents a common misunderstanding about what it means for something to 'fall under' something else.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the phrase 'al-juz'i al-idafi'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that the author substitutes 'aam and akhass for kulli and juz'i. He explicitly states that what is meant is the relative particular, not the real particular. Ask yourself: what is the difference between a relative particular and a real particular?
The relative particular is defined as 'the more specific under the more general'.
3
Understand the correction
The author warns against a misconception: some thought that 'falling under' means merely that the universal is true of the particular. The term 'indiraj' implies that the encompassing term covers both the particular and others. Also note that two equal terms are not each relative particular of the other.
This leads to the next lesson about qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في بعض كتبه بدل الكلي والجزئي الأعم والأخص تصريحاً بأن المراد الجزئي الإضافي لا الحقيقي، وتنبيهاً على أن التفسير الجزئي الإضافي بالمندرج تحت الغير مساو لتفسيره بالأخص تحت الأعم لا أعم منه . على ما سبق إلى بعض الأوهام من أن معنى اندراجه تحت الغير مجرد صدق الغير عليه كلياً، وذلك لأن لفظ الاندراج منبىء عن كون الغير شاملاً له ولغيره، ولم يعرف من اصطلاح القوم أن كلا من المتساويين جزئي إضافي للآخر، فلهذا قال صاحب الطوالع
And he mentioned in some of his books, instead of 'universal' and 'particular', 'the more general' and 'the more specific', explicitly stating that what is meant is the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi), not the real particular, and as a warning that the explanation of the relative particular as 'that which falls under something else' is equivalent to explaining it as 'the more specific under the more general', not as 'more general than it', contrary to what has occurred to some illusions that the meaning of its falling under something else is merely that the other is true of it universally. That is because the term 'indiraj' implies that the other encompasses it and others, and it is not known from the terminology of the people that each of two equals is a relative particular for the other. For this reason, the author of al-Tawali' said...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage clarifies a subtle point in logical terminology. The Imam substituted the terms 'aam (more general) and akhass (more specific) for the standard terms kulli (universal) and juz'i (particular), emphasizing that what is meant is the relative particular (al-juz'i al-idafi), not the real particular (which would be an individual). He warns that explaining relative particular as 'what falls under something else' is the same as explaining it as 'the more specific under the more general', not as 'more general than it'. Some had mistakenly thought that 'falling under' simply means the universal is true of it, but the term 'indiraj' implies that the encompassing term covers it and others. The author then notes that according to the terminology of the logicians, two equal terms are not considered relative particulars of each other, which is why sahib al-Tawali' gave a different definition of qiyas (to be seen in the next lesson).

Key Terms
جزئي إضافي
أعم
أخص
اندراج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some thought that 'falling under' simply means the universal is true of the particular, without implication of others.
Correction: The term 'indiraj' implies that the encompassing term covers both the particular and other things, so relative particular is more specific, not merely universally applicable.

Checkpoint
What does 'relative particular' (al-juz'i al-idafi) mean in this context?
A) A true individual
B) Something more specific than another
C) Something equal to another
D) Something more general than another

Key Takeaway
The relative particular is defined as the more specific under the more general, and it is not the same as a universal or an equal term.

The author warns against a misconception that could arise from sloppy thinking. Sahib al-Tawali's Definition of Qiyas and the Better Formulation
Sahib al-Tawali defines qiyas to include equal terms, an objection is raised, and a better formulation is given.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the problem with Sahib al-Tawali's definition of qiyas and how the better formulation, focusing on the concept of the middle term being always more general, resolves it.

Core Idea
Qiyas relies on the middle term's concept, which is always more general by definition.

In his definition of qiyas, Sahib al-Tawali included reasoning from equals, but this left a gap: it didn't account for cases where terms are coextensive. The author of this text offers a superior formulation: qiyas is about deriving a judgment by observing the concept of the middle term, which is automatically more general. This lesson unpacks that formulation and shows how it applies to various syllogistic forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the initial definition, the objection, and the improved formulation.
Pay attention to the logical examples.
2
Identify the problem
Why does the initial definition fail? What is the objection regarding the meaning of 'rational'?
3
Understand the better formulation
The key is that the concept of the middle term is always more general, even if its extension is equal to the minor term. This solves the problem.
4
Examine the examples
The passage gives several examples to illustrate the point. Work through them to see how the middle term's concept is more general.
5
Apply to conditional syllogisms
The same principle extends to conditional conjunctive syllogisms. For exceptional syllogisms, reduction to the first figure is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن استدل بالكلي على الجزئي أو بأحد المتساويين على الآخر فهو القياس، ليتناول ما إذا كان الأوسط مساوياً للأصغر كقولنا : كل إنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق حيوان . والجواب بأن الناطق معناه شيء ماله النطق، وهو بحسب هذا المفهوم أعم من الإنسان لا يجدي نفعاً، إذ لا يتأتى في مثل قولنا : كل ناطق إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان والأحسن أن يقال مرجع القياس إلى استفادة الحكم على ذات ملاحظة مفهوم الأوسط، وهذا أعم قطعاً، وإن كان مفهوم الأصغر مساوياً له كما في المثالين المذكورين، بل وإن كان أعم منه كما في قولنا : بعض الحيوان إنسان وكل إنسان ناطق - وقولنا بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا شيء من الفرس بإنسان وقولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق، وعلى هذا الحال الاقترانيات الشرطية، حيث يستدل بعموم الأوضاع والتقادير على بعضها، وأما في القياس الاستثنائي فلا يتضح ذلك إلا أن يرجع إلى الشكل الأول، فيقال: مضمون التالي أمر تحقق ملزومه، وكل ما تحقق ملزومه فهو متحقق . أو مضمون المقدم أمر انتفى لازمه وكل ما انتفى لازمه فهو منتف
If one reasons from the universal to the particular or from one of two equals to the other, it is qiyas, so that it includes the case where the middle term is equal to the minor term, as in our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is animal.' The answer that 'rational' means a thing that has speech, and according to this concept it is more general than 'human', is of no avail, because it does not apply to examples like our saying: 'Every rational is human, and every human is animal.' The better way is to say: the reference of qiyas is to derive a judgment on an entity by observing the concept of the middle term, and this is definitely more general, even if the concept of the minor term is equal to it as in the two mentioned examples, or even if it is more general than it as in our saying: 'Some animal is human and every human is rational' — and our saying: 'Some animal is human, and nothing of the horse is human' and our saying: 'Every human is animal, and every human is rational.' And the same applies to conditional conjunctive syllogisms, where one reasons from the generality of situations and circumstances to some of them. As for the exceptional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), this is not clear unless it is reduced to the first figure, so it is said: the content of the consequent is a thing whose antecedent is realized, and everything whose antecedent is realized is realized; or the content of the antecedent is a thing whose consequent is negated, and everything whose consequent is negated is negated.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage addresses a problem in the definition of qiyas. Sahib al-Tawali' had defined qiyas as reasoning from the universal to the particular or from one equal to another, so that it covers cases where the middle term equals the minor term (e.g., 'every human is rational, every rational is animal'). An objection is raised: someone might answer that 'rational' (natiq) means 'a thing that has speech' and so is more general than 'human', but this answer fails because there are examples like 'every rational is human' where 'rational' and 'human' are coextensive. The author then gives the better formulation (al-ahsan): qiyas is about deriving a judgment by observing the concept of the middle term, which is always more general (a'amm) by virtue of being a concept, even if its extension is equal to or broader than the minor term. This is illustrated with several examples. The same principle applies to conditional conjunctive syllogisms (iqtirani shartiyya). For exceptional syllogisms (istithna'i), the middle concept approach is not obvious unless reduced to the first figure, using the rule that if the antecedent (lazim) is realized, the consequent is realized, or if the consequent is negated, the antecedent is negated.

Fuqaha's and Logicians' Qiyas: Definition and Classification
Sanusi distinguishes the fuqaha's definition of qiyas from the logicians' and classifies qiyas into iqtirani and istithna'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between the fuqaha's and logicians' definitions of qiyas, and the classification of qiyas into iqtirani and istithna'i with its subdivisions.

Core Idea
Qiyas means different things to jurists and logicians, and logicians split it into two main forms.

This passage clarifies that the term qiyas is used differently by fuqaha (jurists) and logicians. For fuqaha, qiyas is analogy (tamthil), while for logicians, it is a syllogism that makes the conclusion known. The logicians further classify qiyas into iqtirani and istithna'i, with the latter subdivided based on the type of conditional premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with the fuqaha's usage and then moves to the logicians'.
2
Identify the two definitions of qiyas
The passage presents two definitions: one from the fuqaha (analogy) and one from the logicians (syllogism). Note the key difference: fuqaha emphasize equality of cause; logicians emphasize making the conclusion known.
The logicians' definition is more technical and involves premises and conclusion.
3
Understand the classification into iqtirani and istithna'i
The logicians' qiyas is divided based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated. If it is, it is istithna'i; if not, it is iqtirani. Look for the condition described.
The passage refers to grammarians' view on conditional particles and completeness.
4
Note the subdivision of istithna'i
The istithna'i qiyas is further divided into muttasil (conjunctive) and munfasil (disjunctive), depending on the type of conditional premise. Identify the two types.
This subdivision applies only to the istithna'i form.
5
Reflect on why this classification matters
Ask yourself how this classification helps in logical reasoning. The commentary will explain that it clarifies the structure of arguments and prevents confusion between analogy and syllogism.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفقهاء يجعلون القياس اسماً للتمثيل لما فيه من تسوية الجزأين في الحكم، لتساويهما في العلة، وأما على اصطلاح المنطق فوجهه أن فيه جعل النتيجة المجهولة مساوية للمقدمتين في المعلومية، ثم القياس إن اشتمل على النتيجة أو نقيضها بالفعل بأن يكون ذلك مذكوراً فيه بمادته وصورته، وإن لم تبق قضية بواسطة أداة الشرط على ما صرح به بعض أئمة العربية من أن الكلام قد يخرج عن التمام وعن احتمال الصدق والكذب بسبب زيادة فيه مثل طرفي الشرطية، كما يخرج ذلك النقصان فيه مثل قولنا زيد عالم بحذف الرابطة . وإلا سمي استثنائياً لما فيه من استثناء وضع أحد جزأي الشرطية أو رفعه، وإلا سمي اقترانياً لما فيه من اقتران الحدود بعضها بالبعض أعني الأصغر والأكبر والأوسط، والاستثنائي متصل إن كانت الشرطية المذكورة فيه متصلة، ومنفصل إن كانت منفصلة
The fuqaha (jurists) use qiyas as a name for tamthil (analogy), because it involves equating two parts in judgment due to their equality in the cause. As for the terminology of logic, the point is that it makes the unknown conclusion equal to the two premises in terms of being known. Then qiyas: if it actually contains the conclusion or its contradictory, such that it is mentioned in it in its matter and form, and no proposition remains by means of a conditional particle, as some leading grammarians have stated: speech may depart from completeness and from the possibility of truth and falsehood due to an addition like the two parts of a conditional, just as it departs due to a deficiency like our saying 'Zaid is knowledgeable' with the copula omitted — then it is called istithna'i (exceptional) because it involves excepting (istiqsa') the position of one of the two parts of the conditional or its removal. Otherwise it is called iqtirani (conjunctive) because it involves conjoining (iqtiran) the terms with each other, i.e., the minor, major, and middle. The exceptional (istithna'i) is conjunctive (muttasil) if the conditional mentioned in it is conjunctive (muttasila), and disjunctive (munfasil) if it is disjunctive (munfasila).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage discusses two different uses of the term 'qiyas'. The fuqaha (jurists) use qiyas to mean tamthil (analogy), because in both cases a judgment is extended from one case to another due to shared cause. In logic, however, qiyas means making the unknown conclusion known by equating it with the premises. The author then gives the standard logical classification: qiyas is either istithna'i (exceptional) or iqtirani (conjunctive). The exceptional syllogism is one that explicitly contains the conclusion or its contradictory as a premise, and no proposition remains after the conditional is analyzed. This is explained by referring to grammarians who note that adding conditional parts can remove the sentence from completeness and truth-aptness. The conjunctive syllogism conjoins the three terms (minor, major, middle). Finally, the exceptional syllogism is further subdivided into muttasil (conjunctive conditional) and munfasil (disjunctive conditional) depending on the type of conditional premise.

اقتراني
استثنائي
شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة
فقهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qiyas in Islamic logic always means analogy.
Correction: For logicians, qiyas means syllogism, not necessarily analogy. Fuqaha use qiyas for analogy, but logicians use it for a formal argument with premises and conclusion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The term qiyas has distinct meanings in jurisprudence and logic, and logical qiyas is divided into iqtirani and istithna'i based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated.
