Definition of Qiyas and the Generality of the Middle Term
The author defines qiyas and refutes an objection regarding the middle term's generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defines qiyas and why the concept of the middle term must be more general than the minor term.

Core Idea
The middle term's concept must always be more general than the minor term.

This lesson presents the author's definition of qiyas and his refutation of an objection about the middle term's coextension. You will see why a proper definition must cover cases where the middle term is equal or more general, and how the author resolves the objection by focusing on the concept (mafhum) of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the technical terms like قياس, الأوسط, الأصغر.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Find where the author presents an objection to his definition and see why he dismisses it. Then locate his better formulation.
The objection claims that the example 'Every human is rational' fails because 'rational' is more general. The author shows the answer is insufficient.
3
Understand the generalization to conditional syllogisms
Notice the final sentence extends the same reasoning to conditional conjunctive syllogisms. Ask yourself how the principle of generality applies there.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو بأحد المتساويين على الآخر فهو القياس، ليتناول ما إذا كان الأوسط مساو للأصغر كقولنا : كل إنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق حيوان . والجواب بأن الناطق معناه شيء ماله النطق، وهو بحسب هذا المفهوم أعم من الإنسان لا يجدي نفعاً، إذ لا يتأتى في مثل قولنا : كل ناطق إنسان وكل إنسان حيوان والأحسن أن يقال مرجع القياس إلى استفادة الحكم على ذات ملاحظة مفهوم الأوسط، وهذا أعم قطعاً، وإن كان مفهوم الأصغر مساوياً له كما في المثالين المذكورين، بل وإن كان أعم منه كما في قولنا : بعض الحيوان إنسان وكل إنسان ناطق - وقولنا بعض الحيوان إنسان، ولا شيء من الفرس بإنسان وقولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق، وعلى هذا الحال الاقترانيات الشرطية، حيث يستدل بعموم الأوضاع والتقادير على بعضها
Or if it is with one of the two equal terms over the other, then it is the syllogism, so that it includes the case where the middle term is equal to the minor term, like our saying: 'Every human is rational, and every rational is an animal.' And the answer that 'rational' means something that has speech, and according to this concept it is more general than human, is of no use, since it does not apply in such a case as our saying: 'Every rational is human and every human is animal.' And it is better to say that the reference of the syllogism is to derive the judgment on the essence by considering the concept of the middle term, and this is absolutely more general, even if the concept of the minor term is equal to it as in the two mentioned examples; rather, even if it is more general than it, as in our saying: 'Some animal is human and every human is rational' – and our saying: 'Some animal is human, and nothing from horse is human' and our saying: 'Every human is animal, and every human is rational.' And likewise for conditional conjunctive syllogisms, where one infers by the generality of states and estimations over some of them.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the syllogism (qiyas). It begins by stating that if the syllogism involves one of two equal terms over the other, it is still a qiyas, so it covers cases where the middle term equals the minor term in extension, e.g., 'Every human is rational, every rational is animal.' Then it presents an objection: someone might say that in this example, 'rational' (nāṭiq) means 'something that has speech,' which is more general than 'human' – but this answer fails because it does not apply to the reverse example 'Every rational is human, every human is animal.' The author gives a better formulation: the essence of qiyas is to derive a judgment about the subject by observing the concept of the middle term, and this concept is always more general (aʿamm) than the minor term, even if the minor term's concept is coextensive as in the two examples, or even if the minor term is more general (as in 'some animal is human, every human is rational'). Then he extends this to conditional conjunctive syllogisms. Thus the lesson clarifies that the middle term's concept must be more general, not necessarily coextensive, and that a proper definition of qiyas covers all cases.

The Exceptive Syllogism and Its Reduction to the First Figure
The author explains the exceptive syllogism and its reduction to the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the exceptive syllogism and how it reduces to the first figure through modus ponens and modus tollens.

Core Idea
The exceptive syllogism is valid only when reduced to the first figure.

This lesson introduces the exceptive syllogism, a key type of qiyas. The author explains that its validity becomes clear when it is reduced to the first figure, and he provides two standard forms: affirming the antecedent (modus ponens) and denying the consequent (modus tollens). Understanding this reduction is essential for grasping logical deduction in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to understand the two examples of the exceptive syllogism.
Notice that the passage uses conditional language.
2
Identify the two forms
The first example (content of consequent realized) corresponds to modus ponens; the second (content of antecedent negated) corresponds to modus tollens.
These are the two valid moods of the exceptive syllogism.
3
Reflect on the reduction
Consider why the author says it is 'not clear' unless reduced to the first figure; this shows the foundational role of the first figure in logic.
The first figure is the most evident and self-justifying.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في القياس الاستثنائي فلا يتضح ذلك إلا أن يرجع إلى الشكل الأول، فيقال: مضمون التالي أمر تحقق ملزومه، وكل ما تحقق ملزومه فهو متحقق . أو مضمون المقدم أمر انتفى لازمه وكل ما انتفى لازمه فهو منتف، والفقهاء يجعلون القياس اسماً للتمثيل لما فيه من تسوية الجزأين في الحكم، لتساويهما في العلة، وأما على اصطلاح المنطق فوجهه أن فيه جعل النتيجة المجهولة مساوية للمقدمتين في المعلومية، ثم القياس إن اشتمل على النتيجة أو نقيضها بالفعل بأن يكون ذلك مذكوراً فيه بمادته وصورته ، وإن لم تبق قضية بواسطة أداة الشرط على ما صرح به بعض أئمة العربية من أن الكلام قد يخرج عن التمام وعن احتمال الصدق والكذب بسبب زيادة فيه مثل طرفي الشرطية، كما يخرج ذلك النقصان فيه مثل قولنا زيد عالم بحذف الرابطة . والإعراب سمي استثنائياً لما فيه من استثناء وضع أحد جزأي الشرطية أو رفعه، وإلا سمي اقترانياً لما فيه من اقتران الحدود بعضها بالبعض أعني الأصغر والأكبر والأوسط، والاستثنائي متصل إن كانت الشرطية المذكورة فيه متصلة، ومنفصل إن كانت منفصلة، والاقتراني ملي إن كان تألفه من الجمليات الصرفة، وشرطي إن اشتمل على شرطية
As for the exceptive syllogism, that is not clear unless it is reduced to the first figure. So it is said: The content of the consequent is a thing whose necessary condition is realized, and everything whose necessary condition is realized is itself realized. Or the content of the antecedent is a thing whose necessary consequent is negated, and everything whose necessary consequent is negated is itself negated. The jurists make qiyas a name for tamthil (analogy) because it involves equating two particulars in judgment due to their equality in the cause. As for the logical terminology, its aspect is that it makes the unknown conclusion equal to the two premises in knowability. Then qiyas, if it includes the conclusion or its opposite actually, such that it is mentioned in it in its matter and form, and it does not remain a proposition by means of the conditional particle, as some leading Arab grammarians have stated that speech may exit from completeness and from the possibility of truth and falsehood due to an addition in it like the two parts of a conditional, just as such exits due to a deficiency in it like our saying 'Zayd is learned' by omitting the copula. And it is called exceptive because of the exception of the position of one of the two parts of the conditional or its removal; otherwise it is called conjunctive because of the conjunction of the terms with each other, i.e., the minor, major, and middle. The exceptive is connected if the conditional mentioned in it is connected, and disjunctive if it is disjunctive. The conjunctive is absolute if its composition is from pure universal propositions, and conditional if it includes a conditional.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage classifies qiyas (syllogism) into two main types: exceptive (istithnā'ī) and conjunctive (iqtirānī). It begins with the exceptive syllogism, explaining that it becomes clear only when reduced to the first figure, illustrated by modus ponens (if the consequent is realized, its necessary condition is realized) and modus tollens (if the antecedent is negated, its necessary consequent is negated). Then it distinguishes between the juristic and logical definitions of qiyas: jurists use qiyas for analogy (tamthīl) based on shared cause, while logicians focus on making the unknown conclusion equal to the premises in knowability. The division is introduced: if the syllogism explicitly includes the conclusion or its opposite in matter and form, it is exceptive; otherwise it is conjunctive. It explains the terminology: 'exceptive' because it separates one part of the conditional, 'conjunctive' because it conjoins the minor, major, and middle terms. Further subdivisions: exceptive is connected (muttaṣil) if the conditional is connected, and disjunctive (munfaṣil) if it is disjunctive; conjunctive is absolute (maḥḍ) if composed solely of universal propositions, and conditional (sharṭī) if it includes a conditional. The passage provides the criteria for each classification.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
شكل أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The exceptive syllogism is an independent figure not reducible to the first figure.
Correction: The author explicitly states that its clarity depends on reduction to the first figure.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must an exceptive syllogism be reduced to in order to be clear?
A) The second figure
B) The first figure
C) The third figure
D) The fourth figure

Key Takeaway
The exceptive syllogism is valid only when it can be reduced to the first figure, manifesting as modus ponens or modus tollens.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about causes and effects in your daily life, do you ever mistakenly assume that denying the antecedent disproves the consequent? How can you train yourself to use the correct logical form (modus tollens) to avoid errors in judgment?
?
In your theological beliefs, have you ever accepted a conclusion solely because it followed from a conditional statement without verifying that the conditional itself is true? What steps can you take to ensure your reasoning is sound?
?
This lesson shows that even valid logical forms rely on reduction to a basic figure. In your own learning, which foundational principles do you need to revisit to ensure your understanding of more complex ideas is built on solid ground?

Juristic and Logical Definitions of Qiyas
The author distinguishes juristic and logical definitions of qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between the juristic concept of qiyas as analogy (tamthil) and the logical concept of qiyas as syllogism that makes the unknown conclusion known.

Core Idea
Jurists see qiyas as analogy; logicians see it as syllogism producing knowledge.

In this passage, the author contrasts two understandings of qiyas. For jurists, qiyas is tamthil, equating two particulars based on a shared cause. For logicians, qiyas is a syllogism that renders the unknown conclusion equally known as the premises. This distinction is crucial for appreciating how Islamic scholars applied reasoning in different disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the two definitions of qiyas: juristic and logical.
Notice the terms 'tamthil' and 'illa'.
2
Understand the juristic view
Jurists define qiyas as tamthil, equating two particulars in judgment due to their equality in the 'illa (cause).
This is the basis of analogical reasoning in fiqh.
3
Understand the logical view
Logicians define qiyas as a syllogism that makes the unknown conclusion as known as the premises.
This is the deductive syllogism of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في القياس الاستثنائي فلا يتضح ذلك إلا أن يرجع إلى الشكل الأول، فيقال: مضمون التالي أمر تحقق ملزومه، وكل ما تحقق ملزومه فهو متحقق . أو مضمون المقدم أمر انتفى لازمه وكل ما انتفى لازمه فهو منتف، والفقهاء يجعلون القياس اسماً للتمثيل لما فيه من تسوية الجزأين في الحكم، لتساويهما في العلة، وأما على اصطلاح المنطق فوجهه أن فيه جعل النتيجة المجهولة مساوية للمقدمتين في المعلومية، ثم القياس إن اشتمل على النتيجة أو نقيضها بالفعل بأن يكون ذلك مذكوراً فيه بمادته وصورته ، وإن لم تبق قضية بواسطة أداة الشرط على ما صرح به بعض أئمة العربية من أن الكلام قد يخرج عن التمام وعن احتمال الصدق والكذب بسبب زيادة فيه مثل طرفي الشرطية، كما يخرج ذلك النقصان فيه مثل قولنا زيد عالم بحذف الرابطة . والإعراب سمي استثنائياً لما فيه من استثناء وضع أحد جزأي الشرطية أو رفعه، وإلا سمي اقترانياً لما فيه من اقتران الحدود بعضها بالبعض أعني الأصغر والأكبر والأوسط، والاستثنائي متصل إن كانت الشرطية المذكورة فيه متصلة، ومنفصل إن كانت منفصلة، والاقتراني ملي إن كان تألفه من الجمليات الصرفة، وشرطي إن اشتمل على شرطية
As for the exceptive syllogism, that is not clear unless it is reduced to the first figure. So it is said: The content of the consequent is a thing whose necessary condition is realized, and everything whose necessary condition is realized is itself realized. Or the content of the antecedent is a thing whose necessary consequent is negated, and everything whose necessary consequent is negated is itself negated. The jurists make qiyas a name for tamthil (analogy) because it involves equating two particulars in judgment due to their equality in the cause. As for the logical terminology, its aspect is that it makes the unknown conclusion equal to the two premises in knowability. Then qiyas, if it includes the conclusion or its opposite actually, such that it is mentioned in it in its matter and form, and it does not remain a proposition by means of the conditional particle, as some leading Arab grammarians have stated that speech may exit from completeness and from the possibility of truth and falsehood due to an addition in it like the two parts of a conditional, just as such exits due to a deficiency in it like our saying 'Zayd is learned' by omitting the copula. And it is called exceptive because of the exception of the position of one of the two parts of the conditional or its removal; otherwise it is called conjunctive because of the conjunction of the terms with each other, i.e., the minor, major, and middle. The exceptive is connected if the conditional mentioned in it is connected, and disjunctive if it is disjunctive. The conjunctive is absolute if its composition is from pure universal propositions, and conditional if it includes a conditional.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage classifies qiyas (syllogism) into two main types: exceptive (istithnā'ī) and conjunctive (iqtirānī). It begins with the exceptive syllogism, explaining that it becomes clear only when reduced to the first figure, illustrated by modus ponens (if the consequent is realized, its necessary condition is realized) and modus tollens (if the antecedent is negated, its necessary consequent is negated). Then it distinguishes between the juristic and logical definitions of qiyas: jurists use qiyas for analogy (tamthīl) based on shared cause, while logicians focus on making the unknown conclusion equal to the premises in knowability. The division is introduced: if the syllogism explicitly includes the conclusion or its opposite in matter and form, it is exceptive; otherwise it is conjunctive. It explains the terminology: 'exceptive' because it separates one part of the conditional, 'conjunctive' because it conjoins the minor, major, and middle terms. Further subdivisions: exceptive is connected (muttaṣil) if the conditional is connected, and disjunctive (munfaṣil) if it is disjunctive; conjunctive is absolute (maḥḍ) if composed solely of universal propositions, and conditional (sharṭī) if it includes a conditional. The passage provides the criteria for each classification.

Key Terms
قياس
تمثيل
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Qiyas in logic and jurisprudence are synonymous.
Correction: They differ: juristic qiyas is analogy based on shared cause; logical qiyas is syllogism producing knowledge.

Checkpoint
In the juristic sense, qiyas is equated with which term?
A) Tamthil
B) Ijtihad
C) Istihsan
D) Ijma

Key Takeaway
Jurists use qiyas as analogy based on cause; logicians use it as syllogism that makes the unknown known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make moral judgments by comparing situations, do you intentionally seek a common cause ('illa) as the jurists do, or do you rely on superficial resemblance? How much of your religious reasoning is analogy versus deduction, and are you aware of the difference?
?
The logician's qiyas makes an unknown conclusion as certain as the known premises. In your own faith, which truths do you hold with syllogistic certainty, and which are merely probable analogies? Are you comfortable with that distinction, or have you been conflating them to your own spiritual detriment?
?
If you were to explain the concept of qiyas to a new Muslim, which definition would you emphasize, and why? Does your choice reveal a preference for juristic flexibility or logical rigor, and how does that preference shape your confidence in Islamic teachings?

Classification of Qiyas: Exceptive vs Conjunctive
The author classifies qiyas and explains its subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how qiyas is divided into exceptive and conjunctive based on whether the conclusion is explicitly included, and the further subdivisions of each.

Core Idea
Qiyas is divided into exceptive and conjunctive; the former includes the conclusion, the latter does not.

The author now introduces the main division of qiyas. If the conclusion or its opposite is explicitly stated within the syllogism, it is called exceptive; otherwise, it is conjunctive. He then explains the etymological reasons for these names and lists the subdivisions: the exceptive can be connected or disjunctive, and the conjunctive can be absolute or conditional. This classification provides a map of logical deduction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Understand the criterion for exceptive vs conjunctive based on inclusion of the conclusion.
Look for the phrase 'إن اشتمل على النتيجة أو نقيضها'.
2
Note the etymology
Exceptive is so named because it 'excepts' one part of the conditional; conjunctive because it conjoins the terms.
This explains the origins of the terms.
3
Subdivisions of exceptive
The exceptive is connected if its conditional is connected; disjunctive if disjunctive.
4
Subdivisions of conjunctive
The conjunctive is absolute if composed of pure universal propositions; conditional if it includes a conditional.

Induction: Complete and Incomplete
Definition and classification of induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of induction, its two types (complete and incomplete), and the epistemic status of each.

Core Idea
Induction is certain only when it surveys all particulars.

This lesson explores the first of two non-syllogistic reasoning methods: induction. The author defines it precisely and explains the crucial condition that separates certain induction from mere conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of induction
Read the Arabic text and translation focusing on the definition of istiqra' as surveying particulars to establish a universal ruling.
Note the phrase 'على سبيل العموم' (on the basis of generality).
2
Identify the two types
The author distinguishes complete (tamm) and incomplete (naqis) induction. Find the condition for completeness: knowing the enumeration of particulars and the establishment of the ruling in each.
Complete induction is actually a type of conjunctive conditional syllogism called divisive syllogism (qiyas muqassam).
3
Understand the epistemic outcome
Complete induction yields certainty (yaqin), while incomplete induction yields only conjecture (zann). Reflect on why partial survey cannot guarantee universality.
The absolute term 'istiqra'' usually refers to the incomplete type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاستقراء، وهو تصفح جزئيات كل واحد ليثبت حكمها في ذلك الكلي على سبيل العموم افتام، إن علم انحصار الجزئيات وثبوت الحكم في كل منها، وهذا نوع من القياس الاقتراني الشرطي يسمى القياس المقسم، وإلا فناقص وهو المفهوم من إطلاق الاسم، وهو لا يفيد إلا الظن، وأما التمثيل وهو بيان مساواة جزئي لآخر في علة حكمه، لتثبت مساواتهما في الحكم، فقطعي، إن علم استقلال المشترك بالعلية وهذا نوع من القياس، وذكر المثال حشو وإلا فظني، ومطلق الاسم منصرف إليه، وتفاصيل هذه المباحث في صناعة المنطق
As for induction (istiqra'), it is surveying the particulars of each one to establish their ruling in that universal on the basis of generality. It is complete if the enumeration of particulars and the establishment of the ruling in each of them is known, and this is a type of conjunctive conditional syllogism called divisive syllogism (qiyas muqassam). Otherwise it is incomplete, which is what is understood from the absolute name, and it yields only conjecture (ẓann). As for analogy (tamthil), it is demonstrating that one particular equals another in the cause of its ruling, so that their equality in the ruling is established. It is certain if the independence of the common factor in causality is known, and this is a type of qiyas, and mentioning the example is superfluous. Otherwise it is conjectural, and the absolute name refers to it. Details of these discussions are in the art of logic.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage defines induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil), two forms of reasoning distinct from pure syllogism. Induction is surveying particulars to derive a universal ruling. If all particulars are examined and the ruling is confirmed in each, it is complete (tāmm) and yields certainty. This complete form is actually a type of conjunctive conditional syllogism called divisive syllogism (qiyas muqassam). If the enumeration is incomplete, the induction is incomplete (nāqiṣ) and yields only conjecture (ẓann). Analogy (tamthil) is showing that one particular resembles another in the cause ('illa) of its ruling, so they share the same ruling. It yields certainty (qaṭ'ī) only if the common factor is known to be the sole cause (istiqlāl al-mushtarak bi al-'illiyya). Otherwise, it is conjectural, and the absolute term 'tamthil' usually refers to the conjectural type. The author notes that detailed discussions of these topics belong to the science of logic.

Key Terms
استقراء
ظن
قطعي
قياس مقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction always yields certain knowledge.
Correction: Only complete induction yields certainty; incomplete induction yields only conjecture.

Checkpoint
When does complete induction occur?
A) When the conclusion is obvious.
B) When enumeration of particulars is known and the ruling is established in each.
C) When the induction uses a single example.
D) When the subject matter is about the divine.

Key Takeaway
Complete induction yields certainty, but the common incomplete induction yields only conjecture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Complete induction is rare—it requires surveying every particular. Is there any area of your faith or practice where you have mistaken conjecture for certainty? What would it cost you to examine that area more thoroughly, and are you willing to pay that cost?

Analogy: When Is It Certain?
Definition and epistemic condition of analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of analogy (tamthil) and the condition under which it yields certainty rather than conjecture.

Core Idea
Analogy is certain only when the shared cause is known to be the sole cause.

This lesson examines the second reasoning method: analogy (tamthil). The author defines it as establishing equality between two particulars in the cause of a ruling, then clarifies when such reasoning is certain and when it is merely conjectural.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of analogy
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on tamthil as demonstrating equality of one particular to another in the cause of a ruling.
Analogy aims to extend a ruling from one known case to a new case.
2
Identify the condition for certainty
The author states that analogy yields certainty (qaṭ'ī) if the independence of the common factor in causality (istiqlāl al-mushtarak bi al-'illiyya) is known. This means the shared attribute is the sole operative cause.
If the cause is not the sole cause, the analogy is conjectural.
3
Understand the default usage
The absolute term 'tamthil' usually refers to the conjectural type, since proving exclusive causality is difficult. The author notes that mentioning an example is superfluous in the definition.

Source Text

Al-Tawali' and al-Juwayni on Syllogistic Moods
The author praises al-Tawali' and critiques Imam al-Haramayn's omission of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two major scholars—the author of al-Tawali' and Imam al-Haramayn—treated productive syllogistic moods, and why the latter omitted the fourth figure.

Core Idea
Even concise treatments of logic can be complete; omitting the unnatural fourth figure is not a flaw.

In this passage, the author compares two foundational logical works: al-Tawali' and Imam al-Haramayn's treatment of syllogisms. He praises al-Tawali' for its detailed yet concise presentation of all four figures, while noting that Imam al-Haramayn omitted the fourth figure as unnatural and rephrased the third figure to include negative premises. This comparison highlights different pedagogical approaches in classical Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the author first praises al-Tawali' and then discusses Imam al-Haramayn's approach.
Pay attention to the terms 'شكل رابع' and 'سلب الكبرى'.
2
Identify the two scholars' treatments
Identify what each scholar contributed: al-Tawali' gave full details of productive moods, while Imam al-Haramayn was more concise and omitted the fourth figure.
3
Understand the third figure reformulation
Notice how Imam al-Haramayn expressed the third figure in terms of two descriptions in a subject, covering cases of negative major premise. Work through the example given.
The example shows how 'human' has both animality and non-neighing, leading to the conclusion.
4
Reflect on the omission
Consider why the fourth figure might be considered unnatural and why its omission does not make the logic incomplete. This relates to the common misunderstanding.
The author does not criticize the omission; he simply reports it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد صاحب الطوالع تفاصيل الضروب المنتجة من القياس الاستثنائي المتصل والمنفصل، ومن الأشكال الأربعة للقياس الاقتراني الحملي بعبارة في غاية الحسن ونهاية الإيجاز، وأوردها الإمام على وجه أجمل، إلا أنه أهمل الشكل الرابع لبعده عن الطبع، وعبر عن الشكل الثالث بحصول وصفين في محل أي ثبوت أمرين إيجاباً كان أو سلباً لأمر ثالث، فيشمل صور سلب الكبرى كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بصهال. إذ قد جعل في الإنسان ثبوت الحيوانية ونفي الصهالية، فعلم أن بعض الحيوان ليس بصهال
And the author of al-Tawali' presented details of the productive moods from the connected and disjunctive exceptive syllogism, and from the four figures of the conjunctive categorical syllogism, in a most excellent and highly concise expression. And the Imam (al-Haramayn) presented them in a more concise manner, except that he omitted the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness, and he expressed the third figure as the occurrence of two descriptions in a place, i.e., the affirmation of two things, whether positive or negative, about a third thing, so it includes cases of negative major premise like our saying: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing from a human is a neighing thing.' Since in 'human' the affirmation of animality and the negation of neighing-ness are posited, so it is known that some animal is not a neighing thing.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage comments on two previous scholars' treatments of syllogistic forms. The author of al-Tawali' (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) gave detailed accounts of productive moods for both exceptive (connected and disjunctive) and conjunctive categorical syllogisms in an excellent and concise manner. Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni also covered them but even more briefly, omitting the fourth figure because it is unnatural (ba'īd 'an al-ṭab'). He expressed the third figure as a third term having two descriptions (affirmation or negation of two attributes about a third thing). This covers cases where the major premise is negative, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'Nothing human is a neighing thing' – from this we infer that some animal is not a neighing thing. The implication is that Imam's treatment is still valid and sufficient, even with the omission.

شكل رابع
شكل ثالث
سلب الكبرى
صاحب الطوالع
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Omitting the fourth figure from syllogistic logic makes the logical system incomplete or flawed.
Correction: The author implies that the omission is acceptable because the fourth figure is unnatural; the system still works with the three natural figures.

Checkpoint
Why did Imam al-Haramayn omit the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism?
A) Because it is invalid.
B) Because it is unnatural.
C) Because al-Tawali' already covered it.
D) Because it leads to contradictions.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn omitted the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness, but his handling of the third figure still covers all cases, including negative premises.

The Two Main Types of Qiyas: Conjunctive and Exceptive
Classification of qiyas into conjunctive and exceptive, and subtypes of conjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of qiyas into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i), and the further subdivision of conjunctive into simple and conditional.

Core Idea
Qiyas is divided into two fundamental types: conjunctive and exceptive.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of qiyas (syllogism) in Islamic logic. The author distinguishes between the conjunctive syllogism, which connects premises directly, and the exceptive syllogism, which works by exception. Understanding this classification is essential before delving into more complex types like induction and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two main types
Notice how the author begins with 'disjunctive' (munfasila) and then discusses 'conjunctive' (iqtirani). These are the two main types of qiyas.
3
Note the subtypes of conjunctive
Conjunctive qiyas is further split into simple (mali) and conditional (sharti). Understand what each means: simple uses categorical premises, conditional includes a conditional premise.
4
Reflect on the classification
Ask yourself why this classification matters for logical reasoning. All syllogisms fall into these categories.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منفصلة، والاقتراني ملي إن كان تألفه من الجمليات الصرفة، وشرطي إن اشتمل على شرطية، وأما الاستقراء، وهو تصفح جزئيات كل واحد ليثبت حكمها في ذلك الكلي على سبيل العموم افتام، إن علم انحصار الجزئيات وثبوت الحكم في كل منها، وهذا نوع من القياس الاقتراني الشرطي يسمى القياس المقسم، وإلا فناقص وهو المفهوم من إطلاق الاسم، وهو لا يفيد إلا الظن، وأما التمثيل وهو بيان مساواة جزئي لآخر في علة حكمه، لتثبت مساواتهما في الحكم، فقطعي، إن علم استقلال المشترك بالعلية وهذا نوع من القياس، وذكر المثال حشو وإلا فظني، ومطلق الاسم منصرف إليه، وتفاصيل هذه المباحث في صناعة المنطق، وأورد صاحب الطوالع تفاصيل الضروب المنتجة من القياس الاستثنائي المتصل والمنفصل، ومن الأشكال الأربعة للقياس الاقتراني الحملي بعبارة في غاية الحسن ونهاية الإيجاز، وأوردها الإمام على وجه أجمل، إلا أنه أهمل الشكل الرابع لبعده عن الطبع، وعبر عن الشكل الثالث بحصول وصفين في محل أي ثبوت أمرين إيجاباً كان أو سلباً لأمر ثالث، فيشمل صور سلب الكبرى كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بصهال. إذ قد جعل في الإنسان ثبوت الحيوانية ونفي الصهالية، فعلم أن بعض الحيوان ليس بصهال، وعبر عن الاستثنائي المنفصل بالتقسيم المنحصر في قسمين، ثم رفع أيهما كان ليلزم ثبوت الآخر أو إثبات أيهما كان ليلزم ارتفاع الآخر، ولما كان ظاهره مختص بالمنفصل الحقيقي غيره صاحب المواقف إلى ما هو أوجز وأشمل، وهو أن بين الأمرين فيلزم من ثبوت أيهما كان عدم الآخر، يعني أنه إذا ثبت المنافاة بينهما في الصدق والكذب جميعاً كما في الحقيقة يلزم من ثبوت صدق كل عدم صدق الآخر، ومن ثبوت كذب كل عدم كذب الآخر وإذا كان في الصدق فقط يلزم من ثبوت صدق كل عدم صدق الآخر، وفي إذا كان في الكذب فقط يلزم من ثبوت كذب كل عدم وكذب الآخر.
Disjunctive and conjunctive (al-iqtirani) — [the latter] is simple if composed of pure categoricals, and conditional if it includes a conditional. As for induction (al-istiqra'), which is the examination of the particulars of each one to establish the ruling of that universal as a generality — [it is] complete if the enumeration of particulars is known and the ruling is established in each of them; this is a type of conditional conjunctive syllogism called the divisive syllogism. Otherwise it is incomplete, which is what the name normally denotes, and it yields only probability. As for analogy (al-tamthil), which is the demonstration of the equality of one particular to another in the cause of its ruling, to establish their equality in the ruling — [it is] certain if the independence of the common factor in causality is known; this is a type of syllogism, and mentioning the example is redundant. Otherwise it is probable, and the absolute name is understood to refer to it. The details of these investigations are in the art of logic. The author of al-Tawali' presented the details of the productive modes of the connected and disjunctive exceptive syllogism, and of the four figures of the categorical conjunctive syllogism, in a most beautiful and concise expression. The Imam presented them in a more elegant way, except that he omitted the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness, and expressed the third figure as the occurrence of two attributes in a locus, i.e., the affirmation of two things, whether positive or negative, for a third thing. Thus it includes cases of a negative major premise, such as our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing human is whinnying.' For in 'human' the affirmation of animality and the negation of whinnying have been placed, so it is known that some animal is not whinnying. He expressed the disjunctive exceptive [syllogism] as the division restricted to two parts, then the elimination of whichever one necessitates the affirmation of the other, or the affirmation of whichever one necessitates the elimination of the other. Since its apparent meaning was specific to the true disjunctive, the author of al-Mawaqif changed it to what is more concise and comprehensive: that there is opposition between the two things, so from the affirmation of whichever one follows the non-existence of the other. That is, if opposition between them is established in both truth and falsehood, as in the true [disjunctive], then from the affirmation of the truth of each follows the non-truth of the other, and from the affirmation of the falsehood of each follows the non-falsehood of the other. And if it is in truth only, then from the affirmation of the truth of each follows the non-truth of the other. And if it is in falsehood only, then from the affirmation of the falsehood of each follows the non-falsehood of the other.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage classifies types of qiyas (syllogistic reasoning) in Islamic logic. It begins by distinguishing between the conjunctive (iqtirani) and the exceptive (istithna'i) syllogisms. The conjunctive is further divided into simple (mali) when composed of pure categoricals, and conditional (sharti) when it includes a conditional premise. Then it discusses induction (istiqra'), defined as examining particulars to establish a universal ruling. Complete induction (tamm) requires enumerating all particulars and finding the ruling in each, which is a type of conditional conjunctive syllogism called the divisive syllogism; incomplete induction yields only probability. Next, analogy (tamthil) is defined as showing one particular's equality to another in the cause of a ruling; it is certain if the common cause is independent, otherwise probable. The author notes details belong to logic. Then he mentions that the author of al-Tawali' gave an excellent concise account of the productive modes of the exceptive and the four figures of the categorical conjunctive, while the Imam (likely al-Ghazali) presented them more elegantly but omitted the fourth figure as unnatural, and described the third figure as two attributes in one subject. An example is given with a negative major premise: 'Every human is an animal; no human is whinnying' yields 'Some animal is not whinnying.' Finally, the exclusive disjunctive (istithna'i munfasil) is explained as a division into two parts where eliminating one affirms the other or vice versa. The author of al-Mawaqif expanded it to cover both true and merely truth-exclusive or falsehood-exclusive oppositions, specifying the logical consequences.

Key Terms

اقتراني
استثنائي
ملي
شرطي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a type of qiyas mentioned in this passage?
A) Iqtirani mali
B) Iqtirani sharti
C) Istithna'i munfasil
D) Istiqra'

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is fundamentally divided into conjunctive and exceptive, and the conjunctive is further split into simple and conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you analyze a complex argument about Islamic beliefs, do you take the time to classify its logical structure—whether it is conjunctive or exceptive? How might this skill help you avoid errors in your own reasoning?
?
Have you ever confused a conditional argument (if...then) with a categorical one? How can knowing the difference between simple and conditional qiyas change the way you present proofs for Allah's existence or attributes?
?
This lesson distinguishes between conjunctive and exceptive qiyas. In your own practice of deriving rulings or defending creed, have you mistakenly treated an exceptive argument as conjunctive, or vice versa? What steps will you take to ensure your reasoning adheres to correct logical forms?

Induction and Analogy as Types of Qiyas
Definition and classification of induction and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil), their definitions, classifications, and relation to qiyas.

Core Idea
Induction and analogy are forms of qiyas that yield certainty only under strict conditions.

After establishing the basic types of qiyas, the author introduces two more: induction and analogy. Induction examines particulars to derive a universal ruling, while analogy transfers a ruling from one particular to another based on a shared cause. Both are considered types of qiyas, but their certainty depends on completeness of enumeration or independence of the common cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage for istiqra' and tamthil. Notice how each is defined and then classified.
2
Identify the conditions for certainty
Complete induction requires exhaustive enumeration; certain analogy requires independent common cause. Incomplete versions yield probability.
3
Relate them to earlier classification
Induction is a type of conditional conjunctive syllogism. Analogy is a type of qiyas. Understand how they fit into the broader framework.
4
Reflect on the role of probability
Note that most induction and analogy are probable, not certain. This is a key concept in Islamic legal reasoning.

Misconception & Correction

Figures and the Disjunctive Exceptive Syllogism
Discussion of figures and refinement of the disjunctive exceptive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the discussion of the third figure, the omission of the fourth, and the refined definition of the disjunctive exceptive syllogism.

Core Idea
The disjunctive exceptive syllogism was refined to cover all types of opposition.

This lesson covers the later part of the passage, where the author discusses presentations of the productive modes of syllogisms by earlier scholars, focusing on the third figure and the disjunctive exceptive. The author of al-Mawaqif improved upon an earlier definition to be more comprehensive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on figures
Read about how the author of al-Tawali' and the Imam presented the figures. Note the omission of the fourth figure.
2
Understand the third figure example
The Imam's description of the third figure as two attributes in one subject is illustrated with the example of humans, animals, and whinnying. Follow the logic.
3
Examine the disjunctive definition
The Imam's definition of the disjunctive exceptive was limited to true disjunctives. Understand what that means.
4
Appreciate the refinement
The author of al-Mawaqif made the definition more comprehensive by considering opposition in truth and falsehood separately. See how this covers more cases.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منفصلة، والاقتراني ملي إن كان تألفه من الجمليات الصرفة، وشرطي إن اشتمل على شرطية، وأما الاستقراء، وهو تصفح جزئيات كل واحد ليثبت حكمها في ذلك الكلي على سبيل العموم افتام، إن علم انحصار الجزئيات وثبوت الحكم في كل منها، وهذا نوع من القياس الاقتراني الشرطي يسمى القياس المقسم، وإلا فناقص وهو المفهوم من إطلاق الاسم، وهو لا يفيد إلا الظن، وأما التمثيل وهو بيان مساواة جزئي لآخر في علة حكمه، لتثبت مساواتهما في الحكم، فقطعي، إن علم استقلال المشترك بالعلية وهذا نوع من القياس، وذكر المثال حشو وإلا فظني، ومطلق الاسم منصرف إليه، وتفاصيل هذه المباحث في صناعة المنطق، وأورد صاحب الطوالع تفاصيل الضروب المنتجة من القياس الاستثنائي المتصل والمنفصل، ومن الأشكال الأربعة للقياس الاقتراني الحملي بعبارة في غاية الحسن ونهاية الإيجاز، وأوردها الإمام على وجه أجمل، إلا أنه أهمل الشكل الرابع لبعده عن الطبع، وعبر عن الشكل الثالث بحصول وصفين في محل أي ثبوت أمرين إيجاباً كان أو سلباً لأمر ثالث، فيشمل صور سلب الكبرى كقولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بصهال. إذ قد جعل في الإنسان ثبوت الحيوانية ونفي الصهالية، فعلم أن بعض الحيوان ليس بصهال، وعبر عن الاستثنائي المنفصل بالتقسيم المنحصر في قسمين، ثم رفع أيهما كان ليلزم ثبوت الآخر أو إثبات أيهما كان ليلزم ارتفاع الآخر، ولما كان ظاهره مختص بالمنفصل الحقيقي غيره صاحب المواقف إلى ما هو أوجز وأشمل، وهو أن بين الأمرين فيلزم من ثبوت أيهما كان عدم الآخر، يعني أنه إذا ثبت المنافاة بينهما في الصدق والكذب جميعاً كما في الحقيقة يلزم من ثبوت صدق كل عدم صدق الآخر، ومن ثبوت كذب كل عدم كذب الآخر وإذا كان في الصدق فقط يلزم من ثبوت صدق كل عدم صدق الآخر، وفي إذا كان في الكذب فقط يلزم من ثبوت كذب كل عدم وكذب الآخر.
Disjunctive and conjunctive (al-iqtirani) — [the latter] is simple if composed of pure categoricals, and conditional if it includes a conditional. As for induction (al-istiqra'), which is the examination of the particulars of each one to establish the ruling of that universal as a generality — [it is] complete if the enumeration of particulars is known and the ruling is established in each of them; this is a type of conditional conjunctive syllogism called the divisive syllogism. Otherwise it is incomplete, which is what the name normally denotes, and it yields only probability. As for analogy (al-tamthil), which is the demonstration of the equality of one particular to another in the cause of its ruling, to establish their equality in the ruling — [it is] certain if the independence of the common factor in causality is known; this is a type of syllogism, and mentioning the example is redundant. Otherwise it is probable, and the absolute name is understood to refer to it. The details of these investigations are in the art of logic. The author of al-Tawali' presented the details of the productive modes of the connected and disjunctive exceptive syllogism, and of the four figures of the categorical conjunctive syllogism, in a most beautiful and concise expression. The Imam presented them in a more elegant way, except that he omitted the fourth figure due to its unnaturalness, and expressed the third figure as the occurrence of two attributes in a locus, i.e., the affirmation of two things, whether positive or negative, for a third thing. Thus it includes cases of a negative major premise, such as our statement: 'Every human is an animal, and nothing human is whinnying.' For in 'human' the affirmation of animality and the negation of whinnying have been placed, so it is known that some animal is not whinnying. He expressed the disjunctive exceptive [syllogism] as the division restricted to two parts, then the elimination of whichever one necessitates the affirmation of the other, or the affirmation of whichever one necessitates the elimination of the other. Since its apparent meaning was specific to the true disjunctive, the author of al-Mawaqif changed it to what is more concise and comprehensive: that there is opposition between the two things, so from the affirmation of whichever one follows the non-existence of the other. That is, if opposition between them is established in both truth and falsehood, as in the true [disjunctive], then from the affirmation of the truth of each follows the non-truth of the other, and from the affirmation of the falsehood of each follows the non-falsehood of the other. And if it is in truth only, then from the affirmation of the truth of each follows the non-truth of the other. And if it is in falsehood only, then from the affirmation of the falsehood of each follows the non-falsehood of the other.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage classifies types of qiyas (syllogistic reasoning) in Islamic logic. It begins by distinguishing between the conjunctive (iqtirani) and the exceptive (istithna'i) syllogisms. The conjunctive is further divided into simple (mali) when composed of pure categoricals, and conditional (sharti) when it includes a conditional premise. Then it discusses induction (istiqra'), defined as examining particulars to establish a universal ruling. Complete induction (tamm) requires enumerating all particulars and finding the ruling in each, which is a type of conditional conjunctive syllogism called the divisive syllogism; incomplete induction yields only probability. Next, analogy (tamthil) is defined as showing one particular's equality to another in the cause of a ruling; it is certain if the common cause is independent, otherwise probable. The author notes details belong to logic. Then he mentions that the author of al-Tawali' gave an excellent concise account of the productive modes of the exceptive and the four figures of the categorical conjunctive, while the Imam (likely al-Ghazali) presented them more elegantly but omitted the fourth figure as unnatural, and described the third figure as two attributes in one subject. An example is given with a negative major premise: 'Every human is an animal; no human is whinnying' yields 'Some animal is not whinnying.' Finally, the exclusive disjunctive (istithna'i munfasil) is explained as a division into two parts where eliminating one affirms the other or vice versa. The author of al-Mawaqif expanded it to cover both true and merely truth-exclusive or falsehood-exclusive oppositions, specifying the logical consequences.

Key Terms

شكل رابع
استثنائي منفصل
منفصل حقيقي
صاحب المواقف
صاحب الطوالع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking the disjunctive exceptive only applies to true disjunctives (exclusive or).
Correction: It also applies to non-exclusive disjunctions where opposition is only in truth or falsehood, as refined by al-Mawaqif.

Checkpoint

A) First
B) Second
C) Third
D) Fourth

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive exceptive syllogism was refined to cover both true and merely truth-exclusive or falsehood-exclusive oppositions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a definition that seems too narrow, like the original definition of the disjunctive syllogism, do you tend to accept it or seek a more comprehensive understanding? What prevents you from refining your own beliefs?
?
The refinement of the disjunctive syllogism required recognizing that opposition can be partial. Are there areas of your faith where you have assumed a binary opposition (true/false) when the reality is more nuanced? How would you go about correcting that?
?
The example shows that the third figure can yield a valid conclusion even with a negative premise. Have you ever dismissed a valid argument because it didn't fit a familiar pattern? How can you train yourself to be more open to different forms of reasoning?

Definition of Dalil and Its Conditions
Author defines dalil and its essential conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of dalil (proof) as that which leads to a ruling through sound consideration, including the conditions of possibility and correctness of reflection.

Core Idea
A proof is valid by its nature, not by being used—sound consideration unlocks it.

The author establishes the foundational definition of dalil in theological reasoning. He explains that a proof is whatever allows one, through correct reflection, to arrive at a ruling—whether certain or probable. This lesson unpacks the key elements: the role of sound consideration, the concept of possibility (the proof remains a proof even if unused), and why the ruling is left absolute to include both certain and probable outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage that defines dalil and its conditions. Note the key phrases: 'يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى حكم' and 'ذكر الإمكان'.
The definition includes both the broad sense and the specific conditions.
2
Identify the three conditions
Look for three conditions: (1) possibility (imkan) – the proof is a proof even if not considered, (2) sound consideration (nazar sahih) – consideration must be from the correct direction, (3) absolute ruling – the ruling can be certain or probable.
3
Understand the term 'amara' as counterpart
The passage mentions that the counterpart of dalil is amara (indicator), which leads to probability. This anticipates the next lesson.
amara is defined but its distinction from dalil is clarified later.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وقد يقال الدليل لما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى حكم كالعلم للصانع، وكثيراً ما يختص بالجارم وتقابله الأمارة الأمارة: وهي ما يؤدي النظر فيها إلى الظن كدلالة وجود الفرس بالباب على زيد في المثال والتوصل المذكر وهو الاستدلال الذي هو طلب المدلول. وقد يخص الاستدلال بما يكون من الأثر كالعالم للصانع وعكسه على هذا يسمى تعليلاً كوجود الشمس للإشراق، لأن المؤثر علة للأثر، أي سبب فيه في اصطلاح المنطق هو المقدمات المرتبة المنتجة للمطلوب. وقد يقال للأمر الذي يمكن أن يتأمل فيه وتستنبط منه المقدمات المرتبة كالعالم للصانع، فيفسر بما يمكن التوصل بصحيح النظر فيه إلى حكم قطعياً كان أو ظنياً، وذكر الإمكان لأن الدليل لا يخرج عن كونه دليلاً بعدم النظر فيه، وقيد النظر بالصحيح لأنه لا توصل بالفاسد إليه، وذلك بأن لا يكون النظر فيه من جهة دلالته، وأطلق الحكم ليتناول التفسير الأمارة، وكثيراً ما يخص الدليل بما يفيد العلم، ويسمى ما يتوصل به إلى الظن أمارة والاستدلال هو التوصل المذكور، وقد يخص بما يكون من الأثر إلى المؤثر، كالتوصل بالنظر في العالم إلى
He said: And it is said [that] the proof (al-dalil) is that by which, through sound consideration, one can reach a ruling, like the world for the Maker. And often it is specified for the categorical [syllogism], and its counterpart is the indicator (al-amara). The indicator: it is that which consideration therein leads to probability, like the indication of the presence of a horse at the door for Zayd in the example, and the mentioned inferential process (al-istidlal) is the seeking of the indicated. And istidlal may be specified for [going] from the effect to the cause, like [considering] the world for the Maker; its opposite in this is called ta'lil (causal inference), like the existence of the sun for illumination, because the cause is the cause of the effect, i.e., a cause of it. In the terminology of logic, [proof] is the ordered premises that produce the desired conclusion. And it may be said for the thing in which one can reflect and from which the ordered premises are derived, like the world for the Maker. Thus it is interpreted as that by which, through sound consideration, one can reach a ruling, whether certain or probable. The mention of 'possibility' is because the proof does not cease to be a proof due to the lack of consideration therein. Restricting consideration to 'sound' is because through unsound [consideration] one cannot reach it, [sound meaning] that the consideration is not from the direction of its indication. He made the ruling absolute to include the interpretation of the indicator. And often proof is specified for what yields knowledge, while that by which probability is reached is called an indicator. And istidlal is the aforementioned reaching; it may be specified for [going] from the effect to the cause, like reaching through consideration of the world to
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage provides the definition of dalil (proof) in logic and kalam. It begins by stating that dalil is that which, through sound consideration (nazar sahih), allows one to reach a ruling (hukm), like the world as a proof for the existence of the Creator. Often dalil is restricted to categorical syllogisms (jasim). Its counterpart is amara (indicator), which is defined in a footnote: amara is that which leads to probability (zann) through consideration, such as the presence of a horse at the door indicating Zayd. The term istidlal (inferential process) is the seeking of the indicated (madlul), and may be specified for reasoning from effect to cause (min al-athar ila al-mu'aththir); the opposite is called ta'lil (causal inference), e.g., the sun causes illumination. In logical terminology, dalil is the ordered premises (muqaddimat murattaba) that produce the desired conclusion (matlub). It can also refer to the thing that one reflects on to derive those premises, like the world. The definition is expanded: dalil is whatever allows reaching a ruling through sound consideration, whether the ruling is certain (qat'i) or probable (zanni). The phrase 'possibility' indicates that dalil remains a proof even if not considered. 'Sound consideration' means consideration from the correct direction of indication. The ruling is left absolute to include the interpretation of amara. Often, dalil is restricted to what yields certain knowledge, while what yields probability is called amara. Istidlal is the act of reaching [the conclusion]; it may be specified for reasoning from effect to cause.

نظر صحيح
إمكان
حكم

Checkpoint

A) To indicate that a proof only becomes a proof when someone uses it
B) To indicate that a proof remains a proof even if it is not actually considered
C) To indicate that a proof may lead to either certainty or probability
D) To indicate that consideration must be sound

Key Takeaway
A proof (dalil) is defined as that which, through sound consideration, can lead to a ruling (certain or probable), and it remains a proof even when not used.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If a proof remains a proof even when untouched, what excuses have you made for not deeply considering the signs of Allah in creation? Will you commit to one act of genuine reflection today?
?
The world itself is a proof for the Creator. Have you ever allowed the sheer familiarity of this proof to dull its impact on your heart? What will you do this week to renew your wonder at this evidence?

Distinction Between Dalil and Amara
Author distinguishes dalil from amara and defines inference types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between dalil (proof) for certain knowledge and amara (indicator) for probable knowledge, and the two types of inference: istidlal and ta'lil.

Core Idea
Certain proof gives knowledge; probable proof is merely an indicator—both are valid but differ in certainty.

Building on the general definition of dalil, the author now refines the terminology. He explains that in common usage, dalil is restricted to proofs that yield certain knowledge (yaqin), while amara is used for probable indicators (zann). He also clarifies two forms of inference: istidlal (reasoning from effect to cause) and its opposite, ta'lil (reasoning from cause to effect). This lesson sharpens your understanding of these essential logical tools.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction passage
Read the Arabic passage that focuses on the specific usage of dalil and amara, and the definitions of istidlal and ta'lil. Pay attention to the phrase 'يكثراً ما يخص الدليل بما يفيد العلم'.
2
Understand the difference in certainty
The passage states that often dalil is specified for what yields knowledge ('ilm), while amara is what yields probability (zann). This is a common technical distinction.
The earlier general definition allowed dalil to cover both certain and probable, but this is a narrower usage.
3
Learn the two inference directions
Istidlal is the general inferential process, but it may be specified for reasoning from effect to cause (min al-athar ila al-mu'aththir). The opposite direction (cause to effect) is called ta'lil. Recall the example: the world as an effect leads to the Creator as cause (istidlal), while the sun's existence causing illumination is ta'lil.
These terms help structure arguments in kalam and logic.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that dalil only refers to certain proofs and amara only to probable ones, and that they are mutually exclusive categories for all arguments.
Correction: The passage clarifies that while dalil is often specified for certainty and amara for probability, the general definition of dalil (given earlier) includes both certain and probable. The distinction is a common usage, not absolute. Additionally, istidlal and ta'lil describe the direction of inference, not the certainty level.

Definition of the Disjunctive Conditional
The author defines the disjunctive conditional and its two divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the disjunctive conditional and how its logical behavior varies based on the type of incompatibility between the two propositions.

Core Idea
A disjunctive conditional expresses an incompatibility relationship between two propositions.

This lesson introduces the disjunctive conditional, a key logical tool. The author first presents its two fundamental divisions and then refines the definition to account for different kinds of incompatibility—whether in truth only, falsehood only, or both. This refinement makes the concept more precise and useful in logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the terms 'istithnā'ī munfaṣil' and 'munāfāh'.
2
Identify the two basic divisions
The passage first states that the disjunctive conditional is divided into two types. Find and understand these two: (1) raising one to necessitate affirmation of the other, (2) affirming one to necessitate elevation of the other.
These correspond to the logical forms 'either not A or B' and 'either A or not B'.
3
Understand the refinement by al-Ījī
The author notes that the basic division only covers 'real' disjunctions. Al-Ījī offers a more concise formulation: affirming one entails the non-existence of the other. This covers more cases.
4
Distinguish the three cases of incompatibility
The passage then explains three scenarios: incompatibility in both truth and falsehood (real), in truth only, and in falsehood only. For each, note what follows from affirming truth or falsehood of one proposition.
This step is crucial for applying the concept correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يثبت المنافاة وعبر عن الاستثنائي المنفصل بالتقسيم المنحصر في قسمين، ثم رفع أيهما كان ليلزم ثبوت الآخر أو إثبات أيهما كان ليلزم ارتفاع الآخر، ولما كان ظاهره مختص بالمنفصل الحقيقي غيره صاحب المواقف إلى ما هو أوجز وأشمل، وهو أن بين الأمرين فيلزم من ثبوت أيهما كان عدم الآخر، يعني أنه إذا ثبت المنافاة بينهما في الصدق والكذب جميعاً كما في الحقيقة يلزم من ثبوت صدق كل عدم صدق الآخر، ومن ثبوت كذب كل عدم كذب الآخر وإذا كان في الصدق فقط يلزم من ثبوت صدق كل عدم صدق الآخر، وفي إذا كان في الكذب فقط يلزم من ثبوت كذب كل عدم وكذب الآخر.
He establishes the incompatibility and expressed the disjunctive exception [al-istithnā'ī al-munfaṣil] by the division restricted to two divisions: either raising whichever of the two is [affirmed] to necessitate the affirmation of the other, or affirming whichever of the two is [affirmed] to necessitate the elevation of the other. And since its apparent [meaning] is specific to the real disjunctive, the author of 'al-Mawāqif' changed it to what is more concise and comprehensive, namely that there is between the two matters [such that] from the affirmation of whichever of them is [affirmed] results the non-existence of the other. That is, if incompatibility is established between them in both truth and falsehood, as in the real [disjunctive], it follows from the affirmation of the truth of each the non-truth of the other, and from the affirmation of the falsehood of each the non-falsehood of the other. And if it is in truth only, then from the affirmation of the truth of each follows the non-truth of the other. And if it is in falsehood only, then from the affirmation of the falsehood of each follows the non-falsehood of the other.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines the disjunctive conditional (al-istithnā'ī al-munfaṣil) as a logical tool. The author begins by noting that this conditional is expressed through a division into two parts: either the affirmation of one proposition entails the negation of the other, or the negation of one entails the affirmation of the other. However, this formulation appears limited to 'real' disjunctions (where the two propositions are truly mutually exclusive). The author of al-Mawāqif (al-Ījī) provided a more concise and comprehensive statement: there is between the two matters an incompatibility such that affirming one entails the non-existence of the other. The author then explains that if the incompatibility is in both truth and falsehood (as in a real disjunction), then affirming the truth of one entails the falsehood of the other, and affirming the falsehood of one entails the truth of the other. If the incompatibility is only in truth, then affirming the truth of one entails the falsehood of the other (but falsehood of one does not entail truth of the other). If the incompatibility is only in falsehood, then affirming the falsehood of one entails the truth of the other (but truth of one does not entail falsehood of the other). The passage thus clarifies the logical behavior of disjunctive propositions in different types of opposition.

Key Terms
الاستثنائي المنفصل
المنافاة
الصدق
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All disjunctive conditionals are exclusive 'or' (real disjunctions) where exactly one proposition is true.
Correction: Disjunctive conditionals can also have incompatibility only in truth (i.e., both cannot be true, but both can be false) or only in falsehood (both cannot be false, but both can be true). The logical entailments differ accordingly.

Checkpoint
In a disjunctive conditional where incompatibility is only in truth, what does affirming the falsehood of one proposition entail?
A) The falsehood of the other
B) The truth of the other
C) Nothing about the truth value of the other
D) The negation of the other

Key Takeaway
The disjunctive conditional involves an incompatibility relationship; the type of incompatibility determines what logical entailments follow from affirming or denying a proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own reasoning about theological issues, do you sometimes assume that because one belief is true, another must automatically be false, without considering the possibility of a wider range of logical relationships? How can this lesson help you become more precise in your thinking?
?
Consider a disagreement you have had about a matter of faith. Did you treat the positions as if they were in a real disjunction, when in fact they might have been in a truth-only or false-only relationship? How would recognizing the type of incompatibility have changed the discussion?
?
The author refined the definition of the disjunctive conditional to be more comprehensive. Are there areas of your own understanding of Islamic beliefs that need refinement to avoid oversimplification? What step can you take today to deepen your understanding?

The Indivisible Atom: A Definition from Kalam
Defines the indivisible atom and its properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam defines the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) and its properties, including its relation to motion.

Core Idea
The indivisible atom is a measure of motion, not a stable particle.

In this passage, Sanusi defines a central concept in kalam atomism: the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). He carefully distinguishes it from being composed of time atoms and explains that it is a continuous but non-stable quantity. This definition sets the stage for understanding how atoms are constantly recreated in occasionalist theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text aloud. Notice its concise, logical structure. Then read the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning.
This passage is only one sentence, but it contains a chain of reasoning.
2
Identify what the atom is not
The passage begins with a negation: the atom is not composed of consecutive instants (ānāt mutatāliya). This denies that the atom has temporal parts.
This distinguishes the atom from a series of time atoms.
3
Identify what the atom is
It then affirms two positive properties: it is a continuous quantity (kamm muttasil) and it is non-stable (ghayr qārr). From this, it concludes that the atom is a measure (miqdār) of a non-stable form (hay'a ghayr qārra), which is motion (ḥaraka).
The conclusion ties atomism directly to motion and change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجوهر الفرد، فلا يكون مركباً من آنات متتالية ، فهو إذن كم متصل إلا أنه غير قار فهو إذن مقدار لهيئة غير قارة، وهي الحركة .
The indivisible atom, so it is not composed of consecutive instants, thus it is a continuous quantity, but it is not stable, thus it is a measure of a non-stable form, which is motion.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard) as conceived in kalam. It states that the atom is not composed of consecutive instants (ānāt mutatāliya), meaning it is not a compound of temporal parts. Instead, it is a continuous quantity (kamm muttasil), but it is not stable (ghayr qārr), indicating that it is in a state of constant change. Therefore, it is a measure (miqdār) of a non-stable form (hay'a ghayr qārra), which is motion (ḥaraka). This aligns with the Ash'ari occasionalist conception where atoms are constantly recreated and have no enduring stability.

Key Terms

آنات متتالية
كم متصل
غير قار

الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the indivisible atom is composed of consecutive time atoms, or that it is a stable, enduring substance.
Correction: The passage explicitly denies composition from time atoms and states the atom is non-stable, making it a measure of motion rather than a static particle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the indivisible atom a measure of?
A) A stable form
B) A non-stable form, which is motion
C) Continuous instants
D) A quantity of time

Key Takeaway
The indivisible atom in kalam is not a static particle but a continuous, non-stable quantity that serves as the measure of motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The atom's definition reveals that your existence at every instant depends on a non-stable form that is motion. How does this challenge your sense of personal identity and permanence? Will you consciously affirm your dependence on God's continuous creation today?

Second Argument: Impossibility of Acquisition by Definition or Description
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the argument that existence cannot be defined due to simplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that existence cannot be defined due to its simplicity and how the refutation uses the concept of composition in wholes.

Core Idea
Simplicity does not prevent definition; composites show that the whole can be more than its parts.

Al-Taftazani now presents the second argument against the acquisition of existence. The argument claims existence is simple and thus cannot be defined. However, he refutes this by pointing to composite entities where the whole is additional to the parts. This lesson unpacks the argument and its solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Taftazani uses a very compressed style here, typical of post-classical kalam.
2
Identify the two prongs of the argument
The argument claims existence cannot be acquired either by definition (had) or by description (rasm). Understand why each is said to be impossible.
The discussion of description refers back to an earlier point.
3
Grasp the refutation
The refutation uses a counterexample: in composites, the resulting whole is additional to each part but not to the whole itself. So composition exists, and existence can be defined through its constituents.
This refutes the claim that simplicity precludes definition.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Ask yourself how this argument relates to the question of whether existence is acquired. The refutation shows that the concept of existence can be understood through analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : : أنه معلوم يمتنع اكتسابه إما بالحد فلبساطته إذ لو تركب فأما من الموجودات فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، ومساواة الجزء للكل في ماهيته، أو من غيرها فلا بد أن يحصل عند الاجتماع أمر زائد يكون هو الموجود، لئلا يكون الموجود محض ماليس بموجود والزائد على الشيء عارض، فلا يكون التركيب فيه، وإما بالرسم فلما سبق ورد بالنقض الإجمالي لسائر المركبات. والحل بأن الأمر الحاصل يكون زائداً على كل لا على الكل، بل هو نفس الكل فيكون التركيب فيه، ويكون الوجود محض ما ليس شيء من أجزائه بوجود كسائر المركبات، وحديث الرسم قد سبق .
The second: that it is a known thing whose acquisition is impossible: either by definition (had) because of its simplicity, for if it were composite, then [the definition] would be from existents, necessitating the precedence of a thing over itself, and the equality of the part to the whole in its quiddity; or from other than them, then there must arise at the composition an additional thing that is the existent, so that the existent is not pure non-existent, and the additional thing is accidental to the thing, so there is no composition in it. Or [acquisition] by description (rasm) due to what has preceded, and it was refuted by a general counterexample of other composites. And the solution is that the resulting thing is additional to each [part] but not to the whole; rather it is the whole itself, so there is composition in it, and existence becomes pure that of which no part is existence, like other composites. And the discussion of description has preceded.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument: the concept of existence cannot be acquired because it is simple. If one tries to define it by genus and species, it would be composite, leading to absurdities. If one tries to describe it, similar problems arise. The refutation counters that in composites, the resulting whole is additional to each part but not to the whole; thus composition is present. Existence is like other composites where no single part is existence itself. So the argument fails.

Key Terms

رسم
بساطة
تركيب
موجود
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That simplicity of a concept precludes any definitional acquisition.
Correction: The refutation shows that composites can have a whole that is more than the sum of its parts, so even a simple concept can be defined through its constituent analyses.

Checkpoint
Why does the second argument claim that existence cannot be defined?
A) Because existence is a simple concept without parts.
B) Because existence is a self-evident concept.
C) Because existence is a genus of all things.
D) Because existence is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The argument from simplicity fails because composite wholes can be more than the sum of their parts, so existence can be defined through its constituents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument from simplicity seems logical, but it fails because the whole can be more than the sum of its parts. Where in your own faith or understanding are you holding onto a simplistic view that might actually be more complex—and more real—than you admit?
?
Existence is not a mere accident added to things; it is the whole itself. How does that truth change the way you view your own existence before Allah? Are you living as if you are just a collection of accidental attributes, or as a unified whole accountable to Him?
