Objection on External vs. Mental Parts and Answer
Refutes the objection that existence's parts are external not mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that mental parts are like external parts is refuted, and how existence applies to its parts as an accident.

Core Idea
Mental parts are not external; existence applies to them as an accident.

This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the nature of existence's parts. The objector claims that the earlier solution only works for external parts, but the discussion is about mental parts. The author clarifies that mental parts are 'existences' to which existence applies accidentally, avoiding logical impossibilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's premise
The objector says the solution only applies to external parts, but the discussion concerns mental parts.
External parts are concrete, mental parts are abstract.
2
Understand the objector's challenge
On the homonymy view, there is no single absolute existence, so the earlier solution fails.
3
Examine the answer
The answer redefines the parts as 'existences' to which existence applies accidentally, avoiding the two impossibilities.
This shows mental parts are not like external parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: هذا إنما يستقيم في الأجزاء الخارجية، وكلامنا في الأجزاء العقلية التي يقع بها التحديد إلزاماً لمن اعترف بزيادة الوجود على الماهية، إذ ليس على القول بالاشتراك اللفظي وجود مطلق، يدعي بداهته، أو اكتسابه، بل له معان بعضها بديهي وبعضها كسبي، وحينئذ لا يصح الحل بأن أجزاء الوجود أمور تتصف بالعدم أو بوجود هو عين الماهية أولا تتصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم . قلنا: فالحل ما أشرنا إليه من أنها وجودات أي أمور يصدق عليها الوجود صدق العارض على المعروض، وحينئذ لا يلزم شيء من المحالين، ولا اتصاف الشيء بالوجود قبل تحقق الوجود، لأنه لا تمايز بين الجنس، والفصل، والنوع إلا بحسب العقل دون الخارج. فمعنى قولنا يكون الوجود محض ما ليس شيء من أجزائه بوجود أنه لا يكون شيء من الأجزاء نفس الوجود، وإن كان يصدق عليه الوجود كسائر المركبات بالنسبة إلى الأجزاء العقلية فإنها لا تكون نفس ذلك المركب لكنه يصدق عليها صدق العارض.
If it is said: This only applies to external parts, but our discussion is about mental parts by which definition occurs, as an obligation upon whoever admits the addition of existence to quiddity, since on the view of homonymy there is no absolute existence claimed to be self-evident or acquired, rather it has meanings some of which are self-evident and some acquired; and then the solution that the parts of existence are things that are characterized by non-existence or by an existence that is identical to quiddity, or are not characterized by existence nor non-existence, is not valid. We say: The solution is what we pointed to: that they are existences, i.e., things to which existence applies as an accident applies to its subject; and then neither of the two impossible things follows, nor the characterization of a thing with existence before the realization of existence, because there is no distinction between genus, differentia, and species except in the mind, not externally. So the meaning of our statement 'existence is pure that none of its parts is existence' is that none of the parts is existence itself, even though existence applies to it, as in other composites with respect to mental parts: they are not that composite itself, but existence applies to them accidentally.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objector argues that the author's earlier solution (about parts of existence) only works for external parts, but the discussion is about mental parts (the parts used in definition). The objector notes that on the view that existence is added to quiddity, there is no single absolute existence that is self-evident or acquired; rather, existence has multiple meanings. Therefore, the solution that parts of existence are either non-existent or identical to quiddity fails. The answer (قلنا) clarifies that the parts are 'existences' (entities to which existence applies as an accident). This avoids the two impossibilities: that a thing is characterized by existence before it exists, and that there is a distinction between genus, differentia, and species only in the mind, not externally. The meaning of 'existence is pure that none of its parts is existence' is that no part is existence itself, but existence applies to them as an accident, just as in other mental composites.

أجزاء عقلية
أجزاء خارجية
عارض
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parts of existence must exist externally and intrinsically.
Correction: Mental parts are entities to which existence applies accidentally, not as essential constituents.

Checkpoint

A) As an essential constituent
B) As an accident to a subject
C) As identical to the part
D) As a genus

Key Takeaway
Existence applies to its mental parts as an accident, avoiding the impossibility of characterization before existence.

Answering the Second Exposition: Mental Parts of Existence
Answers the second exposition that existence would be many existences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the view that the parts of existence are existential things without requiring real multiplicity.

Core Idea
Mental multiplicity does not imply real multiplicity in existence.

In this passage, the author directly addresses a key objection to the idea that existence has parts. The opponent argues that if the parts are themselves existences, then the one existence becomes many. The author responds by distinguishing between mental and real multiplicity, a crucial distinction in Islamic philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author states his position and the conditional argument.
2
Identify the opponent's assumption
The opponent assumes that if parts of existence are existences, then existence becomes many. Understand why this seems problematic.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author concedes the parts are existential but denies the necessity of real multiplicity. He points out that it would only follow if existence were identical to the subject. But in reality, multiplicity is only mental.
4
Consider the analogy with other composites
The author mentions other composites of mental parts. Think about how mental composition works in other cases (e.g., universal concepts).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن التقرير الثاني: أنا نختار أن أجزاء الوجود وجوديات، ولا نسلم لزوم كون الوجود الواحد وجودات، وإنما يلزم لو كان وجود الوجودي عينه ولم يكن الوجود الواحد في نفس الأمر وجودات بحسب العقل، ولا استحالة فيه كما في سائر المركبات من الأجزاء العقلية.
And the answer to the second exposition: We choose that the parts of existence are existential things, and we do not concede the necessity that one existence becomes many existences; it would only be necessary if the existence of an existential thing were identical to it, and the one existence in itself were not many existences according to the mind; and there is no impossibility in that, as in other composites of mental parts.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a second exposition (تقرير ثاني). The expositor presumably argued that if the parts of existence are existences, then the one existence would become many existences. The author refutes this: we affirm that the parts are existential (وجوديات), but we do not concede that the one existence becomes many existences in reality. The necessity would only follow if the existence of an existential thing were identical to it (so that each part is a separate existence). But in reality, the one existence is multiple only in mental consideration, and there is no impossibility in this, just as with other composites of mental parts.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the parts of existence are existences, then existence becomes many distinct real existences.
Correction: The multiplicity is only in mental analysis; in reality, existence remains one. It only becomes many if existence were identical to its subject, which it is not.

Refuting Arguments Against Defining Existence by Description
Refutes two arguments that existence cannot be defined by rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key objections to defining existence through description (rasm) and the author's response.

Core Idea
Rasm does not fail because we lack knowledge of specification—it only requires specification itself.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his discussion on whether existence can be defined via description (rasm). He presents two logical objections that aim to show that rasm is impossible for existence, and then he refutes them. Before reading the source, it is helpful to recall that rasm depends on specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) rather than knowledge of it, and that even if it does not fully reveal the essence, it can still be useful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: recall of earlier answer, two objections, then reply.
2
Identify the two objections
Find where the author says 'Two proofs may be adduced' and list both arguments. The first involves circularity (dawr), the second involves the concept of 'more known' (aʿraf).
These are the core challenges to defining existence by rasm.
3
Understand the circularity objection
The first argument claims that rasm requires knowledge of a necessary concomitant (lāzim) which is more specific than existence, leading to circularity. Why is that a problem?
Circularity would make the definition impossible by regress.
4
Understand the 'more known' objection
The second argument says rasm must use something more known than the definiendum, but nothing is more known than existence. Examine the reasoning: induction or universality.
This objection challenges the very possibility of finding a suitable definition.
5
Analyze the author's reply
The author denies most premises of both arguments. He adds that even if existence were the most known, the remaining premises would be unnecessary. How does this refute the objections?
Focus on the strategy: attacking premises rather than accepting the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عما ذكر في امتناع اكتسابه بالرسم ما سبق من أنه إنما يتوقف على الاختصاص لا على العلم بالاختصاص وأنه وإن لم يستلزم إفادة معرفة الحقيقة، لكنه قد يفيدها، وقد يستدل على امتناع اكتسابه بالرسم بوجهين. أحدهما: أنه يتوقف على العلم بوجود اللازم وثبوته للمرسوم، وهو أخص من مطلق الوجود، فيدور. وثانيهما: أن الرسم إنما يكون بالأعرف ولا أعرف من الوجود بحكم الاستقراء، أو لأنه أعم الأشياء بحسب التحقق دون الصدق، والأعم أعرف لكون شروطه ومعانداته أقل. والجواب: منع أكثر المقدمات على أنه لو ثبت كونه أعرف الأشياء لم يحتج إلى باقي المقدمات.
And the answer to what was mentioned about the impossibility of acquiring it by description is what preceded: that it depends only on specification, not on knowledge of specification, and that even if it does not entail providing knowledge of the essence, it may still provide it. And two proofs may be adduced for the impossibility of acquiring it by description: First: it depends on knowledge of the existence of the necessary concomitant and its establishment for the described thing, and it is more specific than absolute existence, so there is circularity. Second: description can only be by what is more known, and nothing is more known than existence by virtue of induction, or because it is the most universal of things in terms of realization, not truth; and the more universal is more known because its conditions and opposites are fewer. And the answer: denial of most premises, and even if it were established that it is the most known of things, the remaining premises would not be needed.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a prior objection about the impossibility of acquiring knowledge of existence through description (rasm). The author first recalls the earlier answer: it depends on specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) not on knowledge of specification, and even if rasm does not give knowledge of the essence, it may still be useful. Then two proofs are presented as objections: (1) definition by rasm requires knowledge of a necessary concomitant (lāzim) which is more specific than absolute existence, leading to circularity. (2) Rasm must use something more known (aʿraf), but nothing is more known than existence, either by induction or because existence is the most universal in realization. The answer refutes these by denying most premises, and adds that even if existence were the most known, the other premises would be unnecessary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rasm always requires knowledge of the necessary concomitant.
Correction: Rasm depends on specification (ikhtiṣāṣ) not on knowledge of specification, so the objection about circularity regarding knowledge is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against defining existence by rasm?
A) It requires knowledge of a necessary concomitant causing circularity
B) Existence is not real
C) Rasm cannot provide any knowledge
D) Existence is too simple for any definition

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of defining existence by rasm is refuted by denying the premises of the objections and emphasizing that rasm can still be useful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that even the most known can be questioned—what in your life have you assumed is 'most known' and therefore beyond examination? Will you examine it today?
?
The author denies premises to break circular reasoning. Think of a belief you hold that might be built on unexamined premises. If you deny those premises, would your belief collapse? Are you willing to follow the logic even if it means changing your mind?
?
Specification, not knowledge, is what rasm requires. In your own understanding of Allah, do you confuse knowing about Him (concepts, definitions) with being specified by Him (living in submission)? Which do you truly possess?

Second Objection: How Can Self-Evident Existence Be Defined?
Presents the second objection and its answer distinguishing real and nominal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining existence does not contradict its self-evident nature, and how the distinction between real and nominal definitions resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Self-evident things don't need real definitions, but nominal definitions are fine.

The second objection argues that if existence were self-evident, scholars wouldn't define it. But they do. Sanusi clarifies that only real definitions are unnecessary; nominal definitions simply clarify which meaning a word picks out. This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the objector argues that if existence were self-evident, no one would bother defining it.
Notice how the objector compares it to the lack of proof for self-evident propositions.
2
Understand the answer's distinction
Read the second part where Sanusi distinguishes real definition (ḥaddī or rasmī) from nominal definition (ismī). Self-evident concepts cannot be defined in the first sense but can be defined in the second.
Real definition aims to give conceptualization; nominal definition clarifies word meaning.
3
See how nominal definitions apply to existence
The author states that definitions of existence found in kalām works are of the nominal type. They specify the intended meaning of the word 'existence' without making the concept itself known.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Consider how this distinction preserves the self-evidence of existence while allowing scholars to discuss it. It shows that definitions are not always about learning new concepts.
This connects to the broader theme of how kalām defends first principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن الوجود لو كان بديهياً لم يشتغل العقلاء بتعريفه، كما لم يشتغلوا بإقامة البرهان على القضايا البديهية، لكونهم عرفوه بوجهه. وعن الثاني : أن البديهي لا يعرف تعريفاً حدياً أو رسمياً لإفادة تصوره لكن قد يعرف تعريفاً اسمياً لإفادة المراد من اللفظ وتصور المعنى من حيث أنه مدلول لفظ وإن كان مت وراً في نفسه، ومن حيث أنه مدلول لفظ آخر، وتعريفات الوجود من هذا القبيل .
The second: That if existence were self-evident, the intelligent would not have busied themselves with defining it, just as they did not busy themselves with establishing proof for self-evident propositions, because they know it by its face. And the answer to the second: That the self-evident is not known by a definition of limit (ḥaddī) or description (rasmī) for the purpose of conceptualizing it, but it may be known by a nominal definition for the purpose of indicating the intended meaning of the word and the conception of the meaning insofar as it is the signification of a word, even if it is concealed in itself, and insofar as it is the signification of another word. And the definitions of existence are of this kind.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection (al-thānī) and its answer. The objection argues that if existence were self-evident, scholars would not have spent effort defining it, just as they do not prove self-evident propositions. The answer distinguishes two types of definition: real definition (ḥaddī or rasmī), which aims to provide conceptualization (taṣawwur) of something not yet conceived, and nominal definition (taʿrīf ismī), which clarifies which meaning a particular word refers to. The self-evident is already conceptually known, so it does not need a real definition. However, because the word 'existence' might be ambiguous or shared among different concepts, a nominal definition is useful to specify the intended referent without contradicting its self-evident nature. The definitions of existence found in kalam works are of this nominal type. Thus, the objection is refuted.

الحدي
الرسمي
الاسمي
التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence can be defined, then it cannot be self-evident.
Correction: The author shows that only real definitions would contradict self-evidence; nominal definitions are simply clarifications of word meaning and do not affect the self-evidence of the concept.

Checkpoint
What type of definition does the author say can apply to self-evident concepts like existence?
A) Real definition (ḥaddī or rasmī)
B) Nominal definition (ismī)
C) Both types
D) No definition at all

Key Takeaway
The objection that defining existence contradicts its self-evidence is refuted by distinguishing real definitions (which provide conceptualization) from nominal definitions (which clarify word meaning).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
How often do you confuse linguistic clarification with real knowledge? Can you recall a time when you thought you understood something because you could define it, but you had not truly internalized it? What will you change after this lesson?

Division of Existence and the Logical Principle of Commonality
Sanusi explains the logical rule of division and gives an absurd counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the division of existence into necessary and possible logically implies a shared concept (al-wujud al-mumkin) and how this principle prevents absurd divisions.

Core Idea
A valid division requires the divided term to apply to every part.

This passage clarifies a crucial logical point in the argument from division. When theologians say existence is divided into necessary and possible, they rely on the rule that the divided whole must be true of each part. Without this, the division would be meaningless. Let's read Sanusi's concise explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the author's concise style.
The passage is a short footnote, so read carefully.
2
Identify the logical principle
The passage states that division into parts indicates commonality because the divided term is true of each part. Make sure you understand this before moving on.
This is the key idea: 'صدق المقسم على كل قسم'.
3
Consider the counterexample
The author gives an absurd example: 'man is either stone or tree'. Why is it absurd? Because 'man' is not true of stone or tree. This clarifies why divisions that don't share the divided term are invalid.
This helps you see the logical necessity of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الوجود الممكن وتقسم الشيء إلى قسمين أو أقسام يدل على اشتراك القسمين أو الأقسام في المقسم لصدقه على كل وإلا كان بمثابة أن يقال مثلاً: الإنسان إما حجر وإما شجر.
And it is possible existence. The division of a thing into two or more parts indicates the commonality of the two or more parts in the divided whole because it is true of each, otherwise it would be like saying, for example: man is either stone or tree.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This is a footnote expanding on the proof from division in the second investigation. It explains that the 'existence' referred to in the division is possible existence (al-wujud al-mumkin). The logical principle is that dividing a thing into two or more parts indicates that the parts share the concept of the whole (the divided term), because the whole is true of each part. If this were not the case, the division would be absurd, like saying 'man is either stone or tree', since neither stone nor tree falls under the concept of man.

Key Terms

التقسيم
المقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Division into two categories always implies a shared concept, even if the categories are unrelated.
Correction: The passage clarifies that shared concept is only valid if the divided term applies to each part; otherwise it's absurd like 'man is stone or tree'.

Checkpoint
Why does the division of existence into necessary and possible imply a common concept?
A) Because both are real.
B) Because the term 'existence' applies to both.
C) Because the whole is true of each part.
D) Because the author says so.

Key Takeaway
For a division to be logically sound, the divided term must be true of every part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often divide your life into 'sacred' and 'secular' — but are both truly about serving Allah? If not, you are making an absurd division like 'man is either stone or tree'. Which part of your life do you treat as though Allah is not its true term? What would it mean to see that part as truly belonging to Him?
?
Consider the categories you use to judge people: 'pious' vs 'sinner'. Are these categories true of every person you place in them? Or do you sometimes label someone 'sinner' even though they have faith? Sanusi's lesson warns that a division must be true of each part. Where have you misapplied a category to someone that doesn't actually fit them?
?
This logical rule seems simple, yet its violation hides in your heart: you might divide your intentions into 'for Allah' and 'for myself', but if a single action truly belongs to both, your division is false. Is there an action you are currently doing where you are trying to split your intention? Will you commit to making every action pure for Allah alone?

The Division Argument for the Unity of Existence
First argument and reply to first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rational division of existent into substance and accident supports the claim that existence is a single universal concept.

Core Idea
If existence were merely a word with multiple meanings, rational division would be impossible.

Taftazani begins his defense of the unity of existence by pointing to a simple observation: any possible existent can be classified as either a substance or an accident. This division, he argues, is valid regardless of how we use words, implying that there is a shared conceptual meaning underlying all instances of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author uses a division to argue for a common concept.
2
Identify the division
The passage states that every possible existent is either substance or accident. That division presupposes a common meaning of 'existent'.
3
Understand the objection
The objector says this division might just be about the ambiguous word 'existence', not a universal concept. The author replies that we are certain of the division even ignoring language.
This reply is crucial because it grounds the argument in rational necessity, not linguistic convention.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: if the division were only about words, how could we be certain about classifying things regardless of language? This shows there is a real common concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكل، بأن يقال الموجود من الممكن إما جوهر أو عرض، ومن الجوهر إما إنسان أو غيره. فإن قيل: على الوجهين الأولين لم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر الباقي المقطوع به هو تحقق معنى من معاني لفظ الوجود لا مفهوم له كلي، وأن يكون التقسيم لبيان مفهومات اللفظ المشترك كما يقال العين إما فوارة وإما باصرة لا لبيان أقسام مفهوم كلي. قلنا: لأنا نجد الجزم وصحة التقسيم مع قطع النظر عن الوضع واللغة، ولفظ الوجود. فإن نوقض الوجهان بالماهية والتشخيص حيث يبقى الجزم بأن لعلة الحادث ماهية وتشخصاً مع التردد في كونها واجباً أو ممكناً وتقسيم كل منهما إلى الواجب والممكن، مع أن شيئاً من الماهيات والتشخصات ليس بمشترك بين الكل. أجيب بأن مطلق الماهية والتشخص أيضاً مفهوم كلي مشترك بين الماهيات والتشخصات المخصوصة فلا نقض، وإنما يرد لو ادعينا أن الوجودات متماثلة حقيقتها مفهوم الوجود ولا خفاء في أن شيئاً من الوجود لا يدل على ذلك. الثالث: أنه لو لم يكن للوجود مفهوم مشترك لم يتم الحصر في الموجود والمعدوم، لأنا إذا قلنا: الإنسان متصف بالوجود بأحد المعاني أو معدوم عند العقل تجويز أن يكون متصفاً بالوجود بمعنى آخر ويفتقر إلى إبطاله وهذا لا يتوقف على اتحاد مفهوم العدم، إذ على تقدير تعدده كان عدم الحصر أظهر لجواز أن يكون متصفاً بالعدم بمعنى آخر فلذا عدلنا عما ذكره القوم من مفهوم العدم واحد فلو لم يتحد مفهوم مقابله لبطل الحصر العقلي وجعلنا اتحاد مفهوم العدم وجهاً رابعاً تقريره أن مفهوم العدم واحد، فلو لم يكن للوجود مفهوم واحد لما كانا نقيضين ضرورة ارتفاعهما عن الوجود بمعنى آخر. واللازم باطل قطعاً. فإن قيل: لا نسلم اتحاد مفهوم العدم بل الوجود نفس الحقيقة والعدم رفعها، فلكل وجود رفع يقابله. قلنا: سواء جعل رفع الوجود بمعنى الكون المشترك، أو بمعنى نفس الحقيقة، فهو مفهوم واحد بالضرورة، وإنما التعدد بالإضافة. فإن قيل: لا خفاء في أن اللا إنسان واللافرس واللاشجر وغير ذلك مفهومات مختلفة فإن لفظ العدم موضوعاً بإزاء كل منها لم يتحد مفهومه. قلنا: الكل مشترك في مفهوم لا. وهو معنى العدم ولا نعني باتحاد المفهوم سوى هذا.
The whole (al-kull) is such that it is said: the existent from the possible is either substance or accident, and from substance either human or other. For if it is said: concerning the first two modes, why is it not permissible that the remaining matter that is certain is the realization of a meaning from the meanings of the word 'existence' without it having a universal concept, and that the division is to explain the connotations of the ambiguous word, as it is said 'the eye is either a spring or a seeing organ', not to explain the divisions of a universal concept? We say: because we find certainty and the validity of division while disregarding convention (al-wad') and language and the word 'existence'. For if the two modes are refuted by quiddity (al-mahiyya) and individuation (al-tashkhis), where certainty remains that the cause of the contingent has a quiddity and individuation, while hesitating as to whether it is necessary or possible, and each of them is divided into necessary and possible, even though none of the quiddities and individuations is common to all. It is answered: absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are also a universal concept common to particular quiddities and individuations, so there is no refutation. It would only be problematic if we claimed that existences are similar in reality to the concept of existence, and it is no secret that no existence indicates that. Third: if existence had no common concept, the exhaustive division (al-hasr) into existent and non-existent would not be complete, because if we say 'human is qualified by existence in one sense or is non-existent in the intellect', it is possible that it is qualified by existence in another sense, and we need to refute that; and this does not depend on the unity of the concept of non-existence, for on the assumption of its multiplicity, the incompleteness of the division would be more obvious due to the possibility of being qualified by non-existence in another sense. Hence we turned away from what the people mentioned that the concept of non-existence is one, so if its opposite did not have a single concept, the rational exhaustive division would be invalid. And we made the unity of the concept of non-existence a fourth argument, its formulation being: the concept of non-existence is one, so if existence did not have a single concept, they would not be contradictories, since they would both be absent for existence in another sense. The consequent is absolutely false. For if it is said: we do not accept the unity of the concept of non-existence; rather, existence is the reality itself and non-existence is its removal, so each existence has a removal corresponding to it. We say: whether we make the removal of existence in the sense of the common being (al-kawn al-mushtarak) or in the sense of the reality itself, it is necessarily one concept; multiplicity is only by relation. For if it is said: it is no secret that 'non-human', 'non-horse', 'non-tree' etc. are different concepts, for the word 'non-existence' is put in place of each of them and its concept is not unified. We say: all share in the concept 'not' (la). That is the meaning of non-existence, and we mean by the unity of the concept nothing other than this.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument defending the thesis that the concept of existence is a single common universal (kulli mushtarak). The author (al-Taftazani) presents three arguments and responds to objections. First, he states that any possible existent is either substance or accident, etc., implying a common division. An objector suggests that the division might only be of the ambiguous word (lafz mushtarak), not of a universal concept. The author replies that we have certainty in the division regardless of conventional language. A second objection is raised: quiddity and individuation also seem to be common yet they are divided into necessary and possible, but they are not common to all. The answer is that absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are themselves universal concepts, so no refutation. The third argument is the strongest: without a single common concept of existence, the exhaustive dichotomy (hasr) between existent and non-existent would fail—since one might be existent in one sense and non-existent in another, thereby escaping the division. The author notes that this does not depend on the unity of the concept of non-existence either; but if we assume unity of non-existence, then existence must also be one to preserve contradiction. The author then turns the unity of non-existence into a fourth argument. An objector denies that non-existence is one, claiming existence and non-existence are correlatives. The author responds that whether we define non-existence as the removal of existence in the sense of common being or in the sense of the reality itself, it is still a single concept; multiplicity is only external. Another objector points out that negations like 'non-human' have different meanings. The author replies that all such negations share the common element 'not' (la), which is the intended meaning of non-existence.

Key Terms
الوجود
المفهوم المشترك
الكل
الجوهر
العرض
الوضع
اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division of existent into substance and accident is just a linguistic classification.
Correction: Taftazani argues that this division is rationally necessary and independent of language, implying a common concept of existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the division of existent into substance and accident prove the unity of the concept of existence?
A) Because the division is exhaustive.
B) Because we are certain of the division regardless of language.
C) Because the word 'existence' is ambiguous.
D) Because substance and accident are opposites.

Key Takeaway
The rational division of existent into substance and accident is only possible if there is a single universal concept of existence that applies to all existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Taftazani insists that we have certainty about the division of existence independent of words. When in your life have you doubted that something is real because of how it was described? How can you ground your faith in rational certainty beyond mere language starting today?
?
If existence is a universal concept that applies to both you and the Creator, what does that imply about your relationship with Him? Are you living as if your existence is ultimately dependent on His? What one change can you make today to acknowledge that dependence?
?
The objector in this argument reduces the division to a game of words. Where in your own thinking about God might you be hiding behind comfortable phrases instead of seeking rational certainty? What step will you take this week to move from mere words to conviction?

The Quiddity and Individuation Objection Answered
Second objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that quiddity and individuation also seem to be common but are not actually common does not undermine the unity of existence.

Core Idea
Absolute quiddity and individuation are themselves universal concepts, so the objection fails.

A second objection is raised against the unity of existence: quiddity and individuation also appear to be common to all things—we say every contingent has a quiddity and an individuation—yet they are not shared between necessary and possible existents in the same way. Taftazani shows that this objection actually supports his position, because absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are universal concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector says that both quiddity and individuation are divided into necessary and possible, yet they are not common to all. How does this challenge the existence argument?
2
Analyze the reply
Taftazani responds that absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are indeed universal concepts shared by all quiddities and individuations. So the objection is not a refutation.
He also clarifies that the argument does not claim existences are similar in reality, only that the concept is shared.
3
Understand the clarification
Note that Taftazani says the objection would only be problematic if we claimed existences are identical in reality. But we only claim the concept is shared.
This shows the nuance: unity of concept does not imply unity of reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكل، بأن يقال الموجود من الممكن إما جوهر أو عرض، ومن الجوهر إما إنسان أو غيره. فإن قيل: على الوجهين الأولين لم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر الباقي المقطوع به هو تحقق معنى من معاني لفظ الوجود لا مفهوم له كلي، وأن يكون التقسيم لبيان مفهومات اللفظ المشترك كما يقال العين إما فوارة وإما باصرة لا لبيان أقسام مفهوم كلي. قلنا: لأنا نجد الجزم وصحة التقسيم مع قطع النظر عن الوضع واللغة، ولفظ الوجود. فإن نوقض الوجهان بالماهية والتشخيص حيث يبقى الجزم بأن لعلة الحادث ماهية وتشخصاً مع التردد في كونها واجباً أو ممكناً وتقسيم كل منهما إلى الواجب والممكن، مع أن شيئاً من الماهيات والتشخصات ليس بمشترك بين الكل. أجيب بأن مطلق الماهية والتشخص أيضاً مفهوم كلي مشترك بين الماهيات والتشخصات المخصوصة فلا نقض، وإنما يرد لو ادعينا أن الوجودات متماثلة حقيقتها مفهوم الوجود ولا خفاء في أن شيئاً من الوجود لا يدل على ذلك. الثالث: أنه لو لم يكن للوجود مفهوم مشترك لم يتم الحصر في الموجود والمعدوم، لأنا إذا قلنا: الإنسان متصف بالوجود بأحد المعاني أو معدوم عند العقل تجويز أن يكون متصفاً بالوجود بمعنى آخر ويفتقر إلى إبطاله وهذا لا يتوقف على اتحاد مفهوم العدم، إذ على تقدير تعدده كان عدم الحصر أظهر لجواز أن يكون متصفاً بالعدم بمعنى آخر فلذا عدلنا عما ذكره القوم من مفهوم العدم واحد فلو لم يتحد مفهوم مقابله لبطل الحصر العقلي وجعلنا اتحاد مفهوم العدم وجهاً رابعاً تقريره أن مفهوم العدم واحد، فلو لم يكن للوجود مفهوم واحد لما كانا نقيضين ضرورة ارتفاعهما عن الوجود بمعنى آخر. واللازم باطل قطعاً. فإن قيل: لا نسلم اتحاد مفهوم العدم بل الوجود نفس الحقيقة والعدم رفعها، فلكل وجود رفع يقابله. قلنا: سواء جعل رفع الوجود بمعنى الكون المشترك، أو بمعنى نفس الحقيقة، فهو مفهوم واحد بالضرورة، وإنما التعدد بالإضافة. فإن قيل: لا خفاء في أن اللا إنسان واللافرس واللاشجر وغير ذلك مفهومات مختلفة فإن لفظ العدم موضوعاً بإزاء كل منها لم يتحد مفهومه. قلنا: الكل مشترك في مفهوم لا. وهو معنى العدم ولا نعني باتحاد المفهوم سوى هذا.
The whole (al-kull) is such that it is said: the existent from the possible is either substance or accident, and from substance either human or other. For if it is said: concerning the first two modes, why is it not permissible that the remaining matter that is certain is the realization of a meaning from the meanings of the word 'existence' without it having a universal concept, and that the division is to explain the connotations of the ambiguous word, as it is said 'the eye is either a spring or a seeing organ', not to explain the divisions of a universal concept? We say: because we find certainty and the validity of division while disregarding convention (al-wad') and language and the word 'existence'. For if the two modes are refuted by quiddity (al-mahiyya) and individuation (al-tashkhis), where certainty remains that the cause of the contingent has a quiddity and individuation, while hesitating as to whether it is necessary or possible, and each of them is divided into necessary and possible, even though none of the quiddities and individuations is common to all. It is answered: absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are also a universal concept common to particular quiddities and individuations, so there is no refutation. It would only be problematic if we claimed that existences are similar in reality to the concept of existence, and it is no secret that no existence indicates that. Third: if existence had no common concept, the exhaustive division (al-hasr) into existent and non-existent would not be complete, because if we say 'human is qualified by existence in one sense or is non-existent in the intellect', it is possible that it is qualified by existence in another sense, and we need to refute that; and this does not depend on the unity of the concept of non-existence, for on the assumption of its multiplicity, the incompleteness of the division would be more obvious due to the possibility of being qualified by non-existence in another sense. Hence we turned away from what the people mentioned that the concept of non-existence is one, so if its opposite did not have a single concept, the rational exhaustive division would be invalid. And we made the unity of the concept of non-existence a fourth argument, its formulation being: the concept of non-existence is one, so if existence did not have a single concept, they would not be contradictories, since they would both be absent for existence in another sense. The consequent is absolutely false. For if it is said: we do not accept the unity of the concept of non-existence; rather, existence is the reality itself and non-existence is its removal, so each existence has a removal corresponding to it. We say: whether we make the removal of existence in the sense of the common being (al-kawn al-mushtarak) or in the sense of the reality itself, it is necessarily one concept; multiplicity is only by relation. For if it is said: it is no secret that 'non-human', 'non-horse', 'non-tree' etc. are different concepts, for the word 'non-existence' is put in place of each of them and its concept is not unified. We say: all share in the concept 'not' (la). That is the meaning of non-existence, and we mean by the unity of the concept nothing other than this.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument defending the thesis that the concept of existence is a single common universal (kulli mushtarak). The author (al-Taftazani) presents three arguments and responds to objections. First, he states that any possible existent is either substance or accident, etc., implying a common division. An objector suggests that the division might only be of the ambiguous word (lafz mushtarak), not of a universal concept. The author replies that we have certainty in the division regardless of conventional language. A second objection is raised: quiddity and individuation also seem to be common yet they are divided into necessary and possible, but they are not common to all. The answer is that absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are themselves universal concepts, so no refutation. The third argument is the strongest: without a single common concept of existence, the exhaustive dichotomy (hasr) between existent and non-existent would fail—since one might be existent in one sense and non-existent in another, thereby escaping the division. The author notes that this does not depend on the unity of the concept of non-existence either; but if we assume unity of non-existence, then existence must also be one to preserve contradiction. The author then turns the unity of non-existence into a fourth argument. An objector denies that non-existence is one, claiming existence and non-existence are correlatives. The author responds that whether we define non-existence as the removal of existence in the sense of common being or in the sense of the reality itself, it is still a single concept; multiplicity is only external. Another objector points out that negations like 'non-human' have different meanings. The author replies that all such negations share the common element 'not' (la), which is the intended meaning of non-existence.

Key Terms
الماهية
التشخيص
مطلق
مفهوم كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quiddity and individuation are not universal concepts because specific quiddities differ.
Correction: The absolute concept of quiddity is universal; it is the specific instances that differ.

Checkpoint
How does Taftazani counter the objection that quiddity and individuation are not common concepts?
A) He denies that quiddity and individuation are divided into necessary and possible.
B) He says absolute quiddity and individuation are universal concepts.
C) He claims existences are identical in reality.
D) He admits the objection is valid.

Key Takeaway
Absolute quiddity and absolute individuation are universal concepts; thus the objection actually reinforces the unity of the concept of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the concept of 'existence', do you tend to confuse the universal idea with the concrete realities of things? How might this distinction change the way you approach theological arguments about God's necessity?
?
Have you ever rejected a shared truth (like the oneness of God) because you saw too much diversity in how people express it? How does the lesson's distinction between absolute concept and particular instances challenge you to embrace unity without ignoring differences?

The Exhaustive Division and the Unity of Non-Existence
Third and fourth arguments with objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the unity of existence based on the exhaustive division between existent and non-existent, and how the unity of non-existence also supports this.

Core Idea
Without a single concept of existence, the exhaustive division between existent and non-existent would collapse.

Taftazani's third argument is perhaps the most powerful: if existence had multiple meanings, then an entity could be existent in one sense and non-existent in another, breaking the exhaustive dichotomy that reason demands. He then introduces a fourth argument from the unity of non-existence, and answers objections about the nature of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Notice the conditional: if existence had no single concept, the exhaustive division (hasr) into existent and non-existent would be incomplete. This is because an entity might be existent in one sense and not in another.
2
Understand the fourth argument
Taftazani then presents that the concept of non-existence is one, so its opposite must also be one to preserve contradiction.
3
Analyze the objections and replies
Two objections are raised: one that existence itself is the reality and non-existence its removal (so each has a unique removal), and another that negations like 'non-human' have different meanings. Taftazani replies to both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكل، بأن يقال الموجود من الممكن إما جوهر أو عرض، ومن الجوهر إما إنسان أو غيره. فإن قيل: على الوجهين الأولين لم لا يجوز أن يكون الأمر الباقي المقطوع به هو تحقق معنى من معاني لفظ الوجود لا مفهوم له كلي، وأن يكون التقسيم لبيان مفهومات اللفظ المشترك كما يقال العين إما فوارة وإما باصرة لا لبيان أقسام مفهوم كلي. قلنا: لأنا نجد الجزم وصحة التقسيم مع قطع النظر عن الوضع واللغة، ولفظ الوجود. فإن نوقض الوجهان بالماهية والتشخيص حيث يبقى الجزم بأن لعلة الحادث ماهية وتشخصاً مع التردد في كونها واجباً أو ممكناً وتقسيم كل منهما إلى الواجب والممكن، مع أن شيئاً من الماهيات والتشخصات ليس بمشترك بين الكل. أجيب بأن مطلق الماهية والتشخص أيضاً مفهوم كلي مشترك بين الماهيات والتشخصات المخصوصة فلا نقض، وإنما يرد لو ادعينا أن الوجودات متماثلة حقيقتها مفهوم الوجود ولا خفاء في أن شيئاً من الوجود لا يدل على ذلك. الثالث: أنه لو لم يكن للوجود مفهوم مشترك لم يتم الحصر في الموجود والمعدوم، لأنا إذا قلنا: الإنسان متصف بالوجود بأحد المعاني أو معدوم عند العقل تجويز أن يكون متصفاً بالوجود بمعنى آخر ويفتقر إلى إبطاله وهذا لا يتوقف على اتحاد مفهوم العدم، إذ على تقدير تعدده كان عدم الحصر أظهر لجواز أن يكون متصفاً بالعدم بمعنى آخر فلذا عدلنا عما ذكره القوم من مفهوم العدم واحد فلو لم يتحد مفهوم مقابله لبطل الحصر العقلي وجعلنا اتحاد مفهوم العدم وجهاً رابعاً تقريره أن مفهوم العدم واحد، فلو لم يكن للوجود مفهوم واحد لما كانا نقيضين ضرورة ارتفاعهما عن الوجود بمعنى آخر. واللازم باطل قطعاً. فإن قيل: لا نسلم اتحاد مفهوم العدم بل الوجود نفس الحقيقة والعدم رفعها، فلكل وجود رفع يقابله. قلنا: سواء جعل رفع الوجود بمعنى الكون المشترك، أو بمعنى نفس الحقيقة، فهو مفهوم واحد بالضرورة، وإنما التعدد بالإضافة. فإن قيل: لا خفاء في أن اللا إنسان واللافرس واللاشجر وغير ذلك مفهومات مختلفة فإن لفظ العدم موضوعاً بإزاء كل منها لم يتحد مفهومه. قلنا: الكل مشترك في مفهوم لا. وهو معنى العدم ولا نعني باتحاد المفهوم سوى هذا.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you live as if the world is a random chaos, or as if every atom is accounted for under a single divine order? What would change if you truly believed this?
?
Taftazani shows that even non-existence shares a common element, reminding you that nothing is outside the grasp of logical and divine delineation. Are there areas of your faith where you have been content with vague or contradictory beliefs, rather than seeking precise understanding? Will you commit to clarifying one such belief today?
?
The objection that non-existence is multiple because each removal is unique was refuted by the shared concept of 'not'. This teaches that underlying all diversity there is unity. How does this affect your view of the diversity of creation? Does it draw you to the One who gives all things their reality, and how can you increase your gratitude for His creation?

Logic and Technical Terms

7 min
The Unity of the Concept of Non-Existence
Proof that non-existence has one concept, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of non-existence must be one and how this supports the logical contradictoriness of existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence is one concept because negation itself is one.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a foundational issue in kalam ontology: whether non-existence (al-ʿadam) has a single concept or multiple. The author argues that non-existence is one, and this has a direct implication for existence. Before reading, consider what it means for two things to be contradictories. The passage begins with a bold claim and then defends it against two plausible objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the author's argument flow.
Pay attention to the two objections raised by an imagined opponent.
2
Identify the main argument
The author claims that if non-existence is one, then its opposite (existence) must also be one, otherwise they wouldn't be genuine contradictories. Can you spot how he uses the concept of 'logical division' (al-ḥaṣr al-ʿaqlī)?
3
Examine the first objection and answer
The opponent says existence is reality itself and non-existence is its removal. The author answers that whether removal is understood as common being or as reality itself, it's one concept. Note the phrase 'multiplicity is only by relation'.
4
Examine the second objection and answer
The opponent gives examples: non-human, non-horse, non-tree appear to have different concepts. The author replies that all share the concept of 'not' (lā), which is the meaning of non-existence. This is a key clarification.
This resolves the common misunderstanding that non-existence is multiple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مفهوم العدم واحد فلو لم يتحد مفهوم مقابله لبطل الحصر العقلي وجعلنا اتحاد مفهوم العدم وجهاً رابعاً تقريره أن مفهوم العدم واحد، فلو لم يكن للوجود مفهوم واحد لما كانا نقيضين ضرورة ارتفاعهما عن الوجود بمعنى آخر. واللازم باطل قطعاً. فإن قيل : لا نسلم اتحاد مفهوم العدم بل الوجود نفس الحقيقة والعدم رفعها، فلكل وجود رفع يقابله. قلنا: سواء جعل رفع الوجود بمعنى الكون المشترك، أو بمعنى نفس الحقيقة، فهو مفهوم واحد بالضرورة، وإنما التعدد بالإضافة. فإن قيل : لا خفاء في أن اللا إنسان واللافرس واللاشجر وغير ذلك مفهومات مختلفة فإن لفظ العدم موضوعاً بإزاء كل منها لم يتحد مفهومه . قلنا : الكل مشترك في مفهوم لا . وهو معنى العدم ولا نعني باتحاد المفهوم سوى هذا.
The concept of non-existence is one. For if the concept of its opposite were not one, the logical division would be invalid. And we made the unity of the concept of non-existence a fourth reason. Its elaboration: that the concept of non-existence is one, for if existence did not have one concept, they would not be contradictories, necessarily they would both be absent from existence in another sense. The consequent is necessarily false. If it is said: We do not accept the unity of the concept of non-existence. Rather, existence is the same as reality, and non-existence is its removal. So for each existence there is a removal opposing it. We say: Whether the removal of existence is understood as the common being (al-kawn al-mushtarak) or as the same reality, it is necessarily one concept. Multiplicity is only by relation. If it is said: It is clear that 'non-human', 'non-horse', 'non-tree' etc. are different concepts, for the word non-existence is posited for each of them, so its concept is not one. We say: All share in the concept 'not' (lā), which is the meaning of non-existence. And we do not mean by unity of concept other than this.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the unity of the concept of non-existence and defends it against two objections. The author first states that the concept of non-existence is one; if the concept of its opposite (existence) were not one, then the logical division (al-ḥaṣr al-ʿaqlī) between existence and non-existence would break down. This is introduced as a fourth reason for the unity of the concept of existence. The argument is that non-existence has a single concept; therefore, if existence had multiple concepts, they would not be strict contradictories (naqīḍayn) because they could both be absent from existence in another sense. The impossibility of this is asserted. Then an objection is raised: the objector does not accept the unity of the concept of non-existence. Rather, existence is the reality itself, and non-existence is its removal; each existence has its own removal. The author answers that whether removal is understood as the common being (al-kawn al-mushtarak) or as the reality itself, it is necessarily one concept. Multiplicity arises only by relation (bi-al-iḍāfa) to different realities. A second objection follows: it is obvious that 'non-human', 'non-horse', 'non-tree' are different concepts, because the word non-existence is applied to each. The author answers that all share in the concept of 'not' (lā), which is the meaning of non-existence. The unity intended is not beyond that. Thus the passage upholds the unity of the concept of non-existence and, by implication, the unity of the concept of existence as well.

Key Terms
مفهوم العدم
الحصر العقلي
الوجود
العدم
نقيضين
الكون المشترك
لا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The concept of non-existence is multiple because 'non-human' and 'non-horse' are different concepts.
Correction: All such negations share the concept 'not', which is the meaning of non-existence; unity is in that shared negation, not in the specific negated objects.

Checkpoint
Why does the author claim that the concept of existence must be one?
A) Because non-existence and existence must be strict contradictories.
B) Because existence is the opposite of non-existence.
C) Because the concept of non-existence is one.
D) Because the objection fails.

Key Takeaway
The concept of non-existence is one because all negations share the single concept of 'not'; this unity is essential for existence and non-existence to be logical contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists', do you mean existence in the same sense as when you say 'this tree exists'? If not, how does that affect your understanding of tawḥīd? Are you willing to accept that existence is a single concept that applies to God and creation in radically different ways, yet still one logical category?
?
The passage argues that all negations share the single concept of 'not'. Have you ever considered that your own non-existence is the same type of non-existence as that of a stone? Does this humble you, knowing that you are a contingent being that came from nothing? How does this shape your gratitude to Allah for bringing you into existence?
?
If existence and non-existence are strict contradictories, there is no middle ground. Your relationship with Allah is either existence (of imān) or non-existence (of kufr). Is there any area of your life where you are living as if you can have both? What step will you take today to eliminate that contradiction?

4 min
First Proof: Validity of Negating Existence
First indicator: validity of negating existence from quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first indicator that existence is additional to quiddity: the validity of negating existence from a quiddity without negating the quiddity itself.

Core Idea
If existence were identical to quiddity, we could not truly say something is not existent.

The first of five indicators presented by the author is the most straightforward: we can meaningfully deny existence of a quiddity (like the mythical ʿAnqā') while we cannot deny the quiddity of itself. This asymmetry points to a real distinction between existence and quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the asymmetry between negating existence and negating quiddity.
2
Understand the asymmetry in negation
The author argues that it is valid to negate existence from a quiddity (e.g., 'the ʿAnqā' is not existent') but it is not valid to negate the quiddity itself from itself (e.g., 'the ʿAnqā' is not the ʿAnqā''). This shows existence is not the same as quiddity.
3
Reflect on why this indicates addition
If existence were part of the quiddity, negating existence would change the quiddity. Since we can negate existence while preserving the quiddity, existence is additional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعلى الثاني صحة سليه عنها، وافادة حمله عليها، واكتساب ثبوته واتحاد مفهومه دونها وانفكاك تعقله عنها . أي ينبه على زيادة الوجود على الماهية أمور تجامع الوجود، وتنافي الماهية وذاتياتها . الأول صحة السلب فإنه يصح سلب الوجود عن الماهية مثل العنقاء ليس بموجود ولا يصح سلب الماهية وذاتياتها عن نفسها . الثاني : إفادة الحمل فإن حمل الوجود على الماهية المعلومة بالكنه يفيد فائدة غير حاصلة بخلاف حمل الماهية وذاتياتها . الثالث : اكتساب الثبوت فإن التصديق بثبوت الوجود للماهية يفتقر إلى كسب ونظر كوجود الجن مثلا بخلاف ثبوت الماهية وذاتياتها لها . الرابع : اتحاد المفهوم، فإن وجود الإنسان والفرس والشجر مفهوم واحد وهو الكون في الأعيان ومفهوم الإنسان والفرس والشجر مختلف . الخامس : الانفكاك في التعقل فإنا قد نتصور الماهية ولا نتصور كونها أما في الخارج فظاهر، وأما في الذهن فلأنا لا نعلم أن التصور هو الوجود في العقل، ولو سلم فلبديل، ولو سلم فتصور الشيء لا يستلزم تعقل تصوره ولو سلم فيجوز أن يوجد في الخارج ما لا نعقله أصلاً وأيضاً قد نصدق ثبوت الماهية وذاتياتها لها بمعنى أنها هي من غير تصديق بثبوت الوجود العيني أو الذهني لها، فأثرها لفظ التعقل ليعم التصور والتصديق، وعبارة الكثيرين أنا نتصور ماهية المثلث ونشك في وجودها العيني والذهني، ويرد عليها الاعتراض بأنه لا يفيد المطلوب لأن حاصله أنا ندرك الماهية تصوراً ولا ندرك الوجود تصديقاً، وهذا لا ينافي اتحادهما . واعلم أن هذه تنبيهات على بطلان القول بأن المعقول من وجود الشيء هو ذلك الشيء وبعضها يدل على ذلك في الواجب والممكن جميعاً، المعقول من .
He said: And on the second [argument], the validity of negating it, the benefit of predicating it, the acquisition of its affirmation, the unity of its concept apart from it, and the detachment of its conception from it. That is, it points to the addition of existence to quiddity by matters that accompany existence and are incompatible with quiddity and its essentials. The first: the validity of negation, for it is valid to negate existence from quiddity, like the 'Anqa' is not existent, but it is not valid to negate quiddity and its essentials from itself. The second: the benefit of predication, for predicating existence of a quiddity known by its essence gives a benefit not obtained, unlike predicating quiddity and its essentials. The third: the acquisition of affirmation, for believing in the affirmation of existence for the quiddity requires acquisition and reflection, like the existence of jinn, for example, unlike the affirmation of quiddity and its essentials for it. The fourth: the unity of the concept, for the existence of man, horse, and tree is one concept, which is being in external reality (al-kawn fi al-aʿyān), while the concept of man, horse, and tree is different. The fifth: detachment in intellection, for we may conceive the quiddity and not conceive its existence. As for [its existence] in the external, it is obvious [that we may not conceive it]; as for in the mind, because we do not know that conception is existence in the intellect. Even if it is conceded, there is an alternative; even if conceded, conception of a thing does not necessitate intellection of its conception; even if conceded, it is possible that there exists in the external what we do not intellectually grasp at all. Also, we may assent to the affirmation of quiddity and its essentials for it, meaning that it is it, without assenting to the affirmation of external or mental existence for it. So the word 'intellection' (al-taʿaqqul) was used to include conception and assent. And the expression of many is: we conceive the quiddity of a triangle and doubt its external and mental existence. And an objection is brought against it, that it does not yield the desired, because its result is that we perceive the quiddity by conception and do not perceive existence by assent, and this does not contradict their unity. And know that these are indicators of the falsity of the statement that the intelligible of the existence of a thing is that thing itself. Some of them indicate that concerning the Necessary and the possible alike. The intelligible of...
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage enumerates five indicators (tanbīhāt) that existence is additional to quiddity (al-māhiyya). The author first gives a summary of the five: (1) the validity of negating existence from quiddity, (2) the benefit of predicating existence, (3) the acquisition of the affirmation of existence, (4) the unity of the concept of existence, and (5) the detachment of conceiving existence from conceiving quiddity. He then explains each in turn. The first indicator: it is valid to say that a quiddity (like the mythical ʿAnqā') is not existent, but it is not valid to negate the quiddity from itself. This shows that existence is not identical to quiddity. The second: predicating existence of a quiddity provides new information, unlike predicating the quiddity's own essentials. The third: affirming existence requires empirical or rational acquisition (kashf wa naẓar), whereas affirming the quiddity itself is self-evident. The fourth: existence has a single concept (e.g., 'being in external reality') across all quiddities, whereas quiddities themselves are multiple. The fifth indicator is the most extensive. The author argues that we can conceive a quiddity without conceiving its existence. This is obvious for external existence, but even for mental existence, we may not know that conception is itself existence in the mind. He then discusses several hypothetical concessions, leading to the point that we may affirm the quiddity itself without affirming its existence. He mentions that many scholars express this by saying they conceive the quiddity of a triangle and doubt its existence. However, an objection is raised against this: the argument only shows a distinction in conception, not a real distinction between existence and quiddity. The objection states that this does not contradict their unity (i.e., existence and quiddity could still be one in reality). The author does not provide a response in this passage. Finally, the author notes that these five indicators collectively disprove the view that the intelligible of existence is the thing itself, and some of them apply to both the Necessary (al-wājib) and the contingent (al-mumkin). The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
صحة السلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first indicator, what does the validity of negating existence from a quiddity show?
A) Existence is the same as quiddity.
B) Existence is additional to quiddity.
C) Quiddity cannot be negated.
D) The ʿAnqā' exists.

Key Takeaway
The first indicator shows that existence and quiddity are logically separable because we can negate one but not the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often identify yourself with your existence—your body, your life. But this proof shows that your quiddity (your essence) is distinct from your existence. If your existence were taken away in death, would you still be 'you' in Allah's knowledge? How does that change your attachment to this fleeting life? Commit today to focusing on the state of your quiddity (your deeds and faith) rather than just your existence.
?
Think of a sin you committed that no longer exists—it's in the past, non-existent now. But its quiddity (the act itself) remains recorded. This lesson shows that quiddity can be negated from existence but not from itself. How does the permanence of your deeds in Allah's knowledge affect your resolve to seek forgiveness? Will you take a concrete step today to seek tawbah?
?
You desire something that does not yet exist—a goal, a relationship, a possession. You conceive of it as non-existent, yet its quiddity is real in your mind and known to Allah. This proof reminds you that existence is not its essence. If you truly believed this, would you worry less about 'having' it and more about 'being' worthy of it? Make a specific commitment to trust Allah with bringing quiddities into existence.

Second and Third Proofs: Predication and Affirmation
Second and third indicators: benefit of predication and acquisition of affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third indicators: predicating existence provides new information, and affirming existence requires acquisition and reflection.

Core Idea
Existence adds new meaning and requires evidence, unlike the self-evident quiddity.

The second and third indicators share a common theme: existence provides additional information beyond quiddity and requires a separate cognitive act to affirm. This further supports the thesis that existence is not identical to quiddity but an added attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for both indicators together. Notice the contrast between existence and quiddity in each case.
2
Analyze the benefit of predication
The second indicator: when we say 'Man is existent', we gain new information beyond 'Man is man'. This shows existence adds meaning.
3
Analyze the acquisition of affirmation
The third indicator: affirming existence requires rational inquiry (e.g., proving the existence of jinn), while affirming the quiddity itself is immediate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Fourth Proof: Unity of the Concept of Existence
Fourth indicator: unity of the concept of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth indicator: the concept of existence is single across all quiddities, while quiddities themselves are diverse.

Core Idea
Existence has one meaning; quiddities have many. This suggests existence is not part of quiddity.

The fourth indicator highlights that existence as a concept is uniform across all things—it always means 'being in external reality'—whereas quiddities like man, horse, and tree have different definitions. This uniformity suggests existence is a separate attribute common to all quiddities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the fourth indicator. Notice the contrast between the unity of existence and the diversity of quiddities.
2
Understand the unity of existence concept
The author states that existence for man, horse, and tree has one concept: 'being in external reality'. This is a single meaning.
3
Contrast with diversity of quiddities
The quiddities themselves have different concepts. If existence were part of each quiddity, its concept would differ, but it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This is obvious for external existence, but even for mental existence, we may not know that conception is itself existence in the mind. He then discusses several hypothetical concessions, leading to the point that we may affirm the quiddity itself without affirming its existence. He mentions that many scholars express this by saying they conceive the quiddity of a triangle and doubt its existence. However, an objection is raised against this: the argument only shows a distinction in conception, not a real distinction between existence and quiddity. The objection states that this does not contradict their unity (i.e., existence and quiddity could still be one in reality). The author does not provide a response in this passage. Finally, the author notes that these five indicators collectively disprove the view that the intelligible of existence is the thing itself, and some of them apply to both the Necessary (al-wājib) and the contingent (al-mumkin). The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
اتحاد المفهوم
الكون في الأعيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth indicator, what does the unity of the existence concept indicate?
A) Existence is different for each thing.
B) Existence is a single attribute added to quiddities.
C) Existence and quiddity are identical.
D) Quiddities have the same concept.

Key Takeaway
The fourth indicator shows existence is a single concept across diverse quiddities, supporting its addition.

What would it take for you to shift your gratitude from 'what you are' to 'that you are'? Fifth Proof: Detachment in Intellection and Objection
Fifth indicator: detachment in intellection, with objection and concluding remark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth indicator (conceptual detachment of existence from quiddity) and the objection that it only proves conceptual distinction, not real distinction.

Core Idea
We can conceive a quiddity without its existence, but this only shows a mental separation, not necessarily a real one.

The fifth indicator is the most subtle: we can think of a quiddity (like a triangle) without thinking of its existence. The author presents several arguments, but an objection is raised that this only shows a distinction in conception, not in reality. This lesson examines the proof and the counterargument, clarifying the boundaries of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the fifth proof
Read the Arabic passage from 'الخامس' to the end. Notice the multiple concessive arguments and the objection at the end.
2
Understand the proof from conceptual detachment
The author argues that we can conceive a quiddity (like a triangle) without conceiving its existence, showing a mental separation.
3
Grasp the objection
The objection states that this only proves we perceive quiddity by conception and existence by assent, which does not rule out their real unity.
4
Reflect on the implications
The author concludes that these indicators collectively refute the view that the intelligible of existence is the thing itself. Some apply to both Necessary and possible beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وعلى الثاني صحة سليه عنها، وافادة حمله عليها، واكتساب ثبوته واتحاد مفهومه دونها وانفكاك تعقله عنها . أي ينبه على زيادة الوجود على الماهية أمور تجامع الوجود، وتنافي الماهية وذاتياتها . الأول صحة السلب فإنه يصح سلب الوجود عن الماهية مثل العنقاء ليس بموجود ولا يصح سلب الماهية وذاتياتها عن نفسها . الثاني : إفادة الحمل فإن حمل الوجود على الماهية المعلومة بالكنه يفيد فائدة غير حاصلة بخلاف حمل الماهية وذاتياتها . الثالث : اكتساب الثبوت فإن التصديق بثبوت الوجود للماهية يفتقر إلى كسب ونظر كوجود الجن مثلا بخلاف ثبوت الماهية وذاتياتها لها . الرابع : اتحاد المفهوم، فإن وجود الإنسان والفرس والشجر مفهوم واحد وهو الكون في الأعيان ومفهوم الإنسان والفرس والشجر مختلف . الخامس : الانفكاك في التعقل فإنا قد نتصور الماهية ولا نتصور كونها أما في الخارج فظاهر، وأما في الذهن فلأنا لا نعلم أن التصور هو الوجود في العقل، ولو سلم فلبديل، ولو سلم فتصور الشيء لا يستلزم تعقل تصوره ولو سلم فيجوز أن يوجد في الخارج ما لا نعقله أصلاً وأيضاً قد نصدق ثبوت الماهية وذاتياتها لها بمعنى أنها هي من غير تصديق بثبوت الوجود العيني أو الذهني لها، فأثرها لفظ التعقل ليعم التصور والتصديق، وعبارة الكثيرين أنا نتصور ماهية المثلث ونشك في وجودها العيني والذهني، ويرد عليها الاعتراض بأنه لا يفيد المطلوب لأن حاصله أنا ندرك الماهية تصوراً ولا ندرك الوجود تصديقاً، وهذا لا ينافي اتحادهما . واعلم أن هذه تنبيهات على بطلان القول بأن المعقول من وجود الشيء هو ذلك الشيء وبعضها يدل على ذلك في الواجب والممكن جميعاً، المعقول من .
He said: And on the second [argument], the validity of negating it, the benefit of predicating it, the acquisition of its affirmation, the unity of its concept apart from it, and the detachment of its conception from it. That is, it points to the addition of existence to quiddity by matters that accompany existence and are incompatible with quiddity and its essentials. The first: the validity of negation, for it is valid to negate existence from quiddity, like the 'Anqa' is not existent, but it is not valid to negate quiddity and its essentials from itself. The second: the benefit of predication, for predicating existence of a quiddity known by its essence gives a benefit not obtained, unlike predicating quiddity and its essentials. The third: the acquisition of affirmation, for believing in the affirmation of existence for the quiddity requires acquisition and reflection, like the existence of jinn, for example, unlike the affirmation of quiddity and its essentials for it. The fourth: the unity of the concept, for the existence of man, horse, and tree is one concept, which is being in external reality (al-kawn fi al-aʿyān), while the concept of man, horse, and tree is different. The fifth: detachment in intellection, for we may conceive the quiddity and not conceive its existence. As for [its existence] in the external, it is obvious [that we may not conceive it]; as for in the mind, because we do not know that conception is existence in the intellect. Even if it is conceded, there is an alternative; even if conceded, conception of a thing does not necessitate intellection of its conception; even if conceded, it is possible that there exists in the external what we do not intellectually grasp at all. Also, we may assent to the affirmation of quiddity and its essentials for it, meaning that it is it, without assenting to the affirmation of external or mental existence for it. So the word 'intellection' (al-taʿaqqul) was used to include conception and assent. And the expression of many is: we conceive the quiddity of a triangle and doubt its external and mental existence. And an objection is brought against it, that it does not yield the desired, because its result is that we perceive the quiddity by conception and do not perceive existence by assent, and this does not contradict their unity. And know that these are indicators of the falsity of the statement that the intelligible of the existence of a thing is that thing itself. Some of them indicate that concerning the Necessary and the possible alike. The intelligible of...
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage enumerates five indicators (tanbīhāt) that existence is additional to quiddity (al-māhiyya). The author first gives a summary of the five: (1) the validity of negating existence from quiddity, (2) the benefit of predicating existence, (3) the acquisition of the affirmation of existence, (4) the unity of the concept of existence, and (5) the detachment of conceiving existence from conceiving quiddity. He then explains each in turn. The first indicator: it is valid to say that a quiddity (like the mythical ʿAnqā') is not existent, but it is not valid to negate the quiddity from itself. This shows that existence is not identical to quiddity. The second: predicating existence of a quiddity provides new information, unlike predicating the quiddity's own essentials. The third: affirming existence requires empirical or rational acquisition (kashf wa naẓar), whereas affirming the quiddity itself is self-evident. The fourth: existence has a single concept (e.g., 'being in external reality') across all quiddities, whereas quiddities themselves are multiple. The fifth indicator is the most extensive. The author argues that we can conceive a quiddity without conceiving its existence. This is obvious for external existence, but even for mental existence, we may not know that conception is itself existence in the mind. He then discusses several hypothetical concessions, leading to the point that we may affirm the quiddity itself without affirming its existence. He mentions that many scholars express this by saying they conceive the quiddity of a triangle and doubt its existence. However, an objection is raised against this: the argument only shows a distinction in conception, not a real distinction between existence and quiddity. The objection states that this does not contradict their unity (i.e., existence and quiddity could still be one in reality). The author does not provide a response in this passage. Finally, the author notes that these five indicators collectively disprove the view that the intelligible of existence is the thing itself, and some of them apply to both the Necessary (al-wājib) and the contingent (al-mumkin). The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
انفكاك التعقل
التصور
التصديق
الواجب
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the fifth proof conclusively proves a real distinction between existence and quiddity.
Correction: The objection shows that the proof only establishes a conceptual distinction; existence and quiddity could still be one in reality.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the fifth proof?
A) We cannot conceive quiddity without existence.
B) The proof only shows a distinction in conception, not in reality.
C) Existence is always known.
D) The triangle example is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The fifth indicator shows conceptual detachment, but the objection warns against inferring a real distinction from mental separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
