Muqayyad Requires Mental Priority of Existence
The author explains the different conceptual treatments of muqayyad and qabil regarding existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a qualifier (muqayyad) must be conceived as existing before it can be considered a source of existence, and how a receptacle (qabil) is conceived differently.

Core Idea
A qualifier must exist in the mind before qualifying; a receptacle must be free of both existence and non-existence.

This passage clarifies a key distinction in kalam: the conceptual priority of existence differs depending on whether something acts as a source or a receiver. Before we can think of something as causing existence, we must first think of it as existing. But a receptacle is considered in a neutral state — neither existent nor non-existent — to avoid logical contradictions. Let us see how the author explains this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for muqayyad
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the author explains why existence must be prior for a muqayyad. Focus on the reasoning: 'because as long as the thing is not observed as existing...'
Notice the contrast: 'contrary to the receptacle of existence.'
2
Understand the qabil's neutrality
Read the description of the receptacle (qabil). The mind must consider it devoid of both existence and non-existence to avoid two logical problems: obtaining the already obtained (hujul al-hasil) and contradiction of opposites.
This neutral state is the essence (mahiyya) as it is in itself.
3
Reflect on the guiding principle
Ask yourself: why does the muqayyad require existence priority while the qabil does not? The answer lies in their roles: one gives existence, the other receives it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بوجوب تقدمها عليه بالوجود فإنه ما لم يلحظ كون الشيء موجوداً امتنع أن يلحظ كونه مبدأ للوجود ومقيداً له بخلاف القابل للوجود فإنه لا بد أن باحظه العقل خالياً عن الوجود أي غير معتبر فيه الوجود لئلا يلزم حصول الحاصل، بل عن العدم أيضاً لئلا يلزم اجتماع المتنافيين. فإذن هي الماهية من حيث هي هي، وأما الذاتيات بالنسبة إلى الماهية والماهية بالنسبة إلى لوازمها فلا يجب تقدمها إلا بالوجود العقلي، لأن تقدمها بالذاتيات واتصافها بلوازمها إنما هو بحسب العقل، وإذا تحققت فتقدم قابل الوجود أيضاً كذلك لما سيجيء من أنه بحسب العقل فقط، لا كالجسم مع البياض.
By the necessity of its priority over it in existence, because as long as the thing is not observed as existing, it is impossible to observe it as a source of existence and a qualifier for it, contrary to the receptacle of existence, for the mind must observe it devoid of existence, i.e., existence is not taken into account in it lest the occurrence of the obtained (hujul al-hasil) be necessitated, and also [devoid] of non-existence lest the conjunction of two opposites be necessitated. Thus, it is the essence (mahiyya) as it is in itself. As for the essentials (dhatiyyat) relative to the essence, and the essence relative to its accessories (lawazim), their priority is not necessary except by mental existence, because their priority in terms of essentials and the attribution of accessories to it is only according to the mind. And if you grasp this, then the priority of the receptacle of existence is also likewise, as will come that it is only according to the mind, not like the body with whiteness.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage deals with the conceptual priority of existence over essence. The author explains that for something that is a source (mabda') or qualifier (muqayyad) of existence for another, its own existence must be mentally prior because otherwise we cannot conceive of it as a source. In contrast, for a receptacle (qabil) of existence, the mind considers it without assuming either existence or non-existence to avoid the problem of obtaining the already obtained (hujul al-hasil) or the contradiction of opposites. The author then extends this to the relationship between the essence (mahiyya) and its essentials (dhatiyyat) and accessories (lawazim): their priority is only mental, not real. The passage concludes by noting that the priority of the receptacle is also mental only, unlike the body with whiteness which has a real priority.

Key Terms
muqayyad
qabil
mahiyya
hujul al-hasil

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that both muqayyad and qabil require existence priority in the same way.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only muqayyad requires existence priority; qabil is considered without existence or non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why must the muqayyad be considered existing before it can be a qualifier?
A) Because otherwise it would be a receptacle.
B) Because the mind cannot conceive of it as a source of existence without first conceiving it as existing.
C) Because it is an essence.
D) Because of the problem of hujul al-hasil.

Key Takeaway
A qualifier (muqayyad) must be mentally prior in existence, while a receptacle (qabil) is considered free from both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah as the Creator, do you mentally assume His existence is a pre-condition for His act of creation? How does this passage reinforce that Allah's existence is necessary and prior? What changes in your understanding of your own dependence on Him?

How does the qabil's neutrality inform your understanding of your own potential to receive truth? Are you ready to see yourself as pure essence before Allah, stripped of both existence and non-existence?

Mental Priority of Essentials and Accessories
The author explains that priority of essentials and accessories over the essence is mental, and that the priority of the receptacle is likewise mental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the priority of essentials (dhatiyyat) over the essence and of the essence over its accessories (lawazim) is only mental, not real, and that the same applies to the priority of the receptacle (qabil).

Core Idea
Essentials and accessories are mentally prior to the essence, not externally.

Having distinguished muqayyad from qabil, the author now extends the analysis to the relationship between the essence and its internal components (dhatiyyat) and external attributes (lawazim). He emphasizes that all such priority is only in the mind, not in external reality. This principle is then applied to the receptacle (qabil), confirming that its priority is also mental only, unlike the real priority of body over whiteness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dhatiyyat and lawazim
Read the second part of the Arabic passage. Note the claim: 'their priority is not necessary except by mental existence.'
This is a key principle: priority relations among universals are mental.
2
Understand the reason
The author explains that the priority of essentials and the attribution of accessories are only according to the mind. That is, when we think of the essence, we mentally separate its essential parts and its necessary attributes, but in reality they are not separate.
3
Apply to the qabil
The author then says that the priority of the receptacle (qabil) is also mental only, unlike the body with whiteness which has a real priority (body is really prior to whiteness).
This reinforces that the qabil's neutrality is a mental consideration.
4
Contrast with a real example
Compare the mental priority of qabil with the real priority of body over whiteness. In reality, whiteness cannot exist without a body, but the body can exist without whiteness. That is real priority. For qabil, the priority is only mental because the qabil is not a distinct existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

By the necessity of its priority over it in existence, because as long as the thing is not observed as existing, it is impossible to observe it as a source of existence and a qualifier for it, contrary to the receptacle of existence, for the mind must observe it devoid of existence, i.e., existence is not taken into account in it lest the occurrence of the obtained (hujul al-hasil) be necessitated, and also [devoid] of non-existence lest the conjunction of two opposites be necessitated. Correction: The passage explicitly states that this priority is only mental, not real.

Clarifying 'Necessary Existence'
Clarifies the intended reference of the terms 'necessary existence'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the term 'necessary existence' refers to in kalam discourse and why the distinction between the essence of the existent and the essence of existence matters.

Core Idea
'Necessary existence' refers to the being, not the abstract concept of existence.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a subtle but crucial terminological point. Before delving into proofs for God's existence, we must be clear about what we mean by 'necessary existence.' Sanusi clarifies that the phrase refers to the essence of the existent (God Himself), not to the abstract idea of existence. This distinction prevents confusion in later arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two phrases Sanusi presents as equivalent.
The Arabic is compact; take note of the contrast between 'ذات الموجود' and 'ذات الوجود'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Focus on the final part: 'المراد ذات الموجود لا ذات الوجود.' Understand that 'ذات الموجود' is the essence of the existent being, while 'ذات الوجود' is the essence of existence as a concept.
This distinction is foundational for all subsequent discussions on God's attributes.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi bother to clarify this? What confusion would arise if 'necessary existence' were taken to refer to the abstract concept?
If it referred to existence itself, then anything that exists would share in necessity, which is not intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فسواء قلنا واجب الوجود لذاته أو الوجود واجب لذاته فالمراد ذات الموجود لا ذات الوجود.
So whether we say 'the existence is necessary by itself' or 'existence is necessary by itself,' the intended meaning is the essence of the existent, not the essence of existence.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a terminological clarification. The author addresses a potential confusion: when one says 'the Necessary Existence (wajib al-wujud) by itself' or 'existence (al-wujud) is necessary by itself,' the reference is not to the abstract concept of existence (al-wujud) but to the essence (dhat) of the existent (al-mawjud). That is, the subject of necessity is the being that exists, not existence as a universal. This distinction prevents misunderstanding in the subsequent discussion about whether existence is additional to essence.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ذات الموجود
ذات الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'necessary existence' refers to the abstract concept of existence rather than the existing being itself.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the intended meaning is the essence of the existent, not the essence of existence. The term refers to the being that necessarily exists, not existence as a universal.

Checkpoint
According to the clarification, what does 'واجب الوجود' refer to?
A) The abstract concept of existence
B) The essence of the existent
C) The essence of existence
D) Both existence and existent

Key Takeaway
The term 'necessary existence' in kalam refers to the essence of the existent being, not the abstract concept of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is the Necessary Existence,' do you truly sense that it is His very being—not a label—that is absolute and inescapable? How would your reliance on Him change if you lived with this reality every moment?
?
If you previously treated 'existence' as a mere concept, what practical steps will you take today to remind yourself that Allah's existence is identical to His essence, and thus His attributes are not separate?
?
This clarification prevents a subtle error in understanding Tawheed. Will you examine your own thoughts to see if you have ever unconsciously reduced Allah's existence to an abstract idea rather than the most concrete reality? What will you do to correct that?

The Resolution of the Existence-Essence Dispute
The author presents and answers an objection about the nature of the dispute over existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute between theologians and philosophers over existence and essence is resolved by recognizing that both sides agree on mental distinction, and the real disagreement is about extra-mental reality.

Core Idea
Existence is mentally distinct from essence, but the real dispute is about extra-mental reality.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents an objection that the debate over existence and essence might be merely verbal. The author responds by carefully analyzing the arguments of the two parties, showing that both accept mental addition. He then clarifies the practical consequence of this agreement, leaving only a subtle disagreement about extra-mental reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objector points out that the term 'existence' is used in multiple senses, potentially making the dispute seem verbal. This sets up the author's response.
The objector suggests that both sides might be talking past each other due to ambiguous terminology.
2
Identify the author's response
The author summarizes the positions: the majority (mutakallimun) argue that the concept of 'existent' is not the same as the concept of essence qualified by it; Ibn Sina argues that essence and existence do not form two distinct identities like body and whiteness.
These two positions set the stage for finding common ground.
3
Find the point of agreement
The author explicitly states: 'فلا خلاف في أن الوجود زائد ذهنا' — there is no disagreement that existence is mentally additional. This means both sides accept mental distinction.
This is the key insight that resolves the apparent conflict.
4
Understand the remaining nuance
The author notes that the dispute is not about mental addition but about whether existence is also extra-mentally distinct. The proponent says 'additional in the mind', the opponent says 'cognitively'.
The practical difference is merely terminological once the mental addition is agreed upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإن قلت لاخفاء مثلاً أن ليس مفهوم الوجود مفهوم الإنشاء، وليس لفظ الوجود والعدم [؟] فيرادفهما من جميع اللغات موضوعاً بالاشتراك لمعان لا تكاد تتناهى واحتجاج الفريقين يشهد بأن النزاع في الوجود بمعنى الكون، ولعلنا نائين [؟] عن أن الوجود كما يطلق على الكون يطلق على الذات على أن مفهوم الذات أيضا مشترك فما وجه هذا الاختلاف ان عمال الشام [؟] قلت : مضمون أدلة الجمهور أن ليس مفهوم الموجود مفهوم الماهية المتصفة به، وأدلة الشيخ أن ليس لهما هويتان متمایزتان تقوم إحداهما بالأخرى كالجسم مع البياض، فلا خلاف في أن الوجود زائد ذهنا بمعنى أن للعقل أن يلاحظ الماهية دون الوجود وبالعكس ولا عيناً بأن يكون الماهية تحقق، ولعارضها المسمى بالوجود تحقق آخر، حتى يجتمعا اجتماع القابل والمقبول، كالجسم والبياض، فعند التحرير لا يبقى نزاع، ويظهر أن حصل الاشتراك لفظي [؟] مكابرة، ولا يتفرع على الوجود الذهني سوى أن للمثبت أن يقول زائد في العقل، وعلى النافي أن يقول عقلاً أو في التعقل وليس المسألة نفي التغاير العقلي والاشتراك المعنوي كما في بدائع المفهومات الكلية سيما المنفية، فإن التعقل عندهم لا يقتضي الثبوت، ولهذا جرت كلمة الجمهور بينهم على أنه مشترك معنى زائد ذهناً) (۱) .
He said: For if you say, for example, that the concept of existence is not the concept of [something else], and that the word 'existence' and 'nonexistence' [?] are synonymous in all languages, set by convention for meanings almost endless, and the arguments of the two parties testify that the dispute is about existence in the sense of 'being', and perhaps we are far from [understanding] that existence, just as it is applied to 'being', is also applied to essence, while the concept of essence is also common; so what is the face of this disagreement? ... I say: The substance of the arguments of the majority is that the concept of 'existent' is not the concept of the essence qualified by it, and the arguments of the Shaykh [i.e., Ibn Sina] are that they do not have two distinct identities, one subsisting by the other, like the body with whiteness. So there is no disagreement that existence is mentally additional, meaning that the mind can observe the essence without existence and vice versa, but not externally such that the essence has a realization and its accident called existence has another realization, so that they come together like the receptacle and the received, like body and whiteness. Thus, upon analysis, no disagreement remains, and it appears that the claim of verbal commonality is a stubbornness. No practical consequence follows from mental existence except that the affirmant can say it is additional in the mind, and the negator can say 'cognitively' or 'in intellection', and the issue is not the denial of mental distinction and conceptual commonality, as in the innovative universal concepts, especially negative ones, for intellection according to them does not require extra-mental reality. Hence, the common phrase among the majority is that it is a common term meaning mentally additional.) (1)
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair that clarifies the real point of disagreement between theologians and philosophers over whether existence is superadded to essence. The objector argues that the dispute seems merely verbal because both sides use the word 'existence' in multiple senses. The author responds by analyzing the arguments: the majority (mutakallimun) argue that the concept of 'existent' is not identical to the concept of the essence; the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) argues that essence and existence are not two separate entities like a body and its whiteness. So both agree that existence is mentally distinct (zā'id dhihnan) – i.e., the mind can separate them – but they disagree on whether there is a real, extra-mental distinction. The author concludes that no real dispute remains after this analysis; the claim that it is mere verbal commonality is obstinate. The only consequence for mental existence is that the proponent can say 'additional in the mind' while the opponent can say 'cognitively', but neither denies conceptual distinction. The footnote (1) likely contains additional clarification.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
ذهن (dhihn)
زائد ذهنا (za'id dhihnan)
تعقل (ta'aqqul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes the dispute is purely verbal with no real substance.
Correction: The author shows that both sides agree on mental addition; the real disagreement is about extra-mental reality, not about mental distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the point of agreement between the majority (mutakallimun) and the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) regarding existence and essence?
A) Existence is externally distinct from essence.
B) Existence is identical to essence in every respect.
C) Existence is mentally distinct from essence.
D) The concept of existence is the same as the concept of essence.

Key Takeaway
Existence is mentally distinct from essence by agreement; the remaining dispute is about extra-mental reality, not about mental addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you ever mentally separate between your essence (who you are as a person) and the fact that you exist? How does this distinction remind you that your existence is not self-derived but a gift from the One whose existence is necessary?
?
The passage shows both sides agree that existence is mentally distinct from essence. If your essence does not guarantee your existence, then every moment you exist is by the will of Another. As you go about your day, will you consciously live with the awareness that your very being is sustained moment by moment by the Creator?
?
Ibn Sina and the mutakallimun disagreed over whether existence is extra-mentally added to essence, but both affirmed the mental distinction. Does your certainty in Allah's necessary existence rest on this philosophical grounding, or on something more immediate? What would it mean for your faith if you could not rationally affirm this distinction?

Three Doctrines on Existence and Essence
The author enumerates three positions on existence-essence relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three classical positions on the relationship between existence and essence according to the Shaykh, the theologians, and the philosophers.

Core Idea
Existence is either identical to essence, an accident, or an ineffable attribute — the dispute is substantive.

Before evaluating any proposed solution to the existence-essence dispute, we must first grasp the views of the major schools. The author concisely presents the doctrines of the Shaykh, the theologians, and the philosophers, setting the stage for the refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three doctrines
Read the Arabic passage describing the views of the Shaykh, the theologians, and the philosophers. Note how each defines 'existence' relative to essence.
Notice the author immediately declares the philosophers' view 'obviously false'.
2
Identify each school's position
Summarize each view in your own words: (1) The Shaykh: existence is the concept of the thing itself. (2) Theologians: existence is an accident inhering in essence. (3) Philosophers: existence is an accident in contingents, but something else in God.
The philosophers' view on the Necessary Being is considered false by the author.
3
Reflect on the author's aim
The author says he wants to 'verify' these doctrines in a way that does not contradict reason. Ask yourself why he begins with a neutral summary before critiquing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد تحقيق مذاهب الشيخ وسائر المتكلمين والحكماء على وجه لا يخالف بديهة العقل فإن الظاهر من مذهب الشيخ أن مفهوم وجود الإنسان هو الخيوان الناطق مثلاً ولفظ الوجود في العربية ولفظ (هستي) في الفارسية إلى غير ذلك معان لا تكاد تتناهى من الموجودات ومن مذهب المتكلمين أن الوجود عرض قائم بالماهية قيام سائر الأعراض بمحالها. ومن مذهب الحكماء أنه كذلك في الممكنات وفي الواجب معنى آخر غير مدرك للعقول، ذلك ظاهر البطلان، وذهب صاحب الصحائف (۲) إلى أن منشأ الاختلاف جميع هو إطلاق لفظ الوجود على مفهوم الكون، ومفهوم الذات، فمن ذهب إلى أنه زائد على الماهية أراد به الكون ومن ذهب إلى أنه نفس الماهية أراد به الذات فعند تحرير المبحث يرتفع الاختلاف وهذا فاسد .
أما أولاً : فلأن احتجاج الفريقين صريح في أن النزاع في الوجود المقابل للعدم وهو بمعنى الكون.
وأما ثانياً : فلأن مفهوم الذات أيضاً معنى واحد مشترك بين الذوات، واشتراك الوجود بين الوجودات من غير اشتراك لفظ، وتعدد وضع .
وأما ثالثاً : فلأن القول بأن ذات الإنسان نفس ذاته وماهيته مما لا يتصور فيه فائدة فضلاً عن أن يحتاج إلى الاحتجاج عليه فنقول. أدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء زائد عليه لا يفيد سوى أن ليس المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من ذلك الشيء من غير دلالة على أنه عرض قائم به قيام العرض بالمحل .
He intends to verify the doctrines of the Shaykh, the rest of the theologians, and the philosophers in a way that does not contradict the intuition of reason. For it is apparent from the doctrine of the Shaykh that the concept of the existence of man is, for example, 'rational animal', and the word 'existence' in Arabic and the word 'hasti' in Persian and so on are meanings that are almost endless among existents. From the doctrine of the theologians, existence is an accident subsisting in the essence like the subsistence of other accidents in their substrates. From the doctrine of the philosophers, it is the same in contingents, but in the Necessary it is another meaning not perceived by intellects—that is obviously false. The author of al-Saha'if (2) held that the source of the entire disagreement is the application of the word 'existence' to the concept of 'being' and the concept of 'essence'. So whoever held that it is additional to essence intended by it 'being', and whoever held that it is identical to essence intended by it 'essence'. Thus, when the topic is analyzed, the disagreement disappears. But this is unsound.
First: because the arguments of both parties are explicit that the dispute is about existence opposite to non-existence, which is 'being'.
Second: because the concept of essence is also a single meaning common among essences, and existence is common among existents without verbal equivocation and multiple coinage.
Third: because the claim that the essence of man is identical to his own essence and quiddity is something from which no benefit can be conceived, let alone requiring proof. So we say: The proofs of those who say that the existence of a thing is additional to it only establish that the concept of the existence of the thing is not the concept of that thing, without indicating that it is an accident subsisting in it like the subsistence of an accident in its substrate.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage classifies the positions of three groups—the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina or a specific earlier figure), the theologians (mutakallimun), and the philosophers (hukama')—on the nature of existence. The Shaykh holds that the concept of existence is the concept of the thing itself (e.g., the existence of man is just 'rational animal'), leading to a multiplicity of meanings for existence across different existents. The theologians hold that existence is an accident that inheres in the essence. The philosophers hold that this accident view applies to contingent beings, but for the Necessary Being, existence is something else beyond reason—this the author rejects as obviously false. Then the author refutes the solution proposed by the author of al-Saha'if (Shams al-Din al-Samarqandi). Samarqandi claimed the entire dispute stems from using 'existence' to mean either 'being' (al-kawn) or 'essence' (al-dhat). The author gives three refutations: (1) Both parties' arguments clearly focus on existence as opposed to non-existence (i.e., 'being'); (2) The concept of essence is itself a univocal concept, and existence is univocal across existents without verbal equivocation; (3) The claim that a thing's essence is identical to its own essence is tautological and useless. The author then clarifies that the proofs of those who affirm addition (ziyada) only show conceptual non-identity, not an accident-substrate relation. Thus, Samarqandi's solution fails.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
عرض (arad)
ذات (dhat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the dispute is merely about words or that the philosophers' view is the same as the theologians'.
Correction: The author distinguishes clearly: the philosophers hold a unique position on the Necessary Being, which he dismisses as false.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what do the theologians (mutakallimun) hold about existence?
A) Existence is identical to the essence.
B) Existence is an accident subsisting in the essence.
C) Existence is a mental construct only.
D) Existence is the same for all things including God.

Key Takeaway
The dispute about existence is not merely verbal; it reflects deep metaphysical commitments about whether existence is a real attribute added to essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate your own existence, do you experience it as something added to your essence or as identical to it? How does this distinction affect your sense of dependency on the Necessary Being?
?
The author dismisses the philosophers' view as obviously false. Are there aspects of your own belief that you hold without rational verification? This lesson calls you to examine the metaphysical foundations of your faith. What is the most deeply held assumption about your existence that you have never questioned?
?
If existence is an accident, then your very being is a gift from the Necessary Being. Does this make you feel fragile or comforted? What does it demand from you today?

Refutation of al-Samarqandi's Resolution
The author refutes al-Samarqandi's verbal resolution with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Samarqandi's proposed resolution to the existence-essence dispute and the three reasons why the author rejects it.

Core Idea
Al-Samarqandi's solution reduces a real dispute to a verbal equivocation — but the author shows it fails on multiple grounds.

After presenting the three schools' views, the author examines a specific attempted reconciliation by the author of al-Saha'if (al-Samarqandi). He introduces Samarqandi's claim that the disagreement stems from using 'existence' to mean either 'being' or 'essence', then delivers three devastating refutations that clarify the true nature of the disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Samarqandi's proposal
Read the passage describing his proposed solution: the dispute is merely verbal — 'existence' can refer to 'being' or 'essence'. Understand the argument.
2
Analyze the first refutation
The author says both parties' arguments explicitly concern existence opposite to non-existence, i.e., 'being'. So Samarqandi misinterprets the dispute.
3
Analyze the second refutation
The concept of 'essence' (dhat) is itself univocal; existence is univocal without equivocation. Thus, Samarqandi's claim of double meaning fails.
4
Analyze the third refutation
The idea that a thing's essence is identical to itself is tautological and useless. The proofs of the additionists only show conceptual non-identity, not an accident-substrate relation.

?

?

?

Refutation of al-Samarqandi's Verbal Solution
Refutation of the claim that the dispute is merely verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dispute about the nature of existence ('wujud') cannot be resolved as merely verbal.

Core Idea
The dispute about existence is real, not verbal — both sides argue over existence as opposite to non-existence.

Al-Samarqandi, a prominent theologian, proposed that the entire dispute over whether existence is additional to essence or identical with it is merely a verbal confusion. He claimed that by clarifying the term 'existence', the disagreement dissolves. In this passage, the author rejects this proposal as false and gives three compelling reasons. As you read, notice how each objection targets a different aspect of al-Samarqandi's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully to get a sense of the overall argument without diving into analysis.
Notice the clear structure signaled by 'Amma awwalan...', 'Amma thaniyan...', 'Amma thalithan...'.
2
Understand al-Samarqandi's position
Identify what al-Samarqandi claimed: the dispute is due to using 'existence' for two different concepts (kawn and dhat). Clarifying this resolves it.
The author says this solution is 'fasid' (false) before listing objections.
3
Analyze the first objection
The first objection: The arguments of both groups explicitly concern existence opposite to non-existence, which is the meaning of 'kawn'. So the dispute is real.
This shows the dispute is not merely verbal because both sides are debating the same concept.
4
Analyze the second and third objections
The second objection: The concept of dhat is also a common meaning, so verbal commonality doesn't help. The third: The claim that 'human's essence is its self' is useless and doesn't need proof.
Each objection undermines a different premise of al-Samarqandi's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب صاحب الصحائف إلى أن منشأ الاختلاف هو إطلاق لفظ الوجود على مفهوم الكون، ومفهوم الذات، فمن ذهب إلى أنه زائد على الماهية أراد به الكون ومن ذهب إلى أنه نفس الماهية أراد به الذات فعند تحرير المبحث يرتفع الاختلاف وهذا فاسد .
أما أولاً : فلأن احتجاج الفريقين صريح في أن النزاع في الوجود المقابل للعدم وهو بمعنى الكون.
وأما ثانياً : فلأن مفهوم الذات أيضاً معنى واحد مشترك بين الذوات، واشتراك الوجود بين الوجودات من غير اشتراك لفظ، وتعدد وضع .
وأما ثالثاً : فلأن القول بأن ذات الإنسان نفس ذاته وماهيته مما لا يتصور فيه فائدة فضلاً عن أن يحتاج إلى الاحتجاج عليه
And the author of al-Saha'if (al-Samarqandi) held that the source of the dispute is using the word 'existence' for the concept of 'being in the world' (kawn) and the concept of 'essence' (dhat). So whoever held that it is additional to the essence intended by it 'being in the world', and whoever held that it is the essence itself intended by it 'essence'. Thus, when the inquiry is refined, the dispute is resolved. But this is false.
As for first: because the arguments of the two groups are explicit that the dispute is about existence opposite to non-existence, which is the meaning of 'being in the world'.
As for second: because the concept of essence is also a single meaning common among essences, and the commonality of existence among existents is without verbal commonality or multiple usages.
As for third: because the statement that the essence of a human is its own self and quiddity is inconceivable to have a benefit, let alone needing argument for it.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage refutes the view of al-Samarqandi, the author of al-Saha'if. Al-Samarqandi claimed that the entire dispute is verbal: 'existence' is used sometimes for the concept of 'being in the world' (kawn) and sometimes for the concept of 'essence' (dhat). Those who say it is additional mean 'being in the world', those who say it is identical mean 'essence'. So with proper analysis, the dispute disappears. The author rejects this as false with three points. First, the arguments of both sides clearly concern existence as opposed to non-existence, which is exactly the meaning of 'being in the world'—so the dispute is real, not verbal. Second, the concept of essence (dhat) is itself a single meaning common to all essences; claiming that existence is common among existents without verbal ambiguity does not help. Third, saying 'the nature of a human is its own self and quiddity' is a useless statement; it does not need argument, so al-Samarqandi's explanation adds nothing.

Key Terms
صاحب الصحائف
الكون
الذات
النزاع اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute about whether existence is additional to essence is merely verbal and can be resolved by redefining terms.
Correction: The author shows that the dispute is substantive because both sides argue about existence opposite to non-existence, and the proposed verbal distinction does not address the actual arguments or add any benefit.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the first objection show al-Samarqandi's view is false?
A) Because the two groups do not argue about existence
B) Because the arguments of both groups are about existence opposite to non-existence
C) Because existence has many meanings
D) Because the concept of essence is not clear

Key Takeaway
The dispute about existence is substantive, not verbal; al-Samarqandi's verbal solution fails to account for the actual arguments of both sides.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a deep theological disagreement as 'just semantics' to avoid the effort of thinking through the arguments? What current issue in your faith are you most tempted to treat as merely verbal, and what is at stake if it is actually real?
?
The author says that claiming 'essence is itself' is a useless tautology. Are there truths you claim to believe that have become empty words you repeat without letting them transform how you live? Name one such truth and a concrete change you will make to take it seriously.

Al-Iji's View on the Dispute and Its Critique
Al-Iji reduces the dispute to mental existence, then the author objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji reduces the dispute over existence and essence to the question of mental existence, and why the author finds this reduction questionable.

Core Idea
The dispute about existence boils down to mental existence—but not everyone agrees.

Al-Iji, the author of al-Mawaqif, proposes a clever reduction: the entire debate about whether existence is additional to essence is really about whether there is mental existence. This passage presents his view and then immediately raises a critical objection. As we read, notice how the objection targets the foundation of al-Iji's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
2
Identify al-Iji's claim
How does al-Iji tie the dispute to mental existence? What are the two positions he describes?
3
Analyze the objection
What is the author's critique? Why does it undermine al-Iji's reduction?
The objection points out that even deniers of mental existence accept conceptual distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب صاحب المواقف إلى أن النزاع راجع إلى النزاع في الوجود الذهني فمن أثبته قال بالزيادة عقلاً . بمعنى أن في العقل أمراً هو الوجود وآخر هو الماهية .
ومن نفاه أطلق القول بأنه نفس الماهية ، لأنه لا تغاير ولا تمايز في الخارج، وليس وراء الخارج أمر يتحقق فيه أحدهما بدون الآخر، فيتحقق التمايز وفيه نظر. لأنه لا نزاع للقائلين بنفي الوجود الذهني في تعقل الكليات والاعتبارات ، والمعدومات والممتنعات، ومغايرة بعضها للبعض بحسب المفهوم وإنما نزاعهم
And the author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) held that the dispute reduces to the dispute about mental existence. So whoever affirms it says that [existence] is additional mentally, meaning that in the mind there is something that is existence and another that is essence. And whoever denies it asserts that it is the essence itself, because there is no distinction or differentiation in the external, and there is nothing beyond the external in which one of them is realized without the other, so differentiation is realized. But there is consideration [objection] to it: because those who deny mental existence do not dispute the conceptualization of universals and considerations, and non-existents and impossibilities, and the differentiation of some from others according to concept. Their dispute is only about...
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents the view of al-Iji, author of al-Mawaqif, and begins a critique. Al-Iji argues that the entire dispute about existence being additional to essence boils down to the dispute about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). Those who affirm mental existence say existence is mentally additional: in the mind, existence and essence are two distinct things. Those who deny mental existence say existence is the essence itself, because in external reality there is no distinction; there is no realm other than the external where one can be found without the other. Therefore, no real differentiation. The author then raises an objection: even those who deny mental existence do not dispute that we conceive universals, considerations, non-existents, and impossibilities, and that these differ in concept. Their disagreement is only about... (the passage cuts off). The critique seems to be that the denial of mental existence does not eliminate conceptual distinctions, so al-Iji's reduction is questionable.

Correction: This passage shows the author immediately critiques it, pointing out that even those who deny mental existence still accept conceptual distinctions.

The Dispute over Existence and Essence: A Conceptual Clarification
Sanusi clarifies that the dispute over existence and essence is merely conceptual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the dispute over whether existence is additional to essence is resolved by distinguishing conceptual addition from real ontological composition.

Core Idea
Existence and essence: different in mind, not in reality.

This passage from a classical kalam text cuts through a major debate. Both sides agree that existence is conceptually distinct from essence, but some mistakenly thought the dispute was about real separation. The author clarifies that once we distinguish mental addition from external addition, the controversy disappears.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the key terms: existence (wujud), essence (mahiyya), mentally (dhihnan), externally ('aynan).
The passage uses a dense, argumentative style typical of kalam.
2
Identify the two senses of 'addition'
The passage distinguishes between addition mentally (conceptual distinction) and addition externally (real separation). Notice how the author argues that all parties agree on the former but not the latter.
3
Understand why the dispute is merely verbal
Once we specify that 'addition' means conceptual distinction, the disagreement dissolves. The author also rejects the extreme view that existence is purely equivocal (every instance has a different meaning).
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن هذا مما لا يقبله العقل وإن وقع في كلام الإمام وغيره، وأدلة القائلين بأن وجود الشيء نفس ذاته لا يفيد سوى أن ليس للشيء هوية، ولعارضه المسمى بالوجود هوية أخرى قائمة بالأولى بحيث يجتمعان اجتماع البياض ذلك والجسم من غير دلالة على أن المفهوم من وجود الشيء هو المفهوم من الشيء. فإن هذا بديهي البطلان فإذا لا يظهر من كلام الفريقين ولا يتصور من المنصف، خلاف في أن الوجود زائد على الماهية ذهناً، أي عند العقل وبحسب المفهوم والتصور بمعنى أن للعقل أن يلاحظ الوجود دون الماهية ، والماهية دون الوجود لا عيناً، أي بحسب الذات والهوية بأن يكون لكل منهما هوية متميزة يقوم أحدهما بالأخرى كبياض الجسم، فعند تحرير المبحث وبيان أن المراد الزيادة في التصور أو في الهوية يرتفع النزاع بين الفريقين، ويظهر أن القول يكون اشتراك الوجود لفظياً، بمعنى أن المفهوم من الوجود المضاف إلى الإنسان غير المفهوم من المضاف إلى الفرس، ولا اشتراك بينهما في مفهوم الكون مكابرة ومخالفة لبديهة العقل.
For this is not accepted by the intellect even if it occurs in the words of the Imam and others. The arguments of those who say that the existence of a thing is its very essence only amount to the thing having no identity, and that its accident called existence is another identity subsisting in the first, such that they combine like whiteness and body, without indicating that the concept of existence of a thing is the same as the concept of the thing. For this is obviously false. Thus there appears from the statements of the two groups—and it cannot be imagined from a fair-minded person—no disagreement that existence is additional to essence mentally (dhihnan), i.e., in the intellect and according to concept and representation, meaning that the intellect can observe existence without essence, and essence without existence—but not externally ('aynan), i.e., according to essence and identity, such that each has a distinct identity subsisting in the other like the whiteness of a body. So when the investigation is refined and it is clarified that what is meant is addition in representation or in identity, the dispute between the two groups is resolved, and it becomes apparent that the statement that existence is equivocal (ishtirak lafzi), meaning that the concept of existence attributed to man is not the same as that attributed to horse, and that there is no commonality between them in the concept of being, is stubbornness and contrary to the intuition of the intellect.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the dispute over whether existence (wujud) is additional to essence (mahiyya). The author argues that the claim that existence is identical to essence is absurd, because it would imply that a thing has no identity and that existence is a separate accident. The author then states that a fair-minded person sees no real disagreement: all agree that existence is additional to essence mentally (dhihnan), meaning conceptually, but not externally ('aynan). The only confusion arises from failure to specify whether 'addition' means conceptual distinction or real ontological separation. Once that is clarified, the dispute disappears. The author also rejects the view that existence is purely equivocal (every instance has a different concept), calling it contrary to reason. The passage thus establishes that existence is univocal in concept and merely added to essence in the mind.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
ذهناً (dhihnan)
عيناً ('aynan)
اشتراك لفظي (ishtirak lafzi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disagreement between philosophers and theologians is about whether existence is a real separate entity from essence in external reality.
Correction: The passage explains that all parties agree on conceptual addition (mental distinction), and the dispute is only about whether the addition is also external. Once clarified as conceptual, the dispute resolves.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'existence is additional to essence mentally (dhihnan)'?
A) Existence is a separate entity in the mind like whiteness in body.
B) The intellect can conceive existence without essence and essence without existence.
C) Existence and essence are identical in the mind.
D) Existence is the same as essence in external reality.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over existence and essence is resolved by recognizing that existence is conceptually distinct from essence but not a separate entity in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that existence is mentally distinct from essence but not a separate reality. How does that affect your understanding of your own temporary existence? Are you treating your existence as something you possess rather than a gift continuously given?
?
The author calls the view that existence is purely equivocal 'stubbornness contrary to intuition'. Is there any area of your faith where you stubbornly cling to a conceptual confusion rather than accept the rational clarification? What would it take for you to let that go?
?
If the dispute over existence and essence is merely conceptual, does that mean theological arguments are just word games? Or does it show the importance of precise language in protecting tawhid? Examine your own attitude toward theological precision: do you dismiss it as unnecessary scholasticism, or do you see it as a safeguard for your heart?

Al-Iji's View on Mental Existence and Its Critique
Presents and critiques al-Iji's linking of the dispute to mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Iji's claim that the dispute over existence-essence reduces to mental existence, the critique of that claim, and how the majority distinguishes between contingent and necessary being.

Core Idea
The dispute is not merely about mental existence but about conceptual distinctions.

Al-Iji, author of al-Mawaqif, proposed a controversial reduction: the entire debate about whether existence is additional to essence hinges on the reality of mental existence. But is that correct? This passage presents his view, its critique, and the nuanced position adopted by the majority of theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the flow: al-Iji's claim, critique, majority view, and the special case of the Necessary Being.
The Arabic has some uncertain words marked by [?]; focus on the overall argument.
2
Identify al-Iji's position
Al-Iji says the dispute reduces to the issue of mental existence. Find how he links the two positions to affirmation or denial of mental existence.
3
Understand the critique
The author argues that denying mental existence does not imply denying conceptual distinctions. Those who deny mental existence still affirm universals and the univocity of existence as a concept.
This critique shows the weakness of al-Iji's reduction.
4
Learn the majority view
The majority of deniers of mental existence actually affirm that existence is univocal and additional in the mind (conceptually). Then note the distinction between contingent and Necessary Being.
For contingents, existence is additional; for the Necessary, His reality is His particular existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب صاحب المواقف إلى أن النزاع راجع إلى النزاع في الوجود الذهني فمن أثبته قال بالزيادة عقلاً . بمعنى أن في العقل أمراً هو الوجود وآخر هو الماهية . ومن نفاه أطلق القول بأنه نفس الماهية ، لأنه لا تغاير ولا تمايز في الخارج، وليس وراء الخارج أمر يتحقق فيه أحدهما بدون الآخر، فيتحقق التمايز وفيه نظر. لأنه لا نزاع للقائلين بنفي الوجود الذهني في تعقل الكليات والاعتبارات ، والمعدومات والممتنعات، ومغايرة بعضها للبعض بحسب المفهوم وإنما نزاعهم في [؟] التعقل [؟] في العقل وفي اقتضائه الثبوت في الجملة، نقلا [؟] بمجرد نفي الوجود الذهني، نفي التغاير بين الوجود والماهية في التصور، بل المفهوم من أحدهما غير المفهوم من الآخر، ولا نفي الاشتراك المعنوي، بمعنى أن العقل يفهم من الوجود معنى كلياً مشتركاً بين الوجودات، كما لا ينفون اختلاف مفهوم الفرس ومفهوم الإنسان. بل غاية الأمر أن لا يقولوا الوجود أمراً زائداً في العقل والمعنى الكلي المشترك ثابت فيه. بل غاية ما يقال: إنه مشترك عقلاً وفي التعقل، بمعنى أن العقل يفهم من أحدهما غير ما يفهم من الآخر، ويدرك منها معنى كلياً يصدق على الكل. ثم إن الجمهور من القائلين بنفي الوجود الذهني يثبتون أن الوجود مشترك معنوي وزائد على الماهية في ذهناً بالمعنى الذي ذكرناه. ثم [يقال]: إن الوجود زائد على الماهية في الممكن، وأما في الواجب فحقيقته وجوده الخاص القائم بذاته المخالف بالحقيقة لسائر الموجودات، المعبر عنه بالوجود الخاص. وعند الفلاسفة حقيقة الواجب وجوده الخاص المطلق الذي هو عين ذاته، لا كالوجود الخاص في الممكنات.
Al-Sahib al-Mawaqif held that the dispute reduces to the dispute about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). Whoever affirms it says that existence is additional in the intellect, meaning that in the intellect there is something that is existence and another that is essence. Whoever denies it says that it is identical to essence, because there is no distinction or differentiation in the external world, and there is nothing beyond the external in which one can be realized without the other, so that differentiation would be realized. But this is questionable. Because those who deny mental existence do not dispute the conception of universals and considerations, non-existents and impossibles, and the difference of some from others in concept. Their dispute is only about [؟] conception [؟] in the intellect and its requiring subsistence in general. [It is claimed that] merely denying mental existence does not negate the distinction between existence and essence in conception; rather, the concept of one is not the concept of the other, nor does it negate univocal sharing (al-ishtirak al-ma'nawi), meaning that the intellect understands from existence a universal meaning common among existents, just as they do not deny the difference between the concept of horse and the concept of man. Rather, the utmost they do is not say that existence is something additional in the intellect and that the universal common meaning is established therein. Rather, the utmost that is said is that it is shared intellectually and in conception, meaning that the intellect understands from one what it does not understand from the other, and perceives from them a universal meaning that applies to all. Then the majority of those who deny mental existence affirm that existence is univocally shared and additional to essence in the mind (dhihnan) in the sense mentioned. Then it is said: Existence is additional to essence in the contingent. As for the Necessary, His reality is His particular existence subsisting by Himself, different in reality from other existents, expressed as 'particular existence' (al-wujud al-khass). According to the philosophers, the reality of the Necessary is His particular absolute existence which is identical to His essence, not like the particular existence in contingents.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the view of al-Iji, the author of al-Mawaqif. Al-Iji claims that the dispute about whether existence is additional to essence reduces to the dispute about mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni): those who affirm mental existence say existence is additional in the intellect, while those who deny it say existence is identical to essence because there is no distinction in external reality. The author criticizes this, arguing that even those who deny mental existence still affirm conceptual distinctions and the univocity of existence (al-ishtirak al-ma'nawi). They simply do not posit existence as an additional mental entity. The author then notes that the majority of those who deny mental existence actually affirm that existence is univocal and additional to essence in the mind in the conceptual sense. The passage then distinguishes between contingent beings (al-mumkinat), where existence is additional to essence, and the Necessary Being (al-wajib), whose reality is His particular existence (al-wujud al-khass) identical to His essence, according to both the theologians and the philosophers, though with different formulations.

Key Terms
وجود ذهني (al-wujud al-dhihni)
اشتراك معنوي (al-ishtirak al-ma'nawi)
وجود خاص (al-wujud al-khass)
واجب (al-wajib)
ممكن (al-mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That denying mental existence entails denying all conceptual distinctions between existence and essence.
Correction: The passage shows that deniers of mental existence still affirm conceptual distinctions and the univocity of existence. They simply do not posit existence as an extra mental entity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the majority of those who deny mental existence affirm?
A) That existence is equivocal (ishtirak lafzi).
B) That existence is univocal and additional to essence in the mind.
C) That existence is identical to essence.
D) That the Necessary Being has existence additional to His essence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Iji's reduction fails because deniers of mental existence still affirm conceptual distinctions; the majority resolve the issue by distinguishing between mental conception and extra-mental reality, and they treat the Necessary Being's existence as identical to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that al-Iji's reduction of a deep metaphysical dispute was criticized for oversimplifying. Where in your own understanding of Islam have you settled for a simplistic 'this vs. that' when the reality is more nuanced? Are you willing to do the intellectual work to see the complexity, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
The passage distinguishes between mental concepts and extra-mental reality. How often do you confuse your mental image of God (which is limited and created) with God Himself? Does this confusion affect how you approach Him in worship? Commit to purifying your conception of tawheed by studying the attribute of existence as identical to His essence.
?
The Necessary Being's existence is identical to His essence, while yours is contingent and dependent. In what ways do you live as though your existence were self-sufficient, forgetting that at every moment you depend on God for your very essence? Today, take one concrete action to remind yourself of your contingency, such as reciting 'Ya Hayyu Ya Qayyum' with full presence of heart.

Distinguishing Two Types of Predication
Answers objection by distinguishing al-muwaṭa'a and al-ishtiqāq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between predication by agreement (al-muwaṭa'a) and predication by derivation (al-ishtiqāq), and why it resolves the objection that existence cannot be non-existent.

Core Idea
Predication 'by agreement' is impossible for opposites; predication 'by derivation' is not.

The objector argues that existence cannot be non-existent because a thing cannot be described by its opposite. The author responds by distinguishing two ways we can predicate something of a subject: by agreement (where the predicate means the same thing) and by derivation (where the predicate is derived from a root but different). This distinction is crucial for understanding logical and theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the flow of the objection and answer.
Notice the compact style of the argument.
2
Identify the two types of predication
In the answer, look for the terms 'al-muwaṭa'a' (by agreement) and 'al-ishtiqāq' (by derivation). Understand the examples given.
The example for muwaṭa'a is 'existence is non-existence'; for ishtiqāq, 'existence is non-existent'.
3
Understand why the distinction answers the objection
Ask yourself: Why does the objector's argument fail? Because it conflates two different kinds of predication.
Only predication by agreement makes a thing identical to its opposite, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: بل يمتنع لذاته لامتناع اتصاف الشيء بنقيضه . قلنا : الممتنع اتصاف الشيء بنقيضه بمعنى جمله عليه بالمواطأة مثل قولنا : الوجود عدم لا بالاشتقاق مثل قولنا : الوجود معدوم
If it is said: Rather, it is impossible for its own sake due to the impossibility of a thing being described by its opposite. We say: What is impossible is describing a thing by its opposite in the sense of predicating it by agreement (al-muwaṭa'a), like our saying: existence is non-existence; not by derivation (al-ishtiqāq), like our saying: existence is non-existent.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector claims that existence cannot be non-existent because that would be describing a thing by its opposite (naqid), which is impossible. The author responds by distinguishing two types of predication (ḥaml). The first is 'predication by agreement' (bi al-muwaṭa'a), where the predicate is identical in meaning to the subject, e.g., 'existence is non-existence' (al-wujud 'adam). This is indeed impossible because a thing cannot be its opposite. The second is 'predication by derivation' (bi al-ishtiqāq), where the predicate is derived from a root but not identical, e.g., 'existence is non-existent' (al-wujud ma'dum). This is not impossible in the same sense, as the predicate is a different concept. The author uses this distinction to show that the objector's argument conflates the two types.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That describing a thing by its opposite is always impossible without qualification.
Correction: It is impossible only when the predication is by agreement (muwaṭa'a), not when it is by derivation (ishtiqāq).

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the author makes to answer the objection?
A) Between necessary and contingent existence
B) Between predication by agreement and predication by derivation
C) Between external and mental existence
D) Between essence and attributes

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of describing a thing by its opposite only applies when the predicate is identical in meaning (muwaṭa'a), not when it is derived (ishtiqāq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider Allah's ability to create from nothing, do you ever unconsciously assume that 'non-existence' as a description of a thing is impossible because you confuse it with the identity 'existence is non-existence'? How would this lesson correct that assumption in your heart?
?
Think of a personal characteristic you struggle with—like weakness or ignorance. Do you treat it as if it defines your very nature (muwaṭa'a), or do you see it as a temporary derived description (ishtiqāq) that Allah can change? Which view strengthens your reliance on Him?
?
This lesson teaches that not all opposite descriptions are impossible. Are there any 'impossibilities' you have accepted in your spiritual life—like thinking you can never overcome a sin or attain a virtue—that might actually be possible if you distinguish the type of predication? Will you repent from limiting Allah's power with your assumptions?
